Fourth Argument: Diversity of the World Cannot Arise from Two Simples
Argument from the diversity of the world against dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the world's vast diversity cannot be explained by the mixing of only two simple substances.

Core Idea
Two simples can produce only one nature, not the diverse world we see.

The final rational argument draws on a basic principle: the mixing of two simple substances yields only one nature, not many. Yet the world contains countless differences in kind, individual, essence, form, natures, and opposite accidents. Therefore, the dualist theory that the world arises from the mixing of light and darkness (two simples) is impossible. Simple things cannot produce effects contrary to their own essences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the principle and the observation.
The phrase 'العقل بالضرورة يقضي' indicates a rational necessity.
2
Understand the principle
The mixing of two simple substances yields only one nature, not multiple.
A simple substance has a single essence; mixture can only produce one resultant nature.
3
Contrast with observed reality
We see numerous differences in the world—kinds, individuals, essences, forms, natures, opposite accidents.
These cannot arise from only two simple things.
4
Draw the conclusion
Hence, the dualist explanation is insufficient; a more complex cause is needed.
Implicitly, this points to a creator with wisdom and power.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If darkness is eternal, the world arises from mixing, and mixing itself is either good or evil; either way, good from evil or evil from good follows. Also, the final outcome (separation from mixture) would then be opposite to the mixture, perpetuating the absurdity. Fourth argument: The mixing of two simple substances yields only one nature, but the world contains many different kinds, individuals, essences, forms, natures, and opposite accidents. These cannot arise from just two simple things, because simple things do not produce effects contrary to their own essences. Thus, the dualist account of the world's diversity is impossible.

Key Terms
جوهرين بسيطين
طبيعة واحدة
اختلافات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, what is the result of mixing two simple substances?
A) Multiple natures
B) One nature only
C) Opposite accidents
D) Infinite diversity

Key Takeaway
The world's diversity cannot arise from only two simple substances, refuting the dualist explanation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever attribute the diverse situations in your life to multiple independent causes, forgetting that all ultimately come from One Creator? How does this argument challenge your daily habit of dividing events into 'from this' and 'from that'? What would change if you consciously traced every difference back to Allah alone?
?
Consider the opposites in your own character or circumstances—joy and sorrow, strength and weakness. If they can't arise from mix of two simples, what does that tell you about the One who created them? How does that truth call you to rely on Him as the sole source of all that you experience?
?
The argument shows that simplicity cannot produce diversity. Your heart is constantly bombarded with conflicting desires and thoughts. Are you trying to manage them as if they come from multiple sources, or do you turn to the One who created them all? What step can you take today to unify your heart's focus under His oneness?

The Dilemma of Darkness's Existence
Poses a dilemma: if darkness exists, it equals light; if not, it cannot be eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a logical dilemma to refute the claim that darkness is an eternal principle.

Core Idea
Darkness cannot be eternal: existence itself is good, and non-existence cannot be eternal.

This lesson begins the refutation of the dualist claim that darkness is an eternal principle alongside light. The author presents a simple but powerful dilemma: either darkness truly exists or it does not. Each option leads to a contradiction with the dualist's own assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the dilemma presented.
2
Identify the two branches
The author posits two possibilities: darkness either exists in reality or does not. Note each horn of the dilemma.
3
Analyze the consequences
For each branch, understand the logical outcome that undermines dualism: if it exists, it equals light; if not, it cannot be eternal or produce mixture.
This connects to the core idea that existence itself is good.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول أيضاً : الظلام لا يخلو إما أن يكون موجودًا حقيقة أو لم يكن، فإن كان وجوده وجودًا حقيقيًّا فقد ساوى النور في الوجود وبطل الامتياز عنه من كل وجه، وكذلك إن ساواه في القدم والوحدة، ثم الوجود من حيث هو وجود خير لا محالة فلم يكن الظلام شراً، وإن لم يكن موجوداً حقيقة فما ليس بموجود حقيقة كيف يكون قديما ؟ وكيف ينافي ضده؟ وكيف يحصل منه امتزاج؟ وكيف يحصل من امتزاجه وجود العالم؟ ونقول أيضا : عندكم وجود خير من شر، ووجود شر من خير لا يتصور. ومن قال بحدوث الظلام فقد لزمه حدوث شر من خير، ثم حدوث العالم من الامتزاج، أوذلك حدوث خير من شر. ومن قال بقدمه فقد لزمه احصول العالم من الامتزاج، والامتزاج إن كان خيرًا فهو حصول خير من شر، وإن كان شراً فهو حصول شر من خير، ثم لو كان الامتزاج خيرًا أوجب أن يكون الخلاص شراً ؛ لأنه ضده، ولو كان شرا فالخلاص خير، وعلى أي وجه قدر فلا بد من حصول خير من شر، وحصول شر من خير. ونقول أيضا : العقل بالضرورة يقضي بأن امتزاج جوهرين بسيطين لا يقتضي إلا طبيعةً واحدة، ونحن نرى مختلفات في العالم اختلافا في النوع والشخص والحقيقة والصورة على طبائع مختلفة وأعراض متضادة، وهذه المختلفات تستحيل أن تحصل من امتزاج أمرين بسيطين فقط، فإنهما لا يوجبان الأمور على خلاف ذاتيهما، ولا يوجدان أكونا على ضد طبيعتيهما فقد بطل قولهم : إن الكون والكائنات حصلت من الامتزاج، بل لا بد من إسنادهما إلى فاطر حكيم قادر عليم.
And we also say: Darkness cannot except either it be existent in reality or not. If its existence is a real existence, then it has equaled light in existence and distinction from it is nullified in every respect. Likewise if it equaled it in eternity and oneness, then existence as such is necessarily good, so darkness would not be evil. And if it is not existent in reality, then that which is not existent in reality—how can it be eternal? And how can it oppose its opposite? And how can mixture come from it? And how can the existence of the world come from its mixture? And we also say: According to you, existence of good from evil and existence of evil from good is inconceivable. Whoever says that darkness is originated is necessarily led to the origination of evil from good, and then the origination of the world from mixture—or that is the origination of good from evil. And whoever says it is eternal is necessarily led to the world coming from mixture. And if mixture is good, then it is an acquisition of good from evil; if it is evil, then it is an acquisition of evil from good. Then if mixture were good, it would necessitate that separation is evil because it is its opposite; and if it were evil, then separation is good. And in whatever way it is considered, there must be an acquisition of good from evil and an acquisition of evil from good. And we also say: Reason necessarily judges that the mixture of two simple substances entails only one nature, yet we see diverse things in the world, differing in species, individual, reality, and form, with different natures and opposing accidents. These differences are impossible to come from the mixture of only two simple things, for they do not produce things contrary to their own essences, nor do they produce existences contrary to their own natures. Thus their statement that the universe and existences came into being from mixture is invalid. Rather, it is necessary to attribute them to a Wise, Powerful, Knowing Originator.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualist claim that darkness is an eternal principle co-existing with light. The author presents a three-part argument. First, he poses a dilemma: either darkness exists as a real entity or it does not. If it exists, it equals light in existence, making the distinction between good (light) and evil (darkness) meaningless, since existence itself is good. If it does not exist, it cannot be eternal, nor can it oppose light, nor can mixture with light produce the world. Second, he argues from the dualists' own principle that good cannot come from evil and vice versa: whether darkness is originated or eternal, the dualist position inevitably involves good coming from evil or evil from good, which is impossible. Third, he argues that reason shows that the mixture of two simple substances can produce only one nature, but the world contains vast diversity. Therefore, the world cannot arise from a mixture of light and darkness alone. The conclusion is that the world must be attributed to a single, wise, powerful, knowing Creator, not to dual principles.

Key Terms
ظلام
نور
وجود
قدم
امتزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies the existence of darkness entirely.
Correction: The argument denies darkness as an eternal principle, not its phenomenal existence.

Checkpoint
If darkness has real existence, why would it be good?
A) Because existence is inherently good.
B) Because darkness is not evil.
C) Because light is evil.
D) Because mixture is good.

Key Takeaway
The dilemma shows that the dualist cannot consistently hold that darkness is both eternal and evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If evil cannot be an eternal, independent force, then what does that mean for the evil you see in your own heart? How will you respond to the reality that your sins are not permanent fixtures but absences of good that you can replace with worship?
?
You have likely attributed your bad habits to your nature or circumstances. But this lesson shows that evil has no real eternal existence. Will you today reject the lie that your flaws are unchangeable and instead actively seek to fill that void with good deeds?
?
The dualist's error was to give darkness a status it does not possess. In what subtle ways do you treat temptations as overwhelming forces rather than mere privations? Will you now face them with the certainty that only good is truly real?

The Good/Evil Entailments
Reduces dualist position to impossibility by showing it entails good from evil or evil from good.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the dualist's own principle to show that their view is self-contradictory.

Core Idea
If good cannot come from evil, then both origination and eternity of darkness lead to absurdity.

The author now turns to a principle that the dualists themselves accept: that good cannot come from evil and evil from good. By examining both possibilities—that darkness is originated or eternal—he shows that in every case, the dualist must accept the impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the logical entailments.
2
Identify the two cases
The author examines two possibilities: darkness is originated (temporal) or eternal. Note the consequences for each.
3
Trace the good/evil chains
For each case, follow how the author derives good from evil or evil from good, regardless of whether mixture is considered good or evil.
This shows the violation of the dualist's own axiom.

?

?

?
The argument forces the dualist to admit that good and evil are intertwined in their system. In your own life, are you trying to separate good and evil too neatly, forgetting that God alone defines good? Where might you be acting as if evil has independent power?

The Mixture Argument and the Need for a Creator
Argues that mixture of two simple substances cannot produce diversity; thus a Creator is needed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the diversity of the world cannot be explained by the mixture of only light and darkness.

Core Idea
Two simple substances can only produce one nature, not the world's diversity.

The author concludes his refutation with a rational argument. Reason dictates that mixing two simple substances yields only a single nature. Yet the world is full of diverse beings. Therefore, the dualist claim that the world arises from mixing light and darkness is false. The world must be attributed to a single, wise, powerful Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the rational argument.
2
Understand the premise
The author states that reason necessarily judges that mixing two simple substances yields only one nature.
Simple substances have only one essence.
3
Observe the empirical evidence
The world contains immense diversity in species, individuals, forms, and accidents.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the mixture of two simples cannot produce such diversity, the dualist theory is invalid. The world must come from a single Creator.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the complexity and diversity in your own life—your relationships, struggles, successes—do you genuinely trace them back to a single Wise Creator, or do you subtly attribute them to a mixture of random causes, circumstances, or even your own efforts? Where have you been 'mixing' other sources of explanation alongside Allah, and what would it look like to let Him be the sole Originator of every detail?
?
The author says reason necessarily judges that two simples yield only one nature. If that is so clear, why do you still find yourself drawn to dualistic thinking—seeing good and evil as equal opposing forces, or your sins as something that can 'mix' with your faith to define you? Will you today choose to see every part of your existence as flowing from the single hand of the All-Wise, not from a chaotic mixture of independent causes?
?
You believe in a Creator who is Powerful, Knowing, and Wise. But as you reflect on the diversity of your own feelings and circumstances—joy and grief, faith and doubt—are you living as if that diversity is still under His wise control, or are you acting as if it's a random mixture you must manage on your own? What specific step can you take right now to submit one area of confusion to the One Who created every difference with purpose?

Critique of the Mu'tazila's Inconsistent Use of Tamānuʿ
The author points out the inconsistency in the Mu'tazila's application of the proof of mutual hindrance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila's use of the proof of mutual hindrance is self-defeating due to their allowance of real differences in will and action.

Core Idea
Inconsistent application of a proof undermines its validity.

In this passage, the Ash'ari author criticizes the Mu'tazila for adopting the proof of mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) to argue for the impossibility of two gods, yet they allow a real difference between God's will and human will. This inconsistency, the author argues, defeats the very purpose of the proof. Let's examine how.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the author's critique. Pay attention to the contrast between 'salkanāhu' (we followed) and 'lam yastamir lahum' (did not hold for them).
2
Identify the two halves of the argument
First, the Mu'tazila use the proof; second, they allow difference in will and action, negating mutual hindrance. The key words are 'ikhtilāfan fī al-irāda' and 'ikhtilāfan fī al-fiʿl'.
3
Reflect on the logical implication
If two wills can differ, then why can't two gods? The proof loses its force.
This is the heart of the critique.
4
Consider the broader theological context
This critique highlights the importance of consistency in theological reasoning. It also shows the tension between divine omnipotence and human agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد سلكت المعتزلة طريق التمانع في استحالة الإلهين كما سلكناه، ولم يستمر لهم ذلك حيث جوزوا اختلافًا في إرادة الباري سبحانه وإرادة العبد، واختلافا في الفعل فلا تمانع.
And the Mu'tazila have followed the path of mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) in the impossibility of two gods, just as we have followed it. But that did not hold for them, for they allowed a difference between the will of the Exalted Creator and the will of the servant, and a difference in action, so there is no mutual hindrance.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a brief critique of the Mu'tazila's inconsistency. The author notes that the Mu'tazila use the same proof of mutual hindrance (burhān al-tamānuʿ) that the Ash'aris use to demonstrate that there cannot be two gods. However, the Mu'tazila undermine this proof by allowing a real difference between God's will and the servant's will, and a corresponding difference in action. If such differences are possible, then there could be two divine wills acting differently, which would contradict the very principle of mutual hindrance that supposedly proves the impossibility of two gods. The author implies that the Mu'tazila have failed to apply the proof consistently, since they admit a form of duality (between God and man's will) that should entail the possibility of two gods if taken to its logical conclusion.

Key Terms
تمانع
إلهين
إرادة
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of mutual hindrance only shows that two gods cannot exist, but it does not rule out differences in will between a single God and His creatures.
Correction: The author argues that if differences in will are allowed between God and man, then the same logic would allow for two gods, because mutual hindrance occurs when two wills conflict. Consistency demands that either the proof is valid for all cases or it is invalid.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the Mu'tazila's use of the proof of mutual hindrance is inconsistent?
A) They do not accept the proof at all.
B) They allow a difference between God's will and man's will, which should also allow for two gods.
C) They apply the proof only to divine attributes, not to the essence.
D) They use the proof to affirm free will.

Key Takeaway
Consistency in applying a logical proof is crucial; the Mu'tazila's allowance of real difference in will and action undermines their own use of the proof of mutual hindrance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, are there core Islamic principles you claim to hold, yet your actions in other areas contradict them? Where do you allow a 'difference in will and action' that undermines a truth you claim to follow? Will you examine that area today and commit to consistency?
?
The Mu'tazila adopted a proof that should have led them to a consistent position, but they failed because of other beliefs. Think of a belief you hold that should logically shape every aspect of your life—are you allowing exceptions? What concrete step will you take this week to align your actions with that conviction?
?
This passage shows that even the learned can be inconsistent. When you notice a gap between your stated faith and your actual choices, do you correct it or dismiss it? After this lesson, identify one inconsistency and resolve it before you sleep tonight.

Two Types of Tashbih and Their Proponents
Classifies two types of anthropomorphism and lists groups holding each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of anthropomorphism (tashbih) as classified by Sanusi and the specific groups associated with each type.

Core Idea
Anthropomorphism takes two opposite forms: lowering God to creation or raising creation to God.

Sanusi now addresses deviant beliefs that violate divine transcendence. He identifies two forms of anthropomorphism found among extremist Shi'a sects and their followers. This passage names the groups and shows how each type distorts the relationship between Creator and creation. Pay attention to the hadith cited by the first group and the comparison to Christian incarnation in the second.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice the two types of tashbih introduced at the beginning.
The word 'tashbih' means likening or comparison.
2
Identify the first type and its groups
Find the first type: likening the Creator to creation. Note the three groups named (Mughiriyya, Bayaniyya, Hashimiyya) and the additional 'mushabbiha al-sifatiyya' who rely on a hadith.
The hadith 'God created Adam in the form of the Merciful' is controversial in interpretation.
3
Identify the second type and its groups
Find the second type: likening creation to Creator. Note that it involves belief in a person being God or containing a part of God. The passage compares this to Christian and incarnationist doctrines.
This type is essentially incarnation (hulul).
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why Sanusi includes this classification. It shows that the fourth principle (divine transcendence) refutes both extremes. The two types are not mutually exclusive—some groups may hold both.
This connects to the broader goal of preserving tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسارت الغالبة من الشيعة إلى نوعي تشبيه؛ أحدهما تشبيه الخالق بالخلق، فقالت المغيرية والبيانية والهاشمية ومن تابعهم: إنه الإله ذو صورة مثل صور الإنسان ونسج على منوالهم جماعة من مشبهة الصفاتية متمسكين بقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: خلق الله آدم على صورة الرحمن وفي رواية: على صورته. والنوع الثاني تشبيه المخلوق بالخالق، فقالت هؤلاء من الغالبة وجماعة أخرى: إن شخصا من الأشخاص إله أو فيه جزء من الإله والإله سبحانه وتعالى عن قولهم متشخص به نسج على منوال النصارى والحلولية في كل أمة.
And the extreme [sect] of the Shi'a went to two types of anthropomorphism: one of them is likening the Creator to the creation, so the Mughiriyya, the Bayaniyya, the Hashimiyya and their followers said: He is a god having a form like the form of a human being; and a group of anthropomorphists among the attribute-people followed their example, clinging to his saying, peace be upon him: 'God created Adam in the form of the Merciful,' and in another narration: 'in His form.' The second type is likening the creation to the Creator, so these from the extreme [sect] and another group said: A certain person is a god or contains a part of the god, and God—exalted and high above their saying—is incarnated in him; they followed the example of the Christians and the incarnationists in every nation.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage classifies anthropomorphism into two types as held by the extremist Shi'a sects. The first type is likening the Creator to the creation (تشبيه الخالق بالخلق). The author names three groups: the Mughiriyya, the Bayaniyya, and the Hashimiyya, who claimed that God has a human-like form. They are followed by a group of anthropomorphists among the attribute-people (مشبهة الصفاتية) who appeal to the hadith 'God created Adam in the form of the Merciful' (or 'in His form'). The second type is likening the creation to the Creator (تشبيه المخلوق بالخالق), where these same extremists and another group say that a certain person is God or contains a part of God, i.e., incarnation. The author notes that this resembles Christian and incarnationist doctrines in every nation. The passage thus highlights deviant doctrines that the fourth principle refutes.

Key Terms
تشبيه الخالق بالخلق
تشبيه المخلوق بالخالق
المغيرية
البيانية
الهاشمية
مشبهة الصفاتية
الحلولية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two types of tashbih are mutually exclusive and belong to different sects.
Correction: The passage shows that the same extremists can hold both types, and the second type also includes other groups.

Checkpoint
Which of the following groups is associated with the first type of tashbih (likening Creator to creation)?
A) The Christians
B) The Mughiriyya
C) The incarnationists
D) The Karramiyya

Key Takeaway
Sanusi identifies two opposing forms of anthropomorphism—lowering God to creation and elevating creation to God—and shows that both deviate from divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever subconsciously think of God in human terms—imagining Him with emotions like anger or love as if He were human? How might that be a subtle form of tashbih? What concrete step can you take today to purify your concept of God?
?
Are there any figures in your life—scholars, leaders, celebrities—whom you mentally place above their status, almost as if they are infallible or divine? How does that mirror the extremists' error? Commit to correcting that by recognizing God's exclusive rights.
?
Sanusi says both types deviate from divine transcendence. Is your daily worship centered on tanzih—declaring God's perfection beyond likeness? Do your prayers, supplications, and thoughts reflect absolute uniqueness? If not, what specific change will you make today?

Karramiyya on God's Nature and Temporal Attributes
Explains Karramiyya beliefs that God is a substance, body, and locus of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key claims of the Karramiyya sect about God's nature and why they conflict with orthodox Islamic theology.

Core Idea
The Karramiyya believed God is a body, in a direction, and subject to temporal change.

The Karramiyya were a sect that departed from orthodox Islamic beliefs about God's transcendence. This passage outlines their controversial views, including that God is a substance, a body, located above, and that temporal attributes like will and perception arise in His essence. As you read, consider how these claims challenge the classical doctrine of tanzih (divine transcendence).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic source once to get the rhythm, then read the literal translation. Note the technical terms used.
Pay attention to the words 'جوهر', 'جسم', 'جهة فوق', 'محل للحوادث'
2
Identify the three main claims
The passage makes claims about God being a substance/body, being in a direction, and being a locus for events. Find these in the text.
3
Analyze the mechanism of temporal attributes
When God creates something, a will occurs in His essence. They also mention hearing and sight occurring. Notice how they avoid the term 'أحدث' and use 'حدثت له' instead.
This implies change in God, which orthodox theology rejects.
4
List the five temporal attributes
The passage states that five temporal attributes arise. Try to identify them from the text: will, Kaf-Nun (creative command), hearing, sight – that is four; the fifth may be implied or the count includes something else.
Reflect on the significance of calling them 'حادثة' (temporal).
5
Connect to orthodox theology
Ask yourself how these views contradict the mainstream Sunni belief that God is not a body, not in a direction, and not subject to temporal events.
This is a core difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الكرامية من صار إلى أنه جوهر وجسم وأطبقوا على أنه بجهة فوق وأنه محل الحوادث قالوا: إذا خلق الله سبحانه جوهرًا أحدث في ذاته إرادة حدوثه، وربما احترزوا عن لفظ أحدث فقالوا: حدثت له إرادة حدوثه وكاف ونون، وإذا كان المحدث مسموعا حدث له تسمع، وإذا كان مبصراً حدث له تبصر، فتحدث له خمس من الصفات الحادثة.
And among the Karramiyya, some held that He is a substance and a body, and they agreed that He is in a direction above and that He is a locus for events. They said: When God, exalted, creates a substance, He causes in His essence a will for its occurrence; and perhaps they avoided the expression 'He caused' and said: 'There occurred for Him a will for its occurrence,' and 'Kaf' and 'Nun' [i.e., the letters of creation]; and when the created thing is audible, there occurs for Him hearing; and when it is visible, there occurs for Him sight; so there occur for Him five temporal attributes.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents the views of the Karramiyya sect regarding the nature of God. Some of them assert that God is a substance (جوهر) and a body (جسم). They all agree that God is in a direction: above (جهة فوق), and that He is a locus for events (محل للحوادث). They elaborate: when God creates a substance, He brings about a will in His own essence (ذاته) for that creation. They are cautious about the term 'He caused' (أحدث) because it implies temporality, so they rephrase it as 'there occurred for Him a will' (حدثت له إرادة). They also apply this to attributes: when a created thing is audible, 'hearing' (تسمع) occurs in God; when visible, 'sight' (تبصر) occurs. Hence, five temporal attributes (صفات حادثة) arise in God. This clearly contradicts the orthodox position that God is not subject to temporal events or accidents.

Key Terms
الكرامية
جوهر
جسم
جهة فوق
محل للحوادث
صفات حادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the Karramiyya believe about the direction of God?
A) He is everywhere
B) He is in no direction
C) He is in a direction above
D) He is in the hearts of believers

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya doctrine, by making God a locus for temporal events, contradicts the orthodox Islamic principle of divine transcendence (tanzih).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you ever catch yourself imagining Him as having a body or being located somewhere? The Karramiyya fell into this error. What practical step can you take today to remind yourself that God is beyond all physical description?
?
The Karramiyya believed that God's attributes change when He interacts with creation. Examine your own heart: Do you sometimes think God's love, anger, or mercy fluctuate like human emotions? How does maintaining His unchanging nature affect your trust in Him?
?
This lesson shows how a sect compromised God's transcendence by overstating His involvement with temporal events. Are there areas in your life where you reduce God to fit your own understanding, rather than submitting to His majesty? Write down one assumption you need to surrender to Him.

Karramiyya: God's Direction and Locus for Accidents
Introduces Karramiyya's doctrines on God's direction and locus for accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya sect's view that God is in a direction above and that He is a locus for originated accidents, and their cautious terminology.

Core Idea
The Karramiyya affirmed God's direction and the indwelling of originated attributes in His essence.

The Karramiyya were a sect known for anthropomorphic tendencies. This passage presents their foundational view that God is spatially above and that accidents (originated events) inhere in His essence. They use careful language to avoid the crudest forms of anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Focus on the two main claims: direction and locus for accidents.
The passage uses Arabic terms like 'جهة فوق' and 'محل الحوادث'.
2
Identify the cautious language
Notice how the Karramiyya avoid the word 'originates' (أحدث) and instead say 'there occurred for Him' (حدثت له). Also note their avoidance of 'indwelling' (حلول) and 'locus' (محل).
They still affirm the attributes as originated in God's essence.
3
Reflect on the theological motivation
Ask yourself why the Karramiyya felt the need for this careful terminology. They wanted to affirm God's attributes without falling into crass anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطبقوا على أنه بجهة فوق وأنه محل الحوادث قالوا: إذا خلق الله سبحانه جوهرًا أحدث في ذاته إرادة حدوثه، وربما احترزوا عن لفظ أحدث فقالوا: حدثت له إرادة حدوثه وكاف ونون، وإذا كان المحدث مسموعا حدث له تسمع، وإذا كان مبصراً حدث له تبصر، فتحدث له خمس من الصفات الحادثة محدث أحدثه، وربما احترزوا عن إطلاق لفظ الحلول والمحل، وإن أطلقوا الاتصاف بالحادث، وفرقوا بين المشيئة والإرادة فقالوا: مشيئة قديمة وإرادة حادثة، ولذلك فرقوا بين التكوين والمكون، والأحداث والمحدث، والخلق والمخلوق، فالخلق حادث في ذاته والمخلوق مباين وكذلك التكوين عبارة عن قوله كن والقول قايم بذاته والمكون مباين.
وكذلك كلامه تعالى صفات تحدث له وهي عبارات منتظمة من حروف وأصوات عند بعضهم، وعند بعضهم من حروف مجردة، فهو حادث ليس بقديم ولا محدث، وأحالوا فناء ما حدث من الصفات في ذاته، ولم يطلقوا لفظ التقدم والتأخر على الحروف والكلمات، واجتهد محمد بن الهيصم منهم في كل مسألة من مسائل التشبيه حتى رد الخلاف فيها إلى ما يسوغ أن يذكر ولا يسفه غير مسألة الحوادث، فإنه تركها على التكال الأول بعلم صاحبه أبي عبد الله الكرام.
And they agreed that He is in a direction above and that He is a locus for accidents. They said: When God—glory be to Him—creates a substance, He originates in His essence a will for its occurrence, and sometimes they were cautious about the word 'originates' and said: 'There occurred for Him a will for its occurrence, and Kaf and Nun.' And when the originated thing is audible, there occurs for Him hearing (tasammu'); and when it is visible, there occurs for Him seeing (tabassur). So there occur for Him five originated attributes: an originated thing He originated. And sometimes they were cautious about using the term 'indwelling' (al-hulul) and 'locus' (al-mahall), although they used the description by the originated. And they distinguished between the eternal will (mashi'a) and the temporal will (irada), saying: the eternal will is eternal and the temporal will is originated. Therefore they distinguished between origination (takwin) and the originated thing (mukawwan), and bringing into existence (ihdath) and the brought into existence (muhdath), and creation (khalq) and the created (makhluq). For creation is originated in His essence while the created is separate; similarly, origination is an expression of His saying 'Be' (kun), and the speech is subsisting in His essence while the originated thing is separate.
And likewise, His speech—exalted is He—are attributes that occur for Him, which are ordered expressions of letters and sounds according to some of them, and according to others of mere letters, so it is originated, not eternal nor created (muhdath). And they deemed impossible the annihilation of what has occurred of the attributes in His essence, and they did not apply the term 'priority' and 'posteriority' to the letters and words. And Muhammad ibn al-Haysam among them exerted himself in every matter of anthropomorphism until he reduced the disagreement in it to what is permissible to mention and is not foolish, except for the issue of originated accidents (al-hawadith), for he left it on the first doctrine (al-takal?) with the knowledge of its proponent Abu 'Abd Allah al-Karrami.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage describes the theological doctrines of the Karramiyya sect regarding God's attributes. It begins by stating their view that God is in a direction (above) and that He is a 'locus for accidents' (mahall al-hawadith), meaning that originated events inhere in His essence. They elaborate: when God creates a substance, He originates a will in His essence for its occurrence. They sometimes avoid the term 'originates' and instead say 'there occurred for Him a will' — a subtle verbal shift. They then mention that if the originated thing is audible, hearing occurs for God; if visible, seeing occurs; thus five originated attributes arise (perhaps will, hearing, seeing, etc., though the text says 'five' but only lists three explicitly). They also try to avoid terms like 'indwelling' (hulul) and 'locus' (mahall) to soften the anthropomorphism, though they still affirm attributes as originated in God's essence. Next, they distinguish between eternal will (mashi'a) and temporal will (irada), claiming the former is eternal and the latter originated. This leads to further distinctions: takwin (origination) is an eternal attribute? Actually they say takwin is God's saying 'Be' (kun), and that speech (al-qawl) is subsisting in His essence, while the originated thing (mukawwan) is separate from Him. Similarly, they differentiate between creation (khalq) as an originated act in His essence and the created thing (makhluq) as external. They then discuss God's speech (kalam): it consists of originated attributes (letters and sounds) according to most, or just letters according to some, and it is originated (hadith) — neither eternal (qadim) nor created (muhdath). They deny the annihilation of these speech-attributes. Finally, they mention Muhammad ibn al-Haysam, who reduced most anthropomorphic issues to acceptable expressions except the problem of originated accidents (mas'alat al-hawadith), which he left as taught by Abu 'Abd Allah al-Karrami, possibly meaning he adhered to the founder's original view.

Key Terms
محل الحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Karramiyya denied all originated attributes in God.
Correction: They actually affirmed five originated attributes in God's essence, but used cautious language.

Checkpoint
How many originated attributes does the passage say occur for God?
A) Three
B) Five
C) Seven
D) None

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya believed God is spatially above and that originated attributes inhere in His essence, but they used euphemistic language to soften anthropomorphism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Karramiyya were cautious in their language to avoid saying God changes. Are there beliefs or practices in your own life where you use ‘safe’ words to avoid facing a hard truth about yourself or your relationship with God? What would it cost you to speak plainly before Him today?
?
When you consider the moment of your creation, do you think God willed you from eternity or at that moment? How does your answer affect your sense of purpose and urgency in each day?
?
The Karramiyya struggled to affirm God's actions while protecting His transcendence. In what area of your faith do you feel torn between accepting a difficult doctrine and making it comfortable for yourself? Will you submit to what God has revealed, even if it unsettles you?

Karramiyya: Distinction Between Will and Origination
Explains Karramiyya's distinction between eternal and temporal will and parallel distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya distinction between eternal will (mashi'a) and temporal will (irada) and their parallel distinctions for origination and creation.

Core Idea
The Karramiyya distinguished between an eternal will and a temporal will in God, and similarly between origination and the originated.

After establishing that attributes occur in God's essence, the Karramiyya refine their doctrine by distinguishing between two types of will: one eternal and one originated. This allows them to maintain God's eternality while still affirming that He wills created things in time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on wills
Read the passage that begins 'وفرقوا بين المشيئة والإرادة' and the translation. Note the two terms: mashi'a and irada.
Mashi'a is often translated as 'will' and irada as 'will' also, but here they are distinguished.
2
Identify the distinction
The passage says mashi'a is eternal (qadima) and irada is originated (haditha). Understand the implication: God has an eternal will that does not change, but also a temporal will that occurs in time.
3
Extend the distinction
Notice how they apply the same pattern to takwin/mukawwan, ihdath/muhdath, khalq/makhluq. In each pair, the first is an attribute in God's essence, the second is the external object.
For example, creation (khalq) is an attribute in God's essence, while the created (makhluq) is separate from Him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطبقوا على أنه بجهة فوق وأنه محل الحوادث قالوا: إذا خلق الله سبحانه جوهرًا أحدث في ذاته إرادة حدوثه، وربما احترزوا عن لفظ أحدث فقالوا: حدثت له إرادة حدوثه وكاف ونون، وإذا كان المحدث مسموعا حدث له تسمع، وإذا كان مبصراً حدث له تبصر، فتحدث له خمس من الصفات الحادثة محدث أحدثه، وربما احترزوا عن إطلاق لفظ الحلول والمحل، وإن أطلقوا الاتصاف بالحادث، وفرقوا بين المشيئة والإرادة فقالوا: مشيئة قديمة وإرادة حادثة، ولذلك فرقوا بين التكوين والمكون، والأحداث والمحدث، والخلق والمخلوق، فالخلق حادث في ذاته والمخلوق مباين وكذلك التكوين عبارة عن قوله كن والقول قايم بذاته والمكون مباين.
وكذلك كلامه تعالى صفات تحدث له وهي عبارات منتظمة من حروف وأصوات عند بعضهم، وعند بعضهم من حروف مجردة، فهو حادث ليس بقديم ولا محدث، وأحالوا فناء ما حدث من الصفات في ذاته، ولم يطلقوا لفظ التقدم والتأخر على الحروف والكلمات، واجتهد محمد بن الهيصم منهم في كل مسألة من مسائل التشبيه حتى رد الخلاف فيها إلى ما يسوغ أن يذكر ولا يسفه غير مسألة الحوادث، فإنه تركها على التكال الأول بعلم صاحبه أبي عبد الله الكرام.
And they agreed that He is in a direction above and that He is a locus for accidents. They said: When God—glory be to Him—creates a substance, He originates in His essence a will for its occurrence, and sometimes they were cautious about the word 'originates' and said: 'There occurred for Him a will for its occurrence, and Kaf and Nun.' And when the originated thing is audible, there occurs for Him hearing (tasammu'); and when it is visible, there occurs for Him seeing (tabassur). So there occur for Him five originated attributes: an originated thing He originated. And sometimes they were cautious about using the term 'indwelling' (al-hulul) and 'locus' (al-mahall), although they used the description by the originated. And they distinguished between the eternal will (mashi'a) and the temporal will (irada), saying: the eternal will is eternal and the temporal will is originated. Therefore they distinguished between origination (takwin) and the originated thing (mukawwan), and bringing into existence (ihdath) and the brought into existence (muhdath), and creation (khalq) and the created (makhluq). For creation is originated in His essence while the created is separate; similarly, origination is an expression of His saying 'Be' (kun), and the speech is subsisting in His essence while the originated thing is separate.
And likewise, His speech—exalted is He—are attributes that occur for Him, which are ordered expressions of letters and sounds according to some of them, and according to others of mere letters, so it is originated, not eternal nor created (muhdath). And they deemed impossible the annihilation of what has occurred of the attributes in His essence, and they did not apply the term 'priority' and 'posteriority' to the letters and words. And Muhammad ibn al-Haysam among them exerted himself in every matter of anthropomorphism until he reduced the disagreement in it to what is permissible to mention and is not foolish, except for the issue of originated accidents (al-hawadith), for he left it on the first doctrine (al-takal?) with the knowledge of its proponent Abu 'Abd Allah al-Karrami.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage describes the theological doctrines of the Karramiyya sect regarding God's attributes. It begins by stating their view that God is in a direction (above) and that He is a 'locus for accidents' (mahall al-hawadith), meaning that originated events inhere in His essence. They elaborate: when God creates a substance, He originates a will in His essence for its occurrence. They sometimes avoid the term 'originates' and instead say 'there occurred for Him a will' — a subtle verbal shift. They then mention that if the originated thing is audible, hearing occurs for God; if visible, seeing occurs; thus five originated attributes arise (perhaps will, hearing, seeing, etc., though the text says 'five' but only lists three explicitly). They also try to avoid terms like 'indwelling' (hulul) and 'locus' (mahall) to soften the anthropomorphism, though they still affirm attributes as originated in God's essence. Next, they distinguish between eternal will (mashi'a) and temporal will (irada), claiming the former is eternal and the latter originated. This leads to further distinctions: takwin (origination) is an eternal attribute? Actually they say takwin is God's saying 'Be' (kun), and that speech (al-qawl) is subsisting in His essence, while the originated thing (mukawwan) is separate from Him. Similarly, they differentiate between creation (khalq) as an originated act in His essence and the created thing (makhluq) as external. They then discuss God's speech (kalam): it consists of originated attributes (letters and sounds) according to most, or just letters according to some, and it is originated (hadith) — neither eternal (qadim) nor created (muhdath). They deny the annihilation of these speech-attributes. Finally, they mention Muhammad ibn al-Haysam, who reduced most anthropomorphic issues to acceptable expressions except the problem of originated accidents (mas'alat al-hawadith), which he left as taught by Abu 'Abd Allah al-Karrami, possibly meaning he adhered to the founder's original view.

Key Terms
المشيئة
الإرادة
التكوين
المكون
الخلق
المخلوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Karramiyya believed in two separate Gods or a changing God.
Correction: They maintained one God with both an eternal will and a temporal will, the latter being an originated attribute in His essence.

Checkpoint
Which pair did the Karramiyya distinguish as eternal vs. originated?
A) Takwin and mukawwan
B) Mashi'a and irada
C) Khalq and makhluq
D) Qadim and hadith

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya distinguished an eternal will from a temporal will in God, and applied the same pattern to origination and creation to explain God's relationship to temporal acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will includes both an eternal dimension and a specific, temporal willing for each event in your life, do you truly live with the conviction that He is intimately and actively involved in the smallest details of your day, or do you functionally behave as if He only set a distant plan and left you to navigate alone?
?
When you face a trial, do you consciously remember that God's temporal will (irāda) is present in that very moment, specifically willing what you experience, or do you only think of His eternal will as a static decree that removes His nearness?
?
How does the Karramiyya teaching that creation itself is an attribute at work in God's essence challenge your perception of God's involvement in the world? Are you comfortable with the idea that God's attributes are not all static but include active, temporal expressions that draw Him closer to His creation?

Karramiyya: God's Speech as Originated Attributes
Explains Karramiyya view on God's speech as originated, composed of letters/sounds, and non-annihilating.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya view that God's speech consists of originated attributes (letters and sounds) and that it is neither eternal nor created.

Core Idea
For the Karramiyya, God's speech is a temporal attribute composed of letters and sounds, yet not a created thing.

The Karramiyya extend their doctrine of originated attributes to God's speech. They affirm that God's speech is composed of letters and sounds that occur in His essence, and they deny both its eternity and its creation. They also deny that these speech-attributes can ever cease.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on speech
Read the Arabic and translation: 'وكذلك كلامه تعالى صفات تحدث له ... وأحالوا فناء ما حدث من الصفات'. Note the description of speech as 'originated' (hadith) but not 'created' (muhdath).
2
Analyze the composition
The passage says speech consists of ordered expressions of letters and sounds, or just letters according to some. This means God's speech is literally composed of linguistic elements that occur temporally.
3
Understand the non-annihilation claim
The Karramiyya believe that once these speech-attributes occur in God's essence, they never cease (fana'). They reject applying 'priority' or 'posteriority' to the letters and words.
This raises questions about the temporality of the Qur'an.
4
Note the final remark on Ibn al-Haysam
The passage ends by mentioning Muhammad ibn al-Haysam, who allowed discussion of most anthropomorphic issues but left the problem of originated accidents unresolved.
This shows internal diversity within the sect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطبقوا على أنه بجهة فوق وأنه محل الحوادث قالوا: إذا خلق الله سبحانه جوهرًا أحدث في ذاته إرادة حدوثه، وربما احترزوا عن لفظ أحدث فقالوا: حدثت له إرادة حدوثه وكاف ونون، وإذا كان المحدث مسموعا حدث له تسمع، وإذا كان مبصراً حدث له تبصر، فتحدث له خمس من الصفات الحادثة محدث أحدثه، وربما احترزوا عن إطلاق لفظ الحلول والمحل، وإن أطلقوا الاتصاف بالحادث، وفرقوا بين المشيئة والإرادة فقالوا: مشيئة قديمة وإرادة حادثة، ولذلك فرقوا بين التكوين والمكون، والأحداث والمحدث، والخلق والمخلوق، فالخلق حادث في ذاته والمخلوق مباين وكذلك التكوين عبارة عن قوله كن والقول قايم بذاته والمكون مباين.

?

The Karrāmiyya Doctrine of Accidents in God's Essence
Sanusi presents the Karrāmiyya view on accidents in God's essence, clarifying their subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karrāmiyya sect's controversial view that accidents (hawadith) can subsist in God's essence, and how they distinguished between different types of divine actions to avoid full anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
The Karrāmiyya limited 'accidents' in God to will and speech, excluding most divine acts.

The Karrāmiyya were a theological sect that argued for the possibility of accidents (temporal attributes) occurring in God's essence. While this might seem to lead to anthropomorphism (tashbih), they developed a nuanced classification to distinguish between attributes that arise from eternal power and those that are genuine 'accidents'. This passage from Sanusi explains their position and the internal disagreements within the sect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to get the overall flow. Notice that the author begins by linking this view to tashbih.
2
Identify the two categories of attributes
The Karrāmiyya divide divine attributes into two categories: those that are 'accidents' (ahdath) and those that are not. Identify what qualifies as an accident and what does not.
Accidents are only will (irada) and speech (qawl) like 'Be' (kun). Other divine speech (reports, etc.) is by eternal power, not accidents.
3
Compare the two Karrāmiyya sub-views
Note the difference between the majority view (accidents = speech and will as two letters) and Muhammad ibn al-Haytham's view (accidents = will and preference, conditioned by speech).
Ibn al-Haytham adds 'preference' and requires speech as a condition.
4
Consider the controversial extension
The passage ends with a note that some allowed a single accident to be related to two creators if they are of the same genus. Consider why this might be problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما أوجب التشبيه قيام الحوادث بذاته سبحانه، وقد ذهبت الكرامية إلى جواز ذلك، ومن مذهبهم إنما يحدث من المحدثات فإنما يحدث بإحداث الباري سبحانه، والأحداث عبارة عن صفات تحدث في ذاته من إرادة لتخصيص الفعل بالوجود ومن أقوال مرتبة من حروف مثل قوله كن، وأما ساير الأقوال كالأخبار عن الأمور الماضية والآتية، والكتب المنزلة على الرسل عليهم السلام، والقصص والوعد والوعيد، والأحكام والأوامر والنواهي، والتسمعات للمسموعات والتبصرات للمبصرات فتحدث في ذاته بقدرته الأزلية، وليست هي من الأحداث في شيء، وعلى طريقة إنما الأحداث في الخلق على مذهب أكثرهم قول وإرادة والقول هما صورتان هما حرفان. وعلى طريقة محمد بن الهيصم الأحداث إرادة وإيثار وذلك مشروط بالقول شرعًا، وجوز بعضهم تعلق أحداث واحد بمحدثين إذا كانا من جنس
And among the things that necessitate anthropomorphism (tashbih) is the occurrence (qiyam) of accidents (hawadith) in His essence (dhat), exalted be He. The Karramiyya (al-Karramiyya) have gone to the permissibility of that. And from their doctrine: that whatever happens (yahduth) from that which comes into existence (muhdathat) happens only by the causation (ihdath) of the Creator, exalted be He. And 'accidents' (ahdath) are attributes that occur (tahduth) in His essence: from will (irada) for specifying the act with existence, and from words (aqwal) arranged from letters (huruf) such as His saying 'Be' (kun). As for the other statements (aqwal)—such as reports about past and future matters, the books revealed to the messengers (peace be upon them), stories, promises, threats, rulings, commands, prohibitions, hearings of heard things, seeings of seen things—they occur (tahduth) in His essence by His eternal power (qudra azaliyya), and they are not among the accidents (ahdath) at all. And according to the way (tariqa), the accidents (ahdath) in creation (khalq) according to the majority of them (the Karramiyya) are speech (qawl) and will (irada), and both are two forms (suratan) which are two letters (harfan). And according to the way of Muhammad ibn al-Haytham, the accidents (ahdath) are will (irada) and preference (ithar), and that is conditioned by speech (qawl) according to the Law (shar'an). And some of them permitted the relation (ta'allaq) of a single accident (hadath) to two creators (muhdithayn) if they are of the same genus (jins)...
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrine of the Karrāmiyya sect concerning accidents (hawadith) subsisting in God's essence, a belief the author considers to lead to anthropomorphism (tashbih). According to their doctrine, everything that occurs (yahduth) does so by God's causation (ihdath). Among these are 'accidents' (ahdath) which are attributes that occur in God's essence: specifically, will (irada) that specifies an act for existence, and speech (qawl) composed of letters, like the command 'Be' (kun). However, other types of divine speech—such as reports of past and future, revealed books, stories, promises, threats, rulings, commands, prohibitions, and perceptions—occur in God's essence by His eternal power (qudra azaliyya) and are not considered 'accidents' (ahdath) at all. The passage then notes that according to most Karrāmiyya, the true accidents in creation are only speech and will, which they describe as two forms that are two letters. Muhammad ibn al-Haytham instead identifies will and preference (ithar) as the accidents, conditioned by speech (shar'an). Finally, it mentions that some of them allowed a single accident to be related to two creators if they are of the same type. The text breaks off here.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Karrāmiyya believed that all divine actions are accidents in God's essence.
Correction: They distinguished between accidents (only will and speech) and other actions by eternal power, which are not accidents.

Checkpoint
According to most Karrāmiyya, what are the 'accidents' (ahdath) that occur in God's essence?
A) Speech and will
B) Will and preference
C) Reports and stories
D) Eternal power and will

Key Takeaway
The Karrāmiyya held a nuanced position: only will and speech (or will and preference) are accidents in God's essence; other divine acts occur by eternal power and are not accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the Karrāmiyya's meticulous distinction between accidents and non-accidents in God's essence challenge you to examine the care you take when describing Allah's attributes? Are there any times you have unwittingly described God in ways that diminish His transcendence? What will you change today to guard your tongue and heart?
?
If the Karrāmiyya went to such lengths to preserve the majesty of God's speech by distinguishing between commands and other statements, how should that impact the reverence with which you recite the Qur'an? Will you commit to reciting even one verse today as if you are hearing it directly from Allah?
?
The passage shows that some even allowed a single accident to be related to two creators. Does your heart ever try to attribute events to causes other than Allah, as if sharing His creative power? Repent of those moments and reaffirm that only Allah creates.

The Karramiyya Objection on Divine Hearing and Seeing
The author presents the Karramiyya objection and an initial counter-argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya objection that God's hearing and seeing new things implies change in God, and the initial refutation that forces them into a dilemma.

Core Idea
Claiming God experiences new perceptions forces either admitting change or contradiction.

The Karramiyya sect argued that since God hears and sees things He did not hear or see before, new faculties must arise in Him. This passage presents that objection and a clever response that exposes a logical inconsistency. By examining this exchange, we see how kalam defends divine immutability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Note the structure: first the objection, then the response beginning with 'فقيل لهم'.
Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'فيجب أن' and 'فقد تناقض'.
2
Identify the Karramiyya's claim
The Karramiyya claim that because God heard what He had not heard before, new attributes of hearing (تسمع) and seeing (تبصر) must occur in Him. Understand why they think this implies change.
3
Understand the refutation dilemma
The response forces the Karramiyya into a dilemma: either admit that God became hearing, seeing, speaking, and willing after not being so (which implies change), or deny change and contradict their own claim that God heard new things. Notice how this exposes an inconsistency.
Think about why this dilemma is effective in defending God's immutability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما شبهة الكرامية أن قالوا: سمع الباري سبحانه ما لم يسمع قبله، ورأى ما لم ير قبله فيجب أن يحدث له تسمع وتبصر. فقيل لهم: أتقولون لم يكن الباري سبحانه سامعاً للأصوات فصار سامع لها، ولا رائيًا للمدركات فصار رائيا لها، ولم يكن قايلا للأوامر والنواهي فصار قايلا، ولم يرد وجود العالم في الوقت الذي سبق وجوده فصار مريدا في الوقت الذي أوجده، فيدل كل ذلك على تجدد وصف له، وإن لم تقولوا: إنه صار سميعاً بصيراً مريدا فقد تناقض قولكم: إنه سمع ما لم يسمع، ورأى ما لم ير، وأراد ما لم يرد.
As for the sophism of the Karramiyya, they said: The Creator, glorified, heard what He had not heard before, and saw what He had not seen before, so a hearing and a seeing must occur for Him. So it was said to them: Do you say that the Creator, glorified, was not hearing sounds and then became hearing them, and was not seeing perceptibles and then became seeing them, and was not saying commands and prohibitions and then became saying them, and did not will the existence of the world at the time before its existence and then became willing at the time He brought it into existence? All of this indicates a renewal of an attribute for Him. And if you do not say that He became hearing, seeing, willing, then your statement contradicts: that He heard what He did not hear, and saw what He did not see, and willed what He did not will.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the Karramiyya sect and an initial response. The objection (shubha) is that since God hears what He did not hear before and sees what He did not see before, new faculties of hearing (tasmī') and seeing (tabṣīr) must arise in Him, implying change. The response (qīla lahum) confronts them with the logical implications of their claim: if they say God became hearing, seeing, speaking, and willing after not being so, then they admit a renewal of attributes in God. If they deny this, they contradict their own assertion that God heard and saw new things. Thus, the Karramiyya are forced into a dilemma: either accept change in God (which they likely reject) or give up their original claim.

Key Terms
شبهة
الكرامية
تسمع
تبصر
تجدد
وصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage presents a valid objection that challenges Sunni orthodoxy.
Correction: This passage actually refutes the Karramiyya by showing their claim leads to contradiction or change in God.

Checkpoint
What is the initial response to the Karramiyya objection?
A) God's hearing and seeing are eternal and do not change.
B) The Karramiyya must admit either that God changed or that their claim is contradictory.
C) God hears and sees only in a metaphorical sense.
D) The objection is invalid because God does not hear sounds.

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya's claim that God hears new things implies either change in God or a logical contradiction, both of which are unacceptable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God hearing your dua, do you subconsciously picture Him 'hearing it for the first time'? How does the Quran's teaching that God's hearing is eternal and unchanging reshape your confidence that He has always heard you—even before you spoke?
?
If you truly believed that God never acquires new knowledge or perceptions, would you still pray with the same urgency? Or does your prayer depend on the false assumption that God becomes aware of your need at the moment you ask?
?
The Karramiyya made God's attributes dependent on creation. In your daily life, are there ways you subtly treat God as if He reacts to you—getting angrier when you sin, or more pleased when you obey—rather than recognizing His attributes are eternally perfect and unaffected by your actions? How can you worship a God who never changes?

Critique of Karamite Corporealism
Criticizes the Karamite claim that God is a body

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author exposes the Karamite redefinition of 'body' as self-subsistence as a deceptive tactic, and you will be able to list the anthropomorphic doctrines of their master.

Core Idea
Redefining 'body' as self-subsistence does not hide crude anthropomorphism.

The Karamite sect attempted to defend corporealism by redefining what 'body' means, claiming they only mean self-subsistence. The author sharply dismisses this as obfuscation and reminds us of the actual, much cruder beliefs of their master. This passage illustrates how theological trickery fails to mask unacceptable doctrines about Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the tone as the author dismisses the Karamite redefinition.
The last sentence is incomplete – that is intentional from the source.

Identify the Karamite redefinition
Notice that some Karamites affirm corporeality but define it as 'self-subsistence' (al-qiyam bi al-nafs). Ask yourself: is that a valid definition?
The author calls it 'talbis' (obfuscation).

The author lists five actual beliefs of their master: locus of accidents, speaking with sounds, sitting on the Throne, direction of above, etc. See the stark difference from the tame redefinition.

Ibn Haytham was a Karamite theologian.

Understand that theological terminology can be twisted, but the actual content of beliefs matters. The author exposes the attempt to hide anthropomorphism behind a redefinition.

Critique of Karramiyya Redefinition of Body
Shahristani rejects the Karramiyya redefinition of body as self-subsistent and Ibn Haytham's reinterpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why redefining 'body' as 'self-subsistent' does not absolve the Karramiyya from anthropomorphism, and how Ibn Haytham's reinterpretations fail to fix the doctrine.

Core Idea
Redefining terms cannot hide flawed theology.

In this passage, Shahristani confronts a rhetorical strategy of the Karramiyya: redefining 'body' to avoid the charge of corporealism. He exposes the deception and also refutes Ibn Haytham's attempts to reinterpret other terms. The lesson examines the argument and its implications for theological methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the critique.
Notice the rhetorical tone and the author's dismissiveness.
2
Identify the Karramiyya redefinition
Find the phrase where the Karramiyya redefine 'body' as 'self-subsistent'. Understand why the author calls it obfuscation.
The author lists their actual beliefs to show the redefinition is dishonest.
3
Analyze Ibn Haytham's reinterpretations
Note the three terms Ibn Haytham reinterprets: body, direction of above, istiwā'. Understand why the author rejects each as a mere repair.
The author compares fixing the doctrine to straightening a crooked stick.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical conclusion
Consider the final rhetorical questions and the implication that a corrupt creed cannot be salvaged by verbal adjustments.
This underscores the lesson's core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تخطى بعض الكرامية إلى إثبات الجسمية فقال : أعني بها القيام بالنفس، وذلك تلبيس على العقلاء، وإلا فمذهب أستاذهم مع اعتقاد كونه محلا للحوادث، قايلا بالأصوات، مستويًا على العرش استقرارًا، مختصا بجهة فوق مكانا واستعلاء، فليس ينجيه من هذه المخازي تزويرات ابن هيصم، فليس يريد بالجسمية القائم بالنفس، ولا بالجهة الفوقية علاء، ولا بالاستواء استيلاء، وإنما هو إصلاح مذهب لا يقبل الإصلاح، وإبرام معتقد لا يقبل الإبرام والإحكام وكيف استوى الظل والعود أعوج ؟! وكيف استوى المذهب وصاحب المقالة أهوج ؟ ! لو الله الموفق.
And some of the Karramiyya overstepped to affirming corporeality and said: 'I mean by it self-subsistence (al-qiyām bi al-nafs).' That is obfuscation upon the rational people. Otherwise, the doctrine of their master, along with his belief that He is a locus for accidents (maḥall li al-ḥawādith), that He speaks with sounds (qā'il bi al-aṣwāt), that He is established upon the Throne as a settlement (istiqrār), that He is specifically in the direction of above as a place and elevation—thus the fabrications of Ibn Haytham will not save him from these disgraces. For he does not intend by 'body' the self-subsistent, nor by 'the direction of above' elevation, nor by 'istiwā' (establishing) domination. Rather, it is a repair of a doctrine that does not accept repair, and a stitching of a belief that does not accept stitching and firmness. How can the shadow be straight when the stick is crooked?! How can the doctrine be straight when its proponent is foolish?! May Allah grant success.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a polemical critique of a specific sect, the Karramiyya, and their apologist Ibn Haytham. The author describes how the Karramiyya attempt to avoid the charge of anthropomorphism by redefining 'body' (jism) as 'self-subsistent' (qā'im bi al-nafs). The author dismisses this as deception (talbīs), because their actual doctrine includes beliefs that God is a locus for accidents, speaks with sounds, is established on the Throne in a literal sense, and is confined to the direction of above. Ibn Haytham's reinterpretations—claiming that by 'body' they mean self-subsistent, by 'direction of above' they mean elevation, and by 'istiwā' they mean domination—are rejected as futile attempts to fix an inherently flawed doctrine. The author concludes with rhetorical questions comparing the impossibility of straightening a crooked stick to reforming such a corrupt creed.

الجسمية (al-jismiyya)

تلبيس (talbīs)
ابن هيصم (Ibn Haytham)
الاستواء (al-istiwā')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Redefining problematic theological terms can make a doctrine acceptable.
Correction: The author argues that the underlying beliefs remain anthropomorphic regardless of redefinition; terms cannot mask the substance.

Key Takeaway
Merely redefining terms does not change the underlying doctrine; true reform requires abandoning anthropomorphic beliefs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Dahriyya and the First Form of Ta'til
Author refutes the Dahriyya claim of chance origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Dahriyya claim about the world arising by chance does not constitute a denial of the Creator, and how the author distinguishes between denying the Maker and misattributing causality.

Core Idea
The Dahriyya are not atheists—they acknowledge the Maker but misattribute causality to chance.

The author examines the first form of ta'til (divesting the world of a Creator) and identifies its purported adherents: a small group of Dahriyya materialists. However, upon closer inspection, he argues that their claim does not actually deny the existence of a Maker. Instead, they err in attributing the origin of the world to random chance rather than purposeful causation. This lesson unpacks that subtle but crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a contrastive structure: 'فلست أراها... إلا...'.
2
Identify the author's key claim
The author says he does not see this as a genuine doctrine and then reframes the Dahriyya as acknowledgers of the Maker.
This is the turning point of the argument.
3
Understand the phrase 'احترازا عن التعليل'
The author explains that the Dahriyya's attribution to chance is a deliberate avoidance of affirming a purpose. This is the crux of their error.
Ta'līl here means causation by a wise agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما تعطيل العالم عن الصانع العليم القدير الحكيم فلست أراها مقالة لأحد، ولا أعرف عليه صاحب مقالة إلا ما نُقل عن شرذمة قليلة من الدهرية أنهم قالوا: العالم كان في الأزل أجزاء مبثوثة تتحرك على غير استقامة واصطكت اتفاقا، فحصل عنها العالم بشكله الذي تراه عليه ودارت الأكوار وكرت الأدوار وحدثت المركبات، ولست أرى صاحب هذه المقالة ممن ينكر الصانع، بل هو معترف بالصانع، لكنه يحيل سبب وجود العالم على البحث والاتفاق احترازا عن التعليل.
As for divesting the world of the knowing, powerful, wise Creator, I do not see that as a doctrine of anyone, and I know no proponent of it except what is reported from a small handful of the Dahriyya (materialists) that they said: The world was in pre-eternity scattered parts moving without regularity and collided by accident, and from that resulted the world in the form you see, and cycles revolved and eras recurred and compounds came into being. And I do not see the proponent of this doctrine as someone who denies the Maker; rather he is an acknowledger of the Maker, but he attributes the cause of the world's existence to chance and accident to avoid [the need for] causation (ta'līl).
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage addresses the first form of ta'til mentioned in the previous lesson: denying that the world has a Creator. The author begins by stating that he does not consider this to be a genuine doctrine held by anyone, except for a tiny group of Dahriyya (materialist philosophers) who claim that the world originated from scattered atoms moving randomly and colliding by chance, eventually forming the observed universe with its cycles and compounds. The author then makes an important observation: the proponents of this view are not actually denying the existence of a Maker. Rather, they still acknowledge a Maker, but they mistakenly attribute the cause of the world to random chance and accident, instead of proper causation (ta'līl). In other words, their error is not atheism but misattributing causality. Thus, the author dismisses this as a genuine case of ta'til because the Dahriyya do not deny the Maker. The term 'احترازا عن التعليل' means they avoid affirming a purposeful cause; they want to escape the implication of a wise agent. This passage clarifies that ta'til in its first sense (denying the world a Creator) has no real adherents.

Key Terms
التعطيل
الدهرية
الصانع
الاتفاق
التعليل
الأجزاء المبثوثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Dahriyya are straightforward atheists who deny the existence of God.
Correction: The author clarifies that the Dahriyya actually acknowledge the Maker; their error lies in attributing the world's origin to chance rather than to purposeful causation.

Checkpoint

A) They are completely atheistic
B) They actually acknowledge the Maker
C) They are polytheists
D) They are undecided

Key Takeaway
The Dahriyya claim does not constitute ta'til because it still acknowledges a Creator, but it mistakenly substitutes chance for purposeful causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever attribute events in your life to 'luck' or 'coincidence' rather than recognizing the purposeful causation of Allah? What would change if you saw every detail as part of His wise plan?
?
The Dahriyya acknowledged the Maker but denied His causal role. In what subtle ways might you, despite believing in Allah, live as though things happen by chance? How can you actively replace 'it just happened' with 'Allah willed it'?
?
If you truly internalized that nothing occurs without Allah's wise causation, what fear or anxiety would you need to let go of? What is one specific area where you can start seeing His hand today?

The Negators' Critique of Ibn al-Jabbā'ī on States
Criticism of Ibn al-Jabbā'ī's denial of knowledge for states

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the negators' criticism of Ibn al-Jabbā'ī's claim that states are not known or unknown, and why this leads to a contradiction in inference.

Core Idea
Without existence, a state cannot be known, making inference to it impossible.

This lesson examines a sharp critique from the negators (those who deny states) against the Mu'tazilī theologian Ibn al-Jabbā'ī. They find it remarkable that he denied states can be described as known or unknown. Since inference aims to establish knowledge of existence, denying both renders inference invalid and speech about states contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the tone of surprise ('wa min al-ʿajā'ib').
2
Identify the two opposing claims
First, identify Ibn al-Jabbā'ī's claim that states are not known/unknown. Then, identify the negators' counter-argument about the goal of inference.
The negators are critiquing Ibn al-Jabbā'ī, not defending states.
3
Understand the logical consequence
See how the negators conclude that inference to states becomes invalid (bāṭil) and speech about them contradictory.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن العجايب أن ابن الجباي قال : لا توصف بكونها معلومة أو مجهولة، وغاية الاستدلال إثبات العلم بوجود الشيء، فإذا لم يتصور له وجود ولا تعلق العلم به ؛ بطل الاستدلال عليه، وتناقض الكلام فيه.
And among the wonders is that Ibn al-Jabbā'ī said: It (the state) is not described as being known or unknown. The ultimate goal of inference is to establish knowledge of the existence of a thing. So if existence is not conceivable for it, and no knowledge attaches to it, then inference to it is invalid, and speech about it becomes contradictory.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage contains a specific criticism by the negators against the Mu'tazilī theologian Ibn al-Jabbā'ī. The negators find it remarkable (ʿajīb) that Ibn al-Jabbā'ī claimed that states (al-aḥwāl) are not described as being known or unknown. They argue that the purpose of inference (istidlāl) is to establish knowledge of the existence of a thing. Since the state has no existence (according to this view) and no knowledge pertains to it, any inference to it is invalid. Consequently, speaking about states leads to contradiction (tanāquḍ al-kalām).

Key Terms

الاستدلال
الوجود
المعلوم والمجهول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can know a non-existent thing.
Correction: This passage argues that knowledge requires existence; without existence, no knowledge is possible.

Checkpoint

A) States are proven to exist.
B) Inference to states becomes invalid.
C) Knowledge of states becomes certain.
D) Ibn al-Jabbā'ī's view is confirmed.

Key Takeaway
The negators argue that if states have no existence and are not known, then inference to them is impossible and discourse about them becomes contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This passage shows that denying knowledge of something while still speaking about it leads to contradiction. In your own faith, do you ever talk about things you claim are beyond human understanding, yet make definitive statements about them? How consistent is that?
?
The negators used logic to expose an inconsistency in Ibn al-Jabbā'ī's view. Are you willing to apply that same rigorous self-examination to your own theological assumptions, even if it means abandoning comfortable ideas?
