The Non-Existent as a Thing: A Survey of Positions
Survey of theological opinions on the non-existent's thingness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the range of theological positions on whether the non-existent can be called a 'thing' and how different schools defined the term.

Core Idea
The term 'thing' is a battlefield of theological definitions.

The question 'Is the non-existent a thing?' might seem abstract, but it has major implications for how we understand God's knowledge and creation. In this passage, the author surveys the different answers given by Islamic theologians, showing that even basic vocabulary can be contested.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main groups
Read the passage and list the different theological positions mentioned. Notice the Ash'aris, al-Shahham, the majority of Mu'tazilites, the opponents, and Jahm ibn Safwan.
Pay attention to how each group defines 'thing'.
2
Understand the Ash'ari position
The Ash'aris do not distinguish between existence, thingness, essence, etc. For them, these terms are synonymous. This is a key contrast with the Mu'tazilites.
3
Examine the Mu'tazilite variations
Al-Shahham said the non-existent is a thing with properties. Most Mu'tazilites followed but limited the properties. Others restricted the term 'thingness' or denied it entirely.
Note that not all Mu'tazilites agreed.
4
Consider the extreme views
Some held that only the eternal is a thing, others (Jahm) that only the temporal is a thing. Reflect on how these views affect theology.
5
Discuss the significance
Why does this debate matter? It touches on God's knowledge of non-existents, the nature of creation, and the language we use for God.
This connects to broader theological issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لعمري قد يختلف الاصطلاح والمواضعة فالأشعرية لا يفرقون بين الوجود والثبوت والشيئية والذات والعين والشحام من المعتزلة أحدث القول بأن المعدوم شيء وذات وعين، وأثبت له خصايص المتعلقات في الوجود، مثل: قيام العرض بالجوهر، وكونه عرضا ولونا، وكونه سوادا وبياضا، وتابعه على ذلك أكثر المعتزلة غير أنهم لم يثبتوا قيام العرض بالجوهر ولا التحيز للجوهر ولا قبوله للعرض، وخالفه جماعة؛ فمنهم من لم يطلق إلا اسم الشيئية، ومنهم من امتنع هذا الإطلاق أيضًا مثل أبي الهذيل وأبي الحسين البصري، ومنهم من من الشيء هو القديم، وأما الحادث فيسمى شيئا بالمجاز والتوسع، وصار جهم بن صفوان إلى أن الشيء هو المحدث، والباري سبحانه مشى الأشياء.
By my life, terminology and convention may differ. The Ash'aris do not distinguish between existence, being established, thingness, essence, and self. And al-Shahham from the Mu'tazilites originated the statement that the non-existent is a thing, an essence, and a self, and he affirmed for it the particularities of the states in existence, such as: the inherence of an accident in a substance, and its being an accident and a color, and its being black and white. And most of the Mu'tazilites followed him, except that they did not affirm the inherence of an accident in a substance, nor occupancy of space for a substance, nor its reception of an accident. And a group opposed him; among them were those who applied only the name 'thingness', and among them were those who also abstained from this application, like Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri. And among them were those who said that 'thing' is the eternal, and as for the temporal, it is called a thing figuratively and by extension. And Jahm ibn Safwan held that 'thing' is the temporal, and the Exalted Creator is the 'thing of things' (the originator of things).
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a classification of theological positions regarding whether the non-existent (المعدوم) can be called a 'thing' (شيء). The author notes that terminology varies. The Ash'aris do not distinguish between existence, being established, thingness, essence, and self—for them, all refer to the same reality. The Mu'tazilite al-Shahham introduced the idea that the non-existent is a thing, an essence, and a self, and he attributed to it certain properties that belong to existent entities, such as a substance having an accident, or a color being black or white. Most Mu'tazilites agreed but stopped short of affirming actual inherence or occupancy of space for the non-existent. Others restricted the term 'thingness' only, or denied it entirely (like Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri). Some held that only the eternal is a thing, and temporal things are called things figuratively. Jahm ibn Safwan held the opposite: only temporal things are things, and God is the 'thing of things' (i.e., the originator). This survey shows the range of views within Islamic theology on the ontological status of non-existents.

Key Terms
المعدوم
الشيء
الشيئية
الوجود
الثبوت
الذات
العين
أبو الهذيل
أبو الحسين البصري
جهم بن صفوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazilites agree that the non-existent is a thing.
Correction: The passage shows significant variation: al-Shahham started it, most followed but with limits, and some like Abu al-Hudhayl denied it entirely.

Checkpoint
Which group does not distinguish between existence, thingness, and essence?
A) The Ash'aris
B) The Mu'tazilites following al-Shahham
C) The followers of Jahm ibn Safwan
D) Abu al-Hudhayl

Key Takeaway
The term 'thing' carries theological commitments; different schools defined it to support their views on existence and God's relationship to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe in God's knowledge of all things, past and future. When you contemplate your own sins before you committed them, were they 'things' in God's knowledge? If they were not real, then how can you repent for something that never had any reality? Yet the scholars debated this very point. What does that debate reveal about your need to take your sins seriously even before they occur? Commit now to bringing your future intentions before God as real concerns.
?
The Ash'aris taught that existence and thingness are identical. If you truly believed that only what exists is a 'thing,' would you spend so much time worrying about what might happen, about futures that do not yet exist? Examine your heart: are you treating non-existent possibilities as if they were solid realities, while ignoring the present moment of your own existence? Will you repent of anxiety over the non-existent and focus on your actual state before God?
?
Some theologians held that only the eternal is a true 'thing,' and all temporal things are called things only figuratively. Then consider your life: your achievements, your wealth, your very body—they are temporary, figurative 'things.' What eternal 'thing' are you investing in? If you died tonight, would your works be real things in God's sight or vanish like a name called in jest? Let this question push you to make your deeds truly attached to the eternal.

The Mu'tazilite Doctrine of States and Its Philosophical Roots
The author defines states and traces their origin to philosophy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite definition of states and how they mixed philosophical ideas with kalām without proper comprehension.

Core Idea
States are neither existent nor non-existent — an unstable middle ground borrowed from philosophy.

The Mu'tazila attempted to describe attributes of things as 'states' that are neither existent nor non-existent. In this passage, the author exposes the confusion behind this concept, showing how the Mu'tazila adopted ideas from Greek philosophy without proper understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage definitions
The passage opens with two definitions of states: one for existents and one for non-existents. Read both carefully and note the paradoxical phrasing.
The definitions are contradictory on purpose.
2
Identify the philosophical sources
The author claims the Mu'tazila borrowed from the partisans of hyle (matter without form) and from logicians/theologians (distinction mental vs extra-mental). Find these references.
3
Understand the accusation
The author says they mixed philosophy with kalām 'unripe' — meaning before fully understanding it. Consider why this is a serious charge in theology.
This sets up the refutation in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من الأجناس والأنواع بالأحوال، وهي صفات وأسماء ثابتة للموجودات إلا توصف بالوجود ولا بالعدم، وتارة يعبرون عنها بالأشياء وهي أسماء وأحوال ثابتة للمعدومات لا تخص بالأخص ولا تعم بالأعم، وذلك أنهم سمعوا كلام الفلاسفة وقرأوا شيئًا من كتبهم، وقبل الوصول إلى كنه حقيقته مزجوه بعلم الكلام غير نضيج، وذلك أنهم أخذوا من أصحاب الهيولي مذهبهم فيها فكسوه مسألة المعدوم، وأصحاب الهيولي هم على خطأ بين من إثبات الهيولي مجرد عن الصورة - كما سنرد عليهم وأخذوا من أصحاب المنطق والإلهيين كلامهم في تحقيق الأجناس والأنواع، والفرق بين المتصورات في الأذهان والموجودات في الأعيان وهم على صواب ظاهر دون الخناثى من المعتزلة لا رجال ولا نساء؛ لأنهم أثبتوا أحوالاً لا موجودة ولا معدومة، والصورة كالمتصور، وينهدم بنيانهم بأوهى نفخة، كما يتضح الحق لأوليائه بأدنى لمحة. فنقول : إذا أشار مشير إلى جوهر بعينه فنسألكم : هل كان هذا الجوهر قبل وجوده شيئًا ثابتاً جوهرًا جسميًا من حيث هو هذا أم كان جوهرًا مطلقا شيئًا عام غير متخصص بهذا؟
From the genera and species [they speak] of states (aḥwāl), which are attributes and names established for existents but not described by existence nor by non-existence; and sometimes they express them by 'things', which are names and states established for non-existents, not specific to the specific nor universal to the universal. That is because they heard the speech of the philosophers and read something of their books, and before reaching the core of its reality they mixed it with kalām, unripe. That is because they took from the partisans of hyle (aṣḥāb al-hayūlā) their doctrine concerning it and clothed it with the problem of the non-existent (mas'alat al-maʿdūm). And the partisans of hyle are in clear error in affirming hyle stripped of form — as we will refute them — and they took from the partisans of logic and the theologians (al-ilāhiyyīn) their speech on the verification of genera and species and the difference between mental conceptions and extra-mental existents, and they are evidently correct — unlike the hermaphrodites (al-khunāthā) of the Muʿtazila, neither men nor women — because they affirmed states that are neither existent nor non-existent, and the form is like the conceived; their building collapses at the weakest puff, just as the truth becomes clear to its friends at the slightest glance. So we say: If a pointer points to a specific substance, then we ask you: Was this substance, before its existence, a fixed thing, a bodily substance insofar as it is this, or was it an absolute substance, a general thing, unspecific to this?
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the Muʿtazilite doctrine of states (aḥwāl). The author explains that the Muʿtazila at times define states as attributes of existents that are neither existent nor non-existent, and at other times as 'things' for non-existents that are neither particular nor universal. This confusion arises because they mixed the views of the philosophers—specifically the partisans of hyle (who believe in matter without form) and the logicians/theologians (who distinguish mental concepts from extra-mental existents)—with kalām before properly understanding them. The author denounces them as hermaphrodites (khunāthā), neither men nor women, for affirming states that are neither existent nor non-existent. Their position is inherently weak. The author then poses a question to expose the contradiction: when one points to a specific substance, was it a fixed bodily substance before existence or a general absolute substance? This question is left to challenge the Muʿtazilite framework.

Key Terms
أحوال
هيولي
صورة
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazilite states are a coherent middle ground between existence and non-existence.
Correction: The author argues that states are neither existent nor non-existent, making them logically unstable and derived from misunderstood philosophy.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do the Mu'tazila define states for existents?
A) As attributes that are both existent and non-existent.
B) As attributes that are not described by existence or non-existence.
C) As attributes that are purely mental.
D) As attributes that are equivalent to the thing itself.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite doctrine of states is a contradictory mixture of philosophical concepts applied to theology without proper understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author calls the Mu'tazila 'hermaphrodites' for holding an unstable position. Are there areas in your faith where you try to hold a middle ground that contradicts itself? What would it cost you to be clear rather than comfortable?
?
The truth becomes clear to its friends at the slightest glance. How urgently are you seeking clear truth in your life, or are you content with the unripe mixtures of your culture and intellect?

The Refutation of States: Incoherence and a Challenging Question
The author denounces the Mu'tazila and poses a question to refute their doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's harsh critique of the Mu'tazilite states and how a simple question about a substance before existence exposes the weakness of their position.

Core Idea
Affirming states that are neither existent nor non-existent is as absurd as being neither male nor female.

The author does not hold back in his refutation, calling the Mu'tazila 'hermaphrodites' for their contradictory stance. He then poses a sharp question about the nature of a substance before existence to dismantle their framework.

3
Examine the challenging question
The author asks whether a specific substance, before existence, was a fixed bodily substance or a general absolute substance. This question aims to expose a contradiction in the Mu'tazilite framework.
The question is left unanswered, inviting the reader to see the flaw.

Refuting the Mu'tazila on States and Non-Existents
Shahrastani criticizes those who mixed kalām with philosophy on the issue of non-existents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Shahrastani's critique of the Mu'tazila for mixing kalam with philosophy and adopting the concept of 'states' (ahwal) for non-existents.

Core Idea
Mixing kalam with philosophy without proper understanding yields contradictory and fragile doctrines.

This passage from Shahrastani's Nihayat al-Aqdam takes aim at the Mu'tazila for adopting the philosophical concept of 'states' (ahwal) and applying it to the issue of non-existents. He argues that they mixed kalam with philosophy before reaching a mature understanding, resulting in a confused position that collapses under scrutiny.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the critical tone and the author's use of analogies.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify what Mu'tazila adopted from philosophers
Find where Shahrastani mentions what the Mu'tazila took from the proponents of hylé and from logicians/theologians.
3
Understand the critique of the Mu'tazila
Analyze how the author describes the Mu'tazila as 'hermaphrodites' and why their building collapses easily.
The author uses strong language to show their position is neither here nor there.
4
Reflect on the author's conclusion
Consider the final sentence: truth is clear to its friends. What does this imply about the Mu'tazila?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من الأجناس والأنواع بالأحوال، وهي صفات وأسماء ثابتة للموجودات لا توصف بالوجود ولا بالعدم، وتارة يعبرون عنها بالأشياء وهي أسماء وأحوال ثابتة للمعدومات لا تخص بالأخص ولا تعم بالأعم، وذلك أنهم سمعوا كلام الفلاسفة وقرأوا شيئًا من كتبهم، وقبل الوصول إلى كنه حقيقته مزجوه بعلم الكلام غير نضيج، وذلك أنهم أخذوا من أصحاب الهيولي مذهبهم فيها فكسوا مسألة المعدوم، وأصحاب الهيولي هم على خطأ بين من إثبات الهيولي مجرد عن الصورة كما سنرد عليهم، وأخذوا من أصحاب المنطق والإلهيين كلامهم في تحقيق الأجناس والأنواع، والفرق بين المتصورات في الأذهان والموجودات في الأعيان وهم على صواب ظاهر دون الخناثى من المعتزلة لا رجال ولا نساء؛ لأنهم أثبتوا أحوالاً لا موجودة ولا معدومة، والصورة كالمتصور، وينهدم بنيانهم بأوهى نفخة، كما يتضح الحق لأوليائه بأدنى لمحة.
From the genera and species by means of states (aḥwāl), which are attributes and names established for existents, not described by existence nor by non-existence. And sometimes they express them as things (ashyā') and they are names and states established for non-existents, not specific to the most specific nor general to the most general. That is because they heard the speech of the philosophers and read something of their books, and before reaching the core of its reality they mixed it with kalām, unripe. That is because they took from the proponents of hylé their doctrine concerning them and clothed the issue of the non-existent. And the proponents of hylé are clearly in error regarding the affirmation of hylé stripped of form, as we will refute them. And they took from the proponents of logic and the theologians their speech concerning the verification of the genera and species, and the difference between conceptualized things in minds and existing things in concrete entities, and they are clearly correct, unlike the hermaphrodites among the Mu'tazila, neither men nor women; because they affirmed states that are neither existing nor non-existing, and the form is like the conceptualized, and their building collapses at the weakest puff, just as the truth becomes clear to its friends at the slightest glance.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a criticism of those who mixed kalām with philosophy, specifically the Mu'tazila who adopted the philosophers' concept of 'states' (aḥwāl) and applied it to the issue of non-existents. The author begins by describing the notion of 'states' as attributes and names that are neither existent nor non-existent, applied to both existents and non-existents in a confused manner. He then explains that this mixing occurred because they heard and read from the philosophers without fully understanding their doctrines, before maturing in kalām. They took from the proponents of hylé (matter) and from logicians and theologians. The proponents of hylé are clearly wrong in affirming a matter stripped of form. The logicians and theologians are correct in distinguishing between mental concepts and external existents. However, the Mu'tazila are like hermaphrodites—neither fully one nor the other—because they affirmed states that are neither existent nor non-existent. The author concludes that their position is fragile and collapses easily, just as truth is clear to its followers.

المعدوم
المعتزلة
الهيولي

D) Because they are neither men nor women

Key Takeaway
Shahrastani argues that the Mu'tazila's adoption of philosophical 'states' without proper grounding in kalam leads to a self-contradictory and fragile doctrine.

The Mu'tazila's Error: Non-Existent as a Thing and States as Fixed
Author critiques the Mu'tazila's mistaken inference about the non-existent and states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila mistakenly inferred from the philosophers' distinction between mental and external existence that the non-existent is a thing and that states are fixed.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's doctrine of the non-existent rests on a misunderstanding of a philosophical distinction.

In this passage, the author critiques the Mu'tazila's theological error by tracing it back to its root: a misinterpretation of the philosophers' division between mental and external existence. He shows how this led to two false conclusions about the non-existent and states. The passage also reveals the author's own method in writing this book.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation without stopping to analyze. Let the author's critique come through.
2
Identify the two conclusions of the Mu'tazila
Notice that the Mu'tazila made two judgments based on their misunderstanding: (1) that the non-existent is a thing, and (2) that states are fixed. They are listed in the passage.
3
Understand the author's condemnation and methodology
The author calls this a bad hearing and bad answer. Then he explains why he is revealing this: to clarify the sources of schools, not for mere polemic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما سمعت المعتزلة من الفلاسفة فرقا بين القسمين ظنوا أن المتصورات في الأذهان هي أشياء ثابتة في الأعيان، فقضوا بأن المعدوم شيء، وظنوا بأن وجود الأجناس والأنواع في الأذهان هي أحوال ثابتة في الأعيان، فقضوا بأن المعدوم شيء وأن الحال ثابت. ساء سمعا وساء إجابة. ولولا أني التزمت على نفسي في هذا الكتاب أن أبين مصادر المذاهب ومواردها واشتراك منتهى إقدام العقول في مسائل الأصول، وإلا لما أهمني كشف الأسرار وهتك الأستار.
And when the Mu'tazila heard from the philosophers a distinction between the two divisions, they thought that the conceptualized things in the minds are things fixed in external entities, so they judged that the non-existent is a thing, and they thought that the existence of genera and species in the minds are states fixed in external entities, so they judged that the non-existent is a thing and that the state is fixed. Evil was it as a hearing and evil as an answer. And were it not that I have committed myself in this book to clarify the sources of the schools and their origins and the common limit of the intellects' striving in the matters of principles, I would not have cared to uncover the secrets and tear the veils.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is an explanation and critique of the Mu'tazila's error. The author states that the Mu'tazila heard from the philosophers a distinction between two kinds of existence—mental and external. They mistakenly thought that mental concepts (conceptualized things) are fixed realities in the external world. From this they drew two conclusions: first, that the non-existent (al-ma'dum) is a thing (shay'), meaning it has some reality in the external; and second, that states (ahwal) are fixed in external entities. The author condemns this as a bad hearing and a bad answer, implying it is a misunderstanding. He then adds a methodological note: his commitment in this book is to expose the sources and origins of the various schools and the common limits of reason in fundamental matters; without this commitment, he would not have bothered to uncover such secrets and reveal hidden matters. This indicates that his purpose is not mere polemic but clarification of doctrinal roots.

Key Terms
المعدوم
الحال
المعتزلة
القسمين
المتصورات
الأعيان
الأذهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the non-existent is a thing with some external reality and that states are fixed attributes existing externally.
Correction: The author shows this is based on a misunderstanding of the distinction between mental and external existence; the non-existent is not a thing in external reality, and states are not fixed entities.

Checkpoint
What did the Mu'tazila mistakenly conclude from the philosophers' distinction between the two divisions?
A) That the non-existent is nothing
B) That the non-existent is a thing and states are fixed
C) That genera and species do not exist
D) That mental concepts are identical to external objects

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's twin doctrines that the non-existent is a thing and that states are fixed are rooted in a misapplication of the philosophical distinction between mental and external existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief firmly simply because it 'made sense' in your mind, without checking whether it aligns with divine revelation or external reality? How does the Mu'tazila's error challenge you to examine your own assumptions?
?
The author writes that he would not have bothered to uncover these secrets except for his commitment to clarify sources. What does that reveal about your own responsibility when you encounter a controversial or complex theological issue—are you willing to dig into the sources rather than accept a comfortable but flawed idea?
?
If the Mu'tazila's mistake was to treat mental concepts as physical realities, what 'mental idols' might you be tempted to treat as absolute truths? How can you actively guard against that error in your own learning and practice?

The Surprising Parallel: Mu'tazila Non-Existent and Philosophers' Hyle
Concluding remark comparing Mu'tazila doctrine with philosophers' view on hyle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Mu'tazila doctrine that the non-existent is a thing has a parallel in the philosophical concept of hyle pre-existing form.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila and some philosophers agree: the non-existent has reality before creation.

In this brief concluding remark, the author reveals a striking overlap between a Mu'tazila theological position and a philosophical theory. Despite the general divide between kalam and philosophy, both schools affirm a kind of pre-existence for certain realities—whether it is the non-existent (al-ma'dum) in Mu'tazila thought or the primordial matter (hyle) in philosophy. Let's explore this parallel.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's tone as he wraps up the discussion.
2
Identify the comparison
The author explicitly compares the Mu'tazila view on the non-existent with the philosophers' view on hyle. Can you see how they are similar?
3
Reflect on the significance
Think about why the author points this out. What does it tell us about the relationship between kalam and philosophy?
This shows that theological and philosophical doctrines can sometimes converge.

وفي المسألة بقايا من المباحثات بين الفريقين، وذلك حظ الحكمة الفلسفية لا حظ الحقائق الكلامية، وقد عرفت من هذه المباحثة أن مذهب المعتزلة في المعدوم شيء هو بعينه مذهب بعض الفلاسفة في أن الهيولي موجودة قبل وجود الصورة، والله الموفق.
And in this issue there remain remnants of discussions between the two groups, and that is the lot of philosophical wisdom, not the lot of theological truths. And you have known from this discussion that the doctrine of the Mu'tazila concerning the non-existent (al-ma'dum) being a thing is precisely the doctrine of some philosophers that hyle exists before the existence of form. And God is the Success giver.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This short passage is a concluding remark that there are remaining debates between the philosophers and the theologians (mutakallimun) on this issue, but these belong to philosophy rather than to kalam. The author notes a surprising parallel: the Mu'tazila doctrine that the non-existent (al-ma'dum) is a thing (shay') is identical to the view of some philosophers that hyle (matter) exists before the existence of form. This means that, just as the Mu'tazila affirm a kind of reality for non-existent entities (like accidents before creation), some philosophers affirm the pre-existence of hyle as a substrate that receives form. The author does not elaborate further, but simply points out the similarity and ends with a prayer.

Correction: This passage shows a doctrinal overlap on the issue of the non-existent.

A) The philosophers' view that form is eternal.
B) The philosophers' view that hyle exists before form.
C) The Ash'ari view of acquisition.
D) The view of the Christians.

The Mu'tazila doctrine of the non-existent as a thing is identical to the philosophers' view that hyle exists before form.

?
God is the Success giver. Do you truly rely on divine guidance when studying doctrinal matters, or do you trust merely in human reasoning? What change can you make today to seek His guidance more earnestly?

Defending the Seen-Unseen Analogy: Two Objections and Answers
Al-Shahrastani presents two objections from the Muʿaṭṭila and the Ashʿari responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari theologians answer two key objections from the Muʿaṭṭila regarding analogical reasoning between the visible and invisible worlds.

Core Idea
Mental considerations, not real states, enable sound analogy; self-evident experience of agency bridges the gap.

The Muʿaṭṭila challenge the entire project of kalām by questioning how Ashʿarīs can compare God to human actions. They present two specific objections: one about the use of 'states' in analogy, another about agency. Al-Shahrastānī records both together with their answers. This lesson explores how the responses preserve the validity of analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the structure of the objections
The passage begins by classifying the questions: two against Ashʿariyya, two against Muʿtazila, and one against all theologians. Then it details the first two objections, which are against Ashʿariyya.
2
Examine the first objection and its answer
The first objection claims that negating states (ḥāl) makes comparison between seen and unseen impossible. The Ashʿari answer: they do not negate mental considerations (wujūh ʿaqliyya), which allow comparison via cause-effect and evidence-evidenced relations.
3
Examine the second objection and its answer
The second objection argues that since humans are not true creators (mūjid), comparing them to God as agent fails. The Ashʿari answer: we sense ease, attainment, and ability in ourselves, distinguishing voluntary from involuntary action; the mode of divine creation is a secondary question.
4
Reflect on the overall strategy
Notice how the Ashʿari responses do not grant the Muʿaṭṭila’s premises but instead redefine the basis of analogy—from real states to mental considerations, and from perfect agency to experienced capability. This preserves analogical reasoning without compromising divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخصومهم من المعطلة على هذه الطريقة أسئلة؛ منها على الأشعرية سؤالان، ومنها على المعتزلة آخران، ومنها سؤال على جميع المتكلمين. أما السؤال الأول فقالوا : أنتم إذا نفيتم الحال وقلتم : الأشياء في حقائقها تتمايز بذواتها ووجودها فكيف يستمر لكم الجمع بين الشاهد والغائب؟ الثاني : إنكم ما أثبتم في الشاهد فاعلا موجدًا على الحقيقة، وإن أثبتم فاعلا مكتسبا فكيف يستمر لكم الجمع بين ما لم يتصور منه الإيجاد وبين ما لم يتصور منه الاكتساب ؟ أجاب الأصحاب عن السؤال الأول : بأنا وإن نفينا الحال صفة ثابتة لعين مشار إليها لم تنف الوجوه والاعتبارات العقلية جمعا بين الشاهد والغايب بالعلة والمعلول والدليل والمدلول وغير ذلك فإن العقل إذا وقف على المعنى الذي لأجله صح الفعل من الفاعل في الشاهد، حكم على كل فاعل كذلك. وأما الثاني : فإنا وإن لم نثبت إيجادا وإبداعا في الشاهد إلا أنا نحس في أنفسنا تيسرا وتأتيا وتمكنا من الفعل، وبذلك الوجه امتازت حركة المرتعش عن حركة المختار وهذا أمر ضروري، ثم وجه تأثير القدرة في المقدور أهو إيجاد أم اكتساب نظر ثان، ونحن بهذا الوجه جمعنا لا بذلك الوجه الذي فرقتم.
And their opponents from the Muʿaṭṭila have questions regarding this method: among them, two questions against the Ashʿariyya, two others against the Muʿtazila, and one question against all theologians. As for the first question, they said: You, when you negate the state (al-ḥāl) and say: things in their realities are distinguished by their essences and existence, how can you maintain the comparison between the seen and the unseen? The second: You have not established in the seen world an agent who truly creates, if you establish an agent who acquires (muktasib), how can you maintain the comparison between one from whom creation is not conceived and one from whom acquisition is not conceived? The companions answered the first question: although we negate the state as an attribute subsisting in a particular entity, we do not negate the mental aspects and considerations (al-wujūh wa-l-iʿtibārāt al-ʿaqliyya) in making the comparison between the seen and the unseen by cause and effect, evidence and evidenced, and other such things; for when the intellect grasps the meaning by which the action becomes correct from the agent in the seen, it judges every agent accordingly. As for the second: although we have not established creation (ījād) and origination (ibdāʿ) in the seen, we do sense in ourselves ease, attainment, and ability for action; and by that aspect the movement of the trembling person is distinguished from the movement of the one who acts freely, and this is a necessary matter. Then, the manner of the effect of power upon the empowered—whether it is creation or acquisition—is a secondary consideration. And we have made the comparison by this aspect, not by that aspect by which you differentiated.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents objections (asʾila) from the Muʿaṭṭila (those who negate attributes) against the Ashʿariyya, the Muʿtazila, and all theologians collectively. Two specific objections are detailed along with their answers. First objection: The Ashʿariyya negate the concept of a state (ḥāl)—an attribute that is neither existent nor non-existent—and hold that things are distinguished by their essences and existence. How then can they compare the seen (shāhid) to the unseen (ghāʾib)? The answer: Although they negate the state as a real attribute, they do not negate mental considerations (wujūh ʿaqliyya). These allow for comparison through relations like cause-effect and evidence-evidenced. The intellect, upon grasping the cause of an action in the seen, applies it to the unseen analogously. Second objection: In the seen world, humans are not true creators (mūjid); at most they are acquirers (muktasib). How can you compare a true creator (God) with a mere acquirer? The answer: Even if we do not observe creation in the seen, we do experience ease and ability (taysīr, taʾattī, tamakkun) in ourselves. This distinguishes voluntary movement from involuntary (e.g., trembling). The question of whether power creates or acquires is secondary. The primary comparison is based on this experience of ease and agency, not on the mode of creation. Thus the analogy holds.

الحال
الشاهد
الغائب
فاعل
موجد
مكتسب
الوجوه والاعتبارات العقلية
المرتعش
المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negating real states (ḥāl) makes analogical reasoning impossible between seen and unseen.
Correction: Ash'aris do not negate mental considerations (wujūh ʿaqliyya) which serve as the basis for legitimate analogy.

Checkpoint
How do the Ash'ari companions answer the objection that they cannot compare seen and unseen because they negate states (ḥāl)?
A) They concede the point and abandon the analogy.
B) They argue that states are not needed because mental aspects (wujūh ʿaqliyya) allow comparison.
C) They claim the unseen is directly known by intuition.
D) They reject the objection as invalid because the Muʿaṭṭila are wrong.

Key Takeaway
The Ashʿarī defense of analogical reasoning rests on mental considerations and the self-evident distinction between voluntary and involuntary action, not on real states or perfect agency in the seen world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sense your own ability to choose—like deciding to pray or give charity—do you truly believe that this ability comes only from God? Or do you secretly credit yourself? This lesson teaches that voluntary action is a self-evident reality. Ask yourself: Is my reliance on God in my actions real, or do I feel a sense of independent power? Commit today to recite 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' before every action, not as a formula but as a deep acknowledgment that your ability is a gift, not a personal possession.
?
Have you ever doubted God's justice because you couldn't see the 'reason' behind a trial? The Ashʿarīs argue that mental considerations—not visible states—are enough to connect the seen and unseen. Right now, is there a difficulty in your life where you are demanding to see a clear cause? Will you trust that God's wisdom is real even when your mind only grasps a faint connection? Write down one trial you face and next to it, write one mental consideration (e.g., 'patience brings reward') that you can hold onto without full sight.
?
This passage shows that a self-evident experience—your sense of being able to act—becomes the basis for knowing the unseen. When was the last time you felt morally paralyzed by fear of sin? That feeling of 'I can't help it' is the opposite of the voluntary agency you know is true. Are you using the excuse of human weakness to avoid responsibility? Repent of any area where you have acted as if you have no choice—Islam affirms you do have ability, even if God creates it. Will you take one concrete step today to prove to yourself that you are a voluntary agent responsible before God?

Mu'tazila Argument: Descriptions Without Multiplicity
Mu'tazila analogize from jawhar/'arad to deny multiple divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that multiple descriptions of a single essence do not necessitate multiple distinct attributes, using the analogy of substance and accident.

Core Idea
Different descriptions of one thing do not imply multiple realities.

The Mu'tazila present a key rationalist argument against the Ash'ari view of multiple divine attributes. They draw an analogy from the physical world: a substance can be described in various ways without implying that it has multiple distinct attributes. In this passage, they apply this reasoning to God, arguing that descriptions like 'knowing' and 'powerful' refer to His single essence, not separate entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: claim, example, application.
2
Identify the analogy
The Mu'tazila use the example of substance (jawhar) and accident ('araḍ). Note how each is described with multiple predicates.
The three descriptions of substance and accident are key.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that these descriptions do not indicate multiplicity in the essence, nor multiple attributes or states. Ask yourself why this matters for divine attributes.
This is the core of the Mu'tazila position.
4
Apply to divine attributes
The Mu'tazila apply the same logic to God: saying He is knowing and powerful does not imply separate attributes.
Contrast with Ash'ari view if familiar.
5
Reflect on key terminology
Note the term 'ṣifāt al-anfus' (attributes of essences). This is central to understanding the Mu'tazila framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة : تختلف وجوه الاعتبارات في شيء واحد ولا يوجب ذلك تعدد الصفة، كما يقال الجوهر: متحيز وقائم بالنفس وقابل للعرض، ويقال: للعرض لون وسواد وقائم بالمحل، فيوصف الجوهر والعرض بصفات هي صفات الأنفس التي لا يعقل الجوهر والعرض دونهما، ثم هذه الأوصاف لا تشعر بتعدد في الذات، ولا بتعدد صفات هي ذوات قائمة بالذات، ولا بتعدد أحوال ثابتة في الذات، كذلك نقول في كون الباري تعالى عالماً قادراً.
The Mu'tazila said: The aspects of considerations differ regarding one thing, and that does not necessitate multiplicity of the attribute. Just as it is said of the substance (jawhar): it is occupying space (mutahayyiz), self-subsistent (qā'im bi-nafsih), and receptive to accidents (qābil li-l-'araḍ). And it is said of the accident ('araḍ): it is color, blackness, and subsistent in a locus (qā'im bi-l-maḥall). Thus the substance and accident are described by attributes that are attributes of their essences (ṣifāt al-anfus) without which the substance and accident cannot be conceived. Then these descriptions do not indicate multiplicity in the essence, nor multiplicity of attributes that are essences subsisting in the essence, nor multiplicity of states (aḥwāl) subsisting in the essence. Similarly we say regarding the Exalted Creator being knowing and powerful.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila argument that multiple descriptions of one thing do not require multiple real attributes. They illustrate with the example of a substance (jawhar) which is described as occupying space, self-subsistent, and receptive to accidents, and an accident ('araḍ) described as color, blackness, etc. These descriptions do not indicate any multiplicity in the essence itself, nor do they imply multiple attributes that are distinct entities subsisting in the essence, nor multiple states (aḥwāl). The Mu'tazila conclude that similarly, describing God as knowing and powerful does not imply multiple distinct attributes—these are merely different ways of referring to His single essence.

Key Terms
وجوه الاعتبارات (wujūh al-iʿtibārāt)
الجوهر (jawhar)
العرض (ʿaraḍ)
صفات الأنفس (ṣifāt al-anfus)
أحوال (aḥwāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila deny all divine attributes entirely.
Correction: The Mu'tazila do not deny that God is described as knowing and powerful; they argue that these descriptions refer to the essence itself without implying multiple distinct attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila in this passage, what does the fact that a substance can be described as 'occupying space' and 'self-subsistent' indicate?
A) That the substance has multiple distinct real attributes.
B) That these descriptions do not imply multiplicity in the essence.
C) That the substance is composed of parts.
D) That the substance is an accident.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that just as a substance's multiple descriptions do not imply multiple attributes, so God's descriptions like 'knowing' and 'powerful' refer to His single essence without multiplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that describing God as 'knowing' and 'powerful' does not imply multiplicity in His essence. Does your heart truly worship God as one unified Being, or do you sometimes imagine His attributes as separate 'parts' of God? How can you train your mind to see all His names as pointing to one single, indivisible reality?
?
If God's attributes are not distinct entities but descriptions of His essence, what does that mean for how you relate to His mercy and wrath? Are you seeking a 'part' of God or the whole of Him when you call upon Him by His names?
?
The Mu'tazila defended divine unity by denying multiple attributes. Does your concept of tawhid go beyond words? Do you live as if God alone is the source of all power and knowledge, or do you inadvertently associate partners with Him by trusting created causes as independent?

Sifatiyya: Descriptions of Essence vs. Real Attributes
Sifatiyya distinguish essential descriptions from real distinct attributes of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sifatiyya's distinction between descriptions that refer to a single divine reality and those that refer to real, distinct attributes.

Core Idea
Some divine descriptions point to one essence; others are real distinct attributes.

The Sifatiyya respond to the Mu'tazila argument by clarifying that not all divine descriptions are alike. They use an analogy from created substances and accidents to show that descriptions like 'existent' and 'eternal' simply indicate the single divine reality, while 'living', 'knowing', and 'powerful' refer to distinct attributes with their own proofs and objects. This passage establishes the foundational distinction between essential descriptions and real attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the argument unfold naturally before analyzing it.
Notice the shift from discussing substances/accidents to applying the same logic to God.
2
Identify the two categories
The Sifatiyya distinguish between descriptions that refer to a single reality (like 'existent, eternal') and those that refer to multiple distinct realities (like 'living, knowing, powerful'). Find the line that marks this transition.
In the Arabic, look for 'ترجع إلى حقيقة واحدة' versus 'حقائق مختلفة'.
3
Examine the analogy
The Sifatiyya first explain that describing a substance as 'voluminous' or an accident as 'black' refers to one reality with a property. Then they extend this to God's essential descriptions. Understand how the analogy supports their position.
The key is that some descriptions indicate a distinguishing property, not a separate attribute.
4
Reflect on the uniqueness of attributes
For the attributes of life, knowledge, and power, each has its own benefit, proof, and object. This shows they are real and distinct. Ask yourself why this distinction matters for theology.
The Sifatiyya affirm multiplicity of attributes while denying it for essential descriptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الصفاتية : ليس في وصف الجوهر والعرض بهذه الأوصاف أكثر من إثبات حقيقة واحدة لها خاصية تتميز بها عن غيرها، وهذه العبارات دالة على تلك الخاصية ؛ وهي الحجمية في الجوهر والسوادية في العرض مثلا، فأما تحيزه وقبوله للعرض فذلك تعرض لنسبة الجوهر إلى الحيز ونسبته إلى العرض إما تقديرًا أو تحقيقا، وبمثل ذلك نقول في حق الباري سبحانه: إنه موجود قديم قائم بنفسه، غني أحد صمد، غير متناهي الوجود والذات، وكل هذه الأوصاف ترجع إلى حقيقة واحدة، وأما وصفه بكونه حيًا عالماً قادرًا إنما هو راجع إلى حقائق مختلفة وخواص متباينة تختص كل صفة بخاصية وحقيقة لا تتعداها ولكل واحدة فائدة خاصة تخصها، ودليل خاص يدل عليها، ومتعلق خاص يختص بها.
The Sifatiyya said: In describing the substance and accident with these descriptions there is nothing more than affirming a single reality that has a property (khāṣṣiyya) by which it is distinguished from others. These expressions indicate that property — such as volume (ḥajmiyya) in the substance and blackness (sawādiyya) in the accident, for example. As for its being occupying space and receptive to accidents, that is a reference to the relation (nisba) of the substance to space and its relation to accident, either by estimation (taqdīran) or actuality (taḥqīqan). Similarly we say regarding the Exalted Creator: He is existent, eternal, self-subsistent, self-sufficient, one, eternal, not finite in existence and essence. All these descriptions refer to a single reality. But describing Him as living, knowing, and powerful goes back to distinct realities (ḥaqā'iq mukhtalifa) and diverse properties (khaṣā'iṣ mutabāyina). Each attribute is characterized by a property and reality that does not extend beyond it. Each one has its own specific benefit (fā'ida), its own specific proof (dalīl), and its own specific object of relation (mutaʿallaq).
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents the Sifatiyya (attributes party) response to the Mu'tazila argument. They agree that descriptions like 'occupying space' for a substance or 'black' for an accident refer to a single reality and its distinguishing property, not multiple real attributes. Similarly, descriptions of God such as 'existent', 'eternal', 'self-subsistent' all refer to one reality. However, they argue that describing God as 'living', 'knowing', and 'powerful' is different: each of these corresponds to a distinct reality (ḥaqīqa) and a distinct property (khāṣṣiyya). Each attribute has its own specific benefit, proof, and object of relation. Thus the Sifatiyya uphold the real multiplicity of attributes like knowledge and power, while distinguishing them from essential descriptions that indicate a single reality.

Key Terms
الصفاتية (al-Ṣifātiyya)
الحقيقة الواحدة (al-ḥaqīqa al-wāḥida)
الخاصية (al-khāṣṣiyya)
الحجمية (al-ḥajmiyya)
السوادية (al-sawādiyya)
الحقائق المختلفة (al-ḥaqā'iq al-mukhtalifa)
الخواص المتباينة (al-khaṣā'iṣ al-mutabāyina)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Sifatiyya deny any real distinctions among divine attributes, treating all descriptions as merely referring to the essence.
Correction: The Sifatiyya do not deny that God has essential descriptions (like existence); they separate those from real attributes like knowledge and power, which are distinct realities.

Checkpoint
According to the Sifatiyya, what kind of descriptions refer back to a single reality?
A) 'Living, knowing, powerful'
B) 'Existent, eternal, self-subsistent'
C) Both A and B
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The Sifatiyya affirm that some divine descriptions point to the single essence, while others like knowledge and power are real, distinct attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God as 'Knowing', does your heart acknowledge that His knowledge is a real, distinct perfection that embraces all things, or do you merely repeat the word without awe? How will you let this truth shape your worship today?
?
If you truly believed that God's power is a distinct attribute with its own object, would you still feel helpless in trials? What specific situation this week will you consciously entrust to His power, not just His existence?
?
The Sifatiyya argue that essential descriptions like 'Eternal' point to one reality, yet many Muslims stop there. Are you satisfied with a general belief in God, or do you actively seek to know His distinct attributes (life, knowledge, power) and let them transform your relationship with Him? What step will you take tonight to deepen your understanding of one of these attributes?

The Contradiction of the Theory of States (Ahwal)
The author refutes Abu Hashim's theory of states and compares it to the Christian Trinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite theory of states (ahwal) is self-contradictory and how it parallels the Christian Trinity.

Core Idea
States are neither existent nor non-existent, leading to a contradiction that mirrors tritheism.

The Mu'tazilite Abu Hashim proposed that divine attributes like knowledge and power are 'states' (ahwal) — neither existent nor non-existent. This passage, from a classical Ash'ari refutation, exposes the logical flaw: the theory is indistinguishable from the Christian Trinity. We'll examine the argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key claim that states are neither existent nor non-existent.
2
Identify the two theories being compared
The author compares the theory of states (ahwal) with the orthodox doctrine of attributes. What is the only difference mentioned?
The difference is that states are described neither by existence nor non-existence, while attributes are existent.
3
Analyze the Trinity comparison
The author says the state theory leads to the Christian position: one essence, three hypostases. Why does the author think this follows?
Hint: The theory posits an essence, a state of knowledge, and a state of power — three aspects that are not fully real.
4
Understand the refutation's conclusion
Ask yourself: Does the orthodox doctrine of attributes avoid this problem? How?
Orthodox attributes are real and existent, so they are not contradictory like states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو هاشم: العالمية حال والقادرية حال، ومفيدهما حال يوجب الأحوال كلها، فلا فرق في الحقيقة بين أصحاب الأحوال وبين أصحاب الصفات، إلا أن الحال متناقض للصفات؛ إذ الحال لا يوصف بالوجود ولا بالعدم، والصفات موجودة ثابتة قائمة بالذات، ويلزمهم مذهب النصارى في قولهم: واحد بالجوهرية ثلاثة بالأقنومية، ولا يلزم ذلك التناقض مذهب الصفاتية.
And Abu Hashim said: Being-knowing is a state and being-powerful is a state, and what brings them about is a state that necessitates all states. So there is no difference in reality between the proponents of states and the proponents of attributes, except that the state is contradictory to attributes, because the state is described neither by existence nor by non-existence, while attributes are existent, established, and subsisting in the essence. And the doctrine of the Christians follows from their view: one in terms of the substance, three in terms of the hypostases. But that contradiction does not follow for the doctrine of the attribute-holders.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-refutation against the Mu'tazilite theory of 'ahwal' (states) as propounded by Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i. The author reports Abu Hashim's claim that the attributes of being-knowing (al-‘ālimiyya) and being-powerful (al-qādiriyya) are states, and that what produces them is also a state that leads to all states. The author argues that this theory is essentially no different from the orthodox view of attributes, except for one crucial point: states are contradictory because they are described neither as existent nor as non-existent, whereas attributes are real, established, and subsist in the essence. The author then draws a damning parallel to the Christian Trinity: the Mu'tazilite theory thereby entails that God is one in substance but three in hypostases (aqānīm), just as Christians say. In contrast, the orthodox (Ash‘ari) doctrine of attributes does not lead to this contradiction, because attributes are understood as real and distinct without compromising divine unity.

Key Terms
حال
أحوال
أبو هاشم
الصفاتية
النصارى
أقنومية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The theory of states is a moderate position between denying and affirming attributes.
Correction: The theory is self-contradictory because states are neither existent nor non-existent, and it leads to tritheism.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that the Mu'tazilite theory of states leads to the Christian Trinity?
A) Because states are considered non-existent, like the hypostases.
B) Because the theory posits three distinct eternal entities similar to the Trinity.
C) Because states are described neither as existent nor non-existent, and the underlying logic of three aspects resembles Christian Trinitarianism.
D) Because the Mu'tazila explicitly borrowed from Christian theology.

Key Takeaway
The theory of states (ahwal) is self-contradictory because states are neither existent nor non-existent, and it unavoidably parallels the Christian Trinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that Allah is 'All-Knowing' and 'All-Powerful', do you truly believe these are real and distinct attributes subsisting in His essence, or do you sometimes reduce them to mere mental concepts? How would your worship change if you treated them as states that are neither existent nor non-existent?
?
The author warns that the theory of states leads to a Trinity-like contradiction. Have you ever caught yourself thinking of Allah's attributes as separate 'parts' that compromise His oneness? What safeguards can you put in place to protect your tawhid from such subtle errors?
?
If someone asked you why the Ash'ari position on attributes is superior to the Mu'tazilite theory of states, could you explain using the passage's argument? Would you be able to show them the logical flaw and the Trinity parallel? What does your answer reveal about your own conviction in this aqeedah?

Mu'tazila and Philosophers Object to Distinct Attributes
Mu'tazila argue attributes are not distinct entities; philosophers add independence argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objections raised by the Mu'tazila and philosophers against the doctrine of eternal attributes distinct from God's essence, including the logical arguments from multiplicity and absolute independence.

Core Idea
If attributes are eternal and distinct, they become other gods or create dependence.

In this lesson, we examine a combined objection from the Mu'tazila and a group of philosophers against the Ash'ari doctrine of eternal, distinct attributes subsisting in God's essence. The Mu'tazila argue that such attributes would either be identical to the essence (contradicting distinctness) or other than it—leading either to temporality (which is rejected) or to a multiplicity of eternal beings, implying other gods. The philosophers then reinforce this by invoking God's absolute independence, showing that distinct attributes would introduce need and dependence on both sides. This passage is central to understanding the rationalist critique of the classical Sunni position on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the structure of the objection come through.
Notice that the passage contains two distinct voices: the Mu'tazila and then the philosophers.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's main argument
The Mu'tazila present a division: if attributes are distinct, they are either identical or other. If other, they are either originated or eternal. Trace each branch and its conclusion.
The key conclusion is that eternal otherness leads to sharing in divinity.
3
Understand the implications of subsistence
The Mu'tazila then argue that attributes subsisting in the essence make them like accidents, needing a substrate. This implies priority and posteriority, which is absurd for God.
4
Analyze the philosophers' addition
The philosophers argue from absolute independence: an eternal attribute subsisting in God would nullify that independence for both the attribute and the described, because the attribute needs the essence and the essence needs attributes for perfection.
They conclude that the absolutely independent must be one in every respect.
5
Reflect on the overall case
Consider how the two arguments complement each other: the Mu'tazila focus on multiplicity and contingency, the philosophers on self-sufficiency. How would a defender of distinct attributes respond?
This reflection prepares you for the subsequent defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة: نحن لا ننكر الوجوه والاعتبارات العقلية لذات واحدة، ولا نثبت الصفات إلا من حيث تلك الوجوه؛ إما إثبات صفات هي ذوات موجودات أزلية قديمة قائمة بذاته هو المنكر عندنا، فإنها إذا كانت موجودات وذوات وراء الذات فإما أن تكون عين الذات وإما أن تكون غير الذات، فإن كانت عين الذات فهو مذهبنا، وبطل قولكم: هي وراء الذات، وإن كانت غير الذات فهي حادثة أو قديمة، وليس من مذهبكم أنها حادثة وإن كانت قديمة فقد شاركت الذات في القدم والوجوب بالذات ونفى الأولية فهي آلهة أخرى، فإن القدم أخص وصف القديم، والاشتراك في الأخص يوجب الاشتراك في الأعم. ومن الإلزامات على قولكم: إنها قائمة بذاته، أن القائم بالشيء محتاج إلى ذلك الشيء حتى لولاه لما تحقق له وجود به، فيلزمكم إثبات خصائص الأعراض في الصفات، فإن العرض هو المحتاج إلى محل يقوم به، ويلزمكم إثبات التقدم والتأخر الذاتي العقلي في الذات والصفات، وهذا كله محال. وساعدهم جماعة من الفلاسفة المعطلة في إلزام هذه الكلمات، وزادوا عليهم بأن قالوا: قام الدليل على أن واجب الوجود مستغن على الإطلاق من كل وجه، فمن أثبت له صفة ذاتية أزلية معنى وحقيقة قائمة بذاته فقد أبطل الاستغناء المطلق من الصفة والموصوف جميعا، وأثبت الاحتياج والفقر في الصفة والموصوف جميعا، أما الصفة فاحتاجت في وجودها إلى ذات تقوم بها؛ إذ يجب أن تقول: قام العلم بالباري، واستحال أن تقول: قام الذات بالعلم، وأما الموصوف فإنما يتم كماله في الإلهية إذا قامت به هذه الصفات حتى لولاها لما كان إلها مستغنيا على الإطلاق، فإذا المستغنى على الإطلاق لا يكون إلا واحدا من كل وجه ولا كثرة فيه من وجه.
The Mu'tazila said: We do not deny rational aspects and considerations for one essence, nor do we affirm attributes except in terms of those aspects. Rather, affirming attributes that are eternal existent essences subsisting in His essence is what we reject. For if they are existents and essences besides the essence, then either they are identical to the essence or they are other than the essence. If they are identical to the essence, that is our doctrine, and your statement that they are besides the essence is invalid. And if they are other than the essence, then they are either originated or eternal. You do not hold that they are originated. And if they are eternal, then they have shared with the essence in eternality and necessity by essence, negating priority, so they would be other gods. For eternality is the most specific description of the eternal, and sharing in the most specific necessitates sharing in the more general. And among the necessary implications of your statement that they subsist in His essence is that that which subsists in something is in need of that thing such that without it its existence would not be realized through it. Thus you are compelled to affirm the characteristics of accidents in the attributes, for the accident is what is in need of a substrate in which to subsist. And you are compelled to affirm essential intellectual priority and posteriority in the essence and the attributes, and all of this is impossible. And a group of the philosophers who deny attributes assisted them in necessitating these words and added to them by saying: The proof has been established that the Necessary Existent is absolutely independent in every respect. So whoever affirms for Him an eternal essential attribute, a meaning and reality subsisting in His essence, has nullified absolute independence from both the attribute and the described, and has affirmed need and poverty in both the attribute and the described. As for the attribute, it needs in its existence an essence in which to subsist, for one must say: Knowledge subsists in the Creator, and it is impossible to say: The essence subsists in knowledge. As for the described, its perfection in divinity is only completed when these attributes subsist in it, such that without them it would not be a god absolutely independent. Thus the absolutely independent can only be one from every respect, with no multiplicity in it from any respect.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the objections of the Mu'tazila and a group of philosophers against the doctrine of distinct eternal attributes of God. The Mu'tazila begin by clarifying that they do not deny rational aspects of one essence; rather, they reject the notion of attributes as separate eternal entities subsisting in the essence. They argue based on a division: if attributes are distinct from the essence, they must be either identical or other. If identical, the Mu'tazila agree, but the opponent's claim that they are 'besides' the essence fails. If other, the attributes must be either originated (which the opponent denies) or eternal. If eternal, they share with the essence in eternality and necessity, which implies they are additional gods, since sharing the most specific property (eternality) entails sharing the general category of divinity. The Mu'tazila then press a further implication: if attributes subsist in the essence, they are like accidents that need a substrate, thus requiring the attributes to have the characteristics of accidents (dependence on a subject). This also leads to a temporal priority of the essence over the attributes, which is absurd. The philosophers then reinforce this by invoking God's absolute independence: affirming an eternal attribute subsisting in Him negates that independence, because both the attribute (needs a substrate) and the described (needs attributes for perfection) become dependent. The philosophers emphasize that the attribute exists by virtue of the essence, not vice versa, and that without attributes the essence would not be a perfect God, thus it would not be absolutely independent. Therefore, the absolutely independent must be one in every respect, with no multiplicity. The passage thus combines two arguments against distinct attributes: one from the Mu'tazila focusing on multiplicity and dependence, and another from philosophers focusing on absolute self-sufficiency.

Key Terms
صفات أزلية (eternal attributes)
عين الذات (identical to essence)
غير الذات (other than essence)
قدم (eternality)
وجوب بالذات (necessity by essence)
عرض (accident)
استغناء مطلق (absolute independence)
واجب الوجود (Necessary Existent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Mu'tazila deny all attributes, including rational aspects.
Correction: The Mu'tazila explicitly say they do not deny rational aspects and considerations (wujūh) of one essence; they only reject attributes as distinct eternal entities subsisting in God.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila in this passage, what follows if attributes are distinct from the essence and are eternal?
A) They are identical to the essence.
B) They are originated (ḥāditha).
C) They would be other gods (āliha ukhrā).
D) They are mere aspects (wujūh).

Key Takeaway
This lesson shows the core rationalist objection: distinct eternal attributes would either multiply divine beings or introduce dependence, challenging the very notion of God's absolute oneness and independence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's attributes—His knowledge, power, will—do you ever unconsciously imagine them as separate 'parts' or 'things' rather than aspects of His one essence? How might this lesson correct that tendency in your own heart?
?
The philosophers say that if God needed attributes to be perfect, He would not be absolutely independent. In your worship, do you ever act as if God is 'more complete' because of His attributes rather than affirming that He is perfect without them? How does this challenge your gratitude?
?
If the Mu'tazila are right that distinct attributes imply multiple gods, then your very conception of God must be examined. Are you willing to let go of any mental image that compromises His absolute oneness, even if it feels unfamiliar or uncomfortable?

Sifatiyya Respond: Attributes Neither Identical Nor Other
Sifatiyya refute the division into identical or other, using analogy of composite realities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sifatiyya's argument that divine attributes are neither identical to the essence nor other in the sense of separable existence, and how they use analogies to defend this position.

Core Idea
Attributes are neither identical nor other: they are conceptually distinct but inseparable from the essence.

The Sifatiyya respond to the Mu'tazila's insistence that attributes must be either identical to the essence or other. They argue that the term 'other' is ambiguous: it can mean mere conceptual distinctness or separable existence. By adopting the first meaning, they reject both identity and separability. They then draw analogies with the concept of 'states' and with composite realities like humanity to illustrate that something can be neither fully identical nor fully other.

Guided Path
1
Read the Sifatiyya response
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice how they begin by mirroring the Mu'tazila's denial.
The Sifatiyya use reciprocal argumentation.
2
Understand the two meanings of 'other'
The passage distinguishes two meanings of 'ghayr' (other). Identify both meanings and see which one the Sifatiyya accept.
This is the core logical move.
3
Examine the analogy with 'states' (aḥwāl)
The Sifatiyya draw a parallel with the proponents of states, who say a state is neither existent nor non-existent. How does this support their position?
4
Analyze the composite reality example
The example of humanity composed of animality and rationality shows that a part is neither identical nor other to the whole. Apply this to attributes.
This concretizes the abstract reasoning.
5
Summarize the Sifatiyya's defense
Write in your own words why the Sifatiyya reject both identity and otherness for attributes.
Check your understanding with the quiz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الصفاتية: كما أنكرتم إثبات صفات أزلية أنكرنا عليكم إثبات موصوف بلا صفة إنكارا بإنكار، واستبعادا باستبعاد، وتقسيمكم أنها عين الذات أم غير الذات إنما يصح إذا كان التقسيم دائرًا بين النقي والإثبات، فإن من قال: هذا عينه وهذا غيره قد يعني به أنهما شيئان فهذا شيء وذاك شيء آخر، وقد يعني به أنهما شيئان، ويجوز وجود أحدهما مع عدم الثاني، أو مع تقدير عدم الثاني، ونحن لا نسلم أن الصفات أغيار ولا نقول: إنها عين الذات؛ لأن حد الغيرين عندنا هو المعنى الثاني لا المعنى الأول، ثم إن جاز لمثبتي الأحوال القول بأن الحال لا موجودة ولا معدومة جاز لمثبتي الصفات القول بأن الصفات لا عين ولا غير الذات، على أن كل حقيقة التأمت من أمرين محققين في الشاهد مثل الإنسانية، فإنها التأمت من حيوانية وناطقية عند القوم، فإذا قيل: الناطقية عين الإنسانية أو غيرها لم يصح بأنها لا عينها ولا غيرها، وكذلك جزء كل شيء لا عينه ولا غيره.
The Sifatiyya said: Just as you have denied affirming eternal attributes, we deny affirming a described without attributes, denial for denial and improbability for improbability. And your division that they are identical to the essence or other than the essence is only valid if the division revolves between negation and affirmation. For he who says 'this is identical to that' or 'this is other than that' may mean that they are two things, so this one thing and that another thing, and may mean that they are two things such that it is possible for one to exist without the other, or with the assumption of the non-existence of the other. And we do not concede that the attributes are others (aghyār) nor do we say that they are identical to the essence, because the definition of 'two others' according to us is the second meaning, not the first. Moreover, if it is permissible for the proponents of states (aḥwāl) to say that the state is neither existent nor non-existent, it is permissible for the proponents of attributes to say that the attributes are neither identical nor other than the essence. Moreover, every reality that is composed of two realized matters in the witnessed world, such as humanity, for it is composed of animality and rationality according to the people, so if it is said 'is rationality identical to humanity or other than it?' it is not correct, but rather it is neither identical to it nor other than it. And similarly, the part of any thing is neither identical to it nor other than it.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is the response of the Sifatiyya (those who affirm attributes) to the Mu'tazila and philosophers. They begin by reciprocating the denial: just as the Mu'tazila deny eternal attributes, the Sifatiyya deny the possibility of a described without attributes, and they consider this equally improbable. Then they analyze the division that the Mu'tazila presented (attributes are either identical to the essence or other). The Sifatiyya argue that this division is valid only if 'other' means something that can exist separately. They explain that the term 'other' (ghayr) can have two meanings: (1) merely that two things are distinct in concept while possibly inseparable, or (2) that they are separate existences such that one can exist without the other. The Mu'tazila, according to the Sifatiyya, assume the second meaning, but the Sifatiyya adopt the first. Thus, they do not concede that attributes are 'others' (aghyār) in the sense of separable existences, nor do they say they are identical. They then draw an analogy with the proponents of 'states' (aḥwāl), who assert that a state is neither existent nor non-existent. Similarly, the Sifatiyya can say that attributes are neither identical nor other. Finally, they provide a concrete example from the physical world: humanity is composed of animality and rationality. When asked whether rationality is identical to humanity or other than it, the correct answer is neither, because rationality is a constituent part and cannot be considered a separate 'other'. Similarly, attributes are parts of the divine essence considered conceptually, not separate existences. Thus, the Sifatiyya defend the doctrine that attributes are neither identical nor other, using the notion of non-separability and the analogy of composite realities.

Key Terms
صفاتية (Sifatiyya)
أغيار (others)
عين (identical)
غير (other)
حد الغيرين (definition of two others)
أحوال (states)
إنسانية (humanity)
حيوانية (animality)
ناطقية (rationality)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sifatiyya believe attributes are identical to the essence.
Correction: They reject both identity and otherness in the sense of separability.

Checkpoint
According to the Sifatiyya in this passage, why is the division of attributes into identical or other not straightforward?
A) Because attributes are neither existent nor non-existent.
B) Because 'other' can mean separable existence, which does not apply to attributes.
C) Because attributes are identical to the essence.
D) Because the Mu'tazila reject all attributes.

Key Takeaway
The Sifatiyya defend divine attributes as conceptually distinct but inseparable from the essence, neither identical nor other in the sense of separable existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say that God is Knowing and Powerful, do you secretly imagine these as separate 'things' attached to Him? How does this lesson challenge you to think of God's attributes as intrinsic yet distinct, and what does that demand from your understanding of tawhid?
?
Are you comfortable with the idea that some aspects of God's nature, like the relationship between attributes and essence, are beyond full human comprehension? Does this lesson push you to accept a level of mystery in your faith, and how should that affect your humility before God?
?
The Sifatiyya use a physical example (humanity) to explain divine attributes. Does this analogy help you grasp the concept, or do you worry it might lead to anthropomorphism? How can you hold onto the truth that God is unlike creation while still using such analogies for understanding?

Ibn Sina's View and a Classificatory Objection
Quotation of Ibn Sina and a proposal to classify intellects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's alternative classification of intellects proposed in response to Ibn Sina's definition.

Core Idea
An objector challenges Ibn Sina's view of intellect by offering a threefold classification.

This passage captures a critical exchange in debates about divine knowledge. Ibn Sina asserts that God knows things directly, not through external things. An objector then rejects a specific definition of intellect—as mere impression of one essence on another—and proposes either a redefinition or a classification that distinguishes among intellects that impress, are impressed, or neither. This objection tests the coherence of Ibn Sina's position.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's statement
Read the opening line where Ibn Sina says God knows things not from things, but things are from Him. Reflect on the contrast.
This is a classic position on divine knowledge.
2
Understand the objector's challenge
The objector says that if the definition of intellect as 'impression of an essence in an essence' is false, then two alternatives are proposed: either redefine intellect as 'form (rasm) of an essence in an essence', or classify intellects into three types.
The objector is forcing the argument.
3
Analyze the classification
Identify the three types: (1) intellect that is impressed, (2) intellect that impresses, (3) intellect that neither impresses nor is impressed. Note how this applies to God's intellect.
The passage cuts off, but the classification is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن سينا: إنه تعالى عالم بالأشياء لا من الأشياء، بل الأشياء منه ومن علمه. قيل له: فإذا بطل تقريرك الثاني أن العقل لا معنى له إلا الارتسام ماهية بماهية، فهلا قلت: العقل رسم ماهية في ماهية، أو هلا قسمت الأمر فقلت: من العقول ما يرتسم، ومن العقول ما يرسم ومنها ما لا يرسم ولا يرتسم، فعقله
Ibn Sina said: He, exalted, knows things not from things, but rather things are from Him and from His knowledge. It was said to him: So if your second account is false, that intellect has no meaning except the impression of an essence in an essence, why do you not say: intellect is the form (rasm) of an essence in an essence, or why did you not divide the matter and say: among intellects there is that which is impressed, and among intellects there is that which impresses, and among them there is that which neither impresses nor is impressed? His intellect...
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage presents a quotation from Ibn Sina that God knows things not from them, but things are from Him and His knowledge. Then an objector challenges this view: given that the definition of intellect as mere impression (irtisām) of one essence in another has been refuted (presumably earlier in the discussion), the objector suggests two alternatives: either redefine intellect as form (rasm) of one essence in another, or classify intellects into three types: those that receive impression (yartasim), those that give impression (yarsum), and those that neither give nor receive. The objector then begins to apply this classification to God's intellect, but the passage cuts off at 'His intellect' (fa-'aqlahu), leaving the argument incomplete. This is a classic kalam technique of proposing alternative categorizations to test a position.

Key Terms
ابن سينا
ارتسام
رسم
عقل
يرتسم
يرسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might assume that Ibn Sina's view is accepted without challenge in this passage.
Correction: The passage records an objection that scrutinizes Ibn Sina's position and proposes alternative definitions or classifications, showing it is not accepted uncritically.

Checkpoint
What classification of intellects does the objector propose?
A) Material and immaterial
B) Active and passive
C) Impressed, impressing, and neither
D) Necessary and possible

Key Takeaway
The objector challenges Ibn Sina's definition of intellect by offering a classification of impressed, impressing, and neither, leaving the question of God's intellect open.
