Refutation of Analogies with Jahm, Hisham, and Ash'ari on Speech
Rejects analogies with Jahm and Hisham, and criticizes appeal to Ash'ari's view on divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the opponent's analogies with Jahm, Hisham, and al-Ash'ari on divine speech are invalid and how al-Ash'ari's view actually contradicts the opponent.

Core Idea
Analogies with heretical views and al-Ash'ari's own stance undermine the opponent's position.

The opponent tries to strengthen their position by comparing it to the views of Jahm ibn Safwan, Hisham, and even al-Ash'ari on speech. But the author shows that these analogies do not work—addressing them actually refutes the opponent, and al-Ash'ari's own view contradicts the opponent's claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the two analogies the opponent uses.
Notice the concise style; each analogy is dismissed with a key reason.
2
Identify the first analogy
The opponent likens their view to that of Jahm ibn Safwan and Hisham. The author says this analogy is impossible and the same refutation applies.
Jahm and Hisham were known for heretical views; this comparison actually hurts the opponent.
3
Identify the second analogy
The opponent appeals to al-Ash'ari's doctrine on divine speech. The author argues this is inconsistent with al-Ash'ari's own principles.
4
Analyze al-Ash'ari's position
Al-Ash'ari held that God's speech is heard by humans (e.g., in the Quran) and will be seen by them. This directly contradicts the opponent's claim that divine attributes have no relation to creation.
Understanding this shows the opponent's analogy is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التمثل بمذهب جهم ابن صفوان وهشام فمثل محال والرد عليه كالرد عليكم، والتمثل بمذهب الأشعري في تكليم البشر غير مستقيم على أصله، فإن عنده كلام الله مسموع بسمع البشر، كما أنه مرئي برؤية البشر.
As for the analogy with the doctrine of Jahm ibn Safwan and Hisham, it is an impossible analogy, and the refutation of it is like the refutation of you. And the analogy with the doctrine of al-Ash'ari regarding the speech of humans is not consistent with his principle, for he holds that the speech of God is heard by the hearing of humans, just as it is seen by the sight of humans.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage deals with two attempted analogies by the opponent. First, the opponent might compare their view to that of Jahm ibn Safwan or Hisham. The author dismisses this, saying the analogy is impossible and the same arguments that refute those views also refute the opponent. Second, the opponent might appeal to al-Ash'ari's own view on divine speech. The author argues that this is not consistent with al-Ash'ari's principles, because al-Ash'ari held that God's speech is heard by humans (e.g., in the Quranic revelation) and will be seen by them in the afterlife. Therefore, al-Ash'ari's position does not support the opponent's view that divine attributes exist without any relation to creation. Rather, al-Ash'ari's view of speech is that it is a real attribute that can be perceived.

The Ash'ari Response to Mu'tazili Proof Texts: Will and Sins
The Ash'ari author lists Mu'tazili proof verses and gives the general response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key Ash'ari distinction that God's will and approval do not relate to sins insofar as they are sins, resolving the apparent conflict with Quranic verses.

Core Idea
God's will attaches to actions as creations, not as sins.

The Mu'tazila cite several Quranic verses to argue that God cannot will evil, implying that humans have independent power. The Ash'ari response does not deny the verses but refines our understanding of God's will. This passage presents the core Ash'ari answer: God's will, approval, and love do not attach to the sinful aspect of actions—only to the act as a created event. This distinction removes the dispute and paves the way for further interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the list of Quranic verses the Mu'tazila use as proof texts.
These verses appear to show God not willing or not liking certain things.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili argument
Understand that the Mu'tazila argue: if God does not will or approve of sin, then humans must have free will independent of God's will.
3
Grasp the Ash'ari response
The Ash'ari author says that God's will, approval, and love do not relate to sins 'insofar as they are sins'. This means God creates the act but does not will the disobedience aspect.
Key phrase: 'من حيث إنها معاص' (insofar as they are sins).
4
Understand the analogy with power
The author draws a parallel: just as God's power does not relate to actions as 'acquisitions' (kasb), so His will does not relate to sins as sins. This shows consistency in Ash'ari theology.
5
Reflect on the flexibility of interpretation
The author notes that if needed, each verse can be interpreted according to its wording and context. This shows that the Ash'ari approach is not rigid but allows for nuanced understanding.
This sets the stage for detailed tafsir of each verse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تمسكت المعتزلة بكلمات من ظواهر الكتاب ؛ منها قوله تعالى : ( وَلَا يَرْضَى لِعِبَادِهِ الْكُفْرَ ) [الزمر : 7]، وقوله : ( وَاللهُ يُرِيدُ أَن يَتُوبَ عَلَيْكُمْ وَيُرِيدُ الَّذِينَ يَتَّبِعُونَ الشَّهَوَاتِ أَن يَمِيلُوا مَيْلاً عَظِيمًا ) [النساء : 27]، وقوله : ( يُرِيدُ اللهُ بِكُمُ الْيُسْرَ وَلَا يُرِيدُ بِكُمُ الْعُسْرَ ) [البقرة : 185]، وقوله : ( تُرِيدُونَ عَرَضَ الدُّنْيَا وَاللَّهُ يُرِيدُ الآخِرَةَ ) [الأنفال : 67]، وقوله : ( وَمَا اللَّهُ يُرِيدُ ظُلْمًا لِلْعِبَادِ ) [غافر : 31]، وقوله : ( لا يُحِبُّ اللَّهُ الْجَهْرَ بِالسُّوءِ مِنَ الْقَوْلِ إِلَّا مَن ظُلِمَ ) [النساء : 148]، وقوله : ( سَيَقُولُ الَّذِينَ أَشْرَكُوا ) [الأنعام : 148]، وقوله : ( وَمَا خَلَقْتُ الْجِنَّ وَالْإِنسَ إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ ) [الذاريات : 56]، وأجابت الأشعرية بتأويلات وتخصيصات. وأحسن الأجوبة أن نقول : إرادة الله ومشيئته أو رضاه ومحبته لا تتعلق بالمعاصي قط من حيث إنها معاص، كما لا تتعلق قدرته تعالى بأفعال العباد من أكسابهم فيرتفع النزاع ويندفع التشنيع، وخرج ما قدرناه من التحقيق، وإن شئت حملت كل آية على معنى يشعر به اللفظ ويدل عليه الفحوى.
And the Mu'tazila have clung to words from the apparent meanings of the Book; among them is His saying: 'And He does not approve for His servants disbelief' (Q 39:7), and His saying: 'And Allah wants to turn in mercy to you, and those who follow lusts want you to deviate greatly' (Q 4:27), and His saying: 'Allah wants ease for you and does not want hardship for you' (Q 2:185), and His saying: 'You desire the fleeting world and Allah desires the Hereafter' (Q 8:67), and His saying: 'And Allah does not want injustice for the servants' (Q 40:31), and His saying: 'Allah does not love the public mention of evil except by one who has been wronged' (Q 4:148), and His saying: 'Those who associate partners will say' (Q 6:148), and His saying: 'And I did not create the jinn and mankind except to worship Me' (Q 51:56). And the Ash'ariyya answered with interpretations and specifications. And the best of the answers is that we say: The will of Allah and His volition, or His approval and His love, does not relate to sins at all insofar as they are sins, just as His power does not relate to the actions of the servants as their acquisitions, so the dispute is removed and the criticism is repelled, and what we have estimated from verification emerges, and if you wish, you may carry every verse upon a meaning that the wording suggests and the import indicates.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage lists eight Quranic verses that the Mu'tazila use as proof texts to argue that God does not will evil or sin, implying that human actions are independent. The Ash'ari author first notes that the Ash'aris respond with interpretations and specifications. He then gives what he considers the best answer: that God's will, volition, approval, and love do not relate to sins at all insofar as they are sins. This is a crucial distinction: God's will attaches to the action as a created act, but not as an act of disobedience. Similarly, God's power does not relate to the actions of servants as their acquisitions (kasb), but only as creations. This resolves the apparent contradiction between the verses and the Ash'ari doctrine that all things, including evil, occur by God's will. The author concludes that if one wishes, one can interpret each verse according to its wording and context. This sets the stage for the detailed interpretations that follow.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazili believe that if God does not will sin, then sin must occur independently of God's will, implying human free will and separate creative power.
Correction: The Ash'ari response clarifies that God's will does not attach to the sinful aspect of an action, only to the act as a created entity. Thus God creates the act but does not will the disobedience.

Key Takeaway

Three Schools on Divine Speech
Summary of Ash'ari, Karramiyya, and Mu'tazila views on divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contrasting views of the Ash'aris, Karramiyya, and Mu'tazila on the nature of God's speech.

Core Idea
God's speech: one eternal meaning, or created letters and sounds?

The nature of divine speech was a major point of contention among early theological schools. The Ash'aris, Karramiyya, and Mu'tazila each offered distinct explanations. This passage from al-Shahrastani's Nihayat al-Aqdam summarizes their core positions, setting the stage for the author's own argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the translation. Notice the parallel structure: each school is introduced with 'ذهبت'. Try to grasp the key claim of each.
The passage uses a comparative summary style.
2
Identify each school's position
For each school, write down one sentence that captures their view on God's speech. Focus on what speech is (one meaning, many meanings, letters/sounds) and whether it is eternal or created.
Pay attention to the Karramiyya distinction between power and utterance.
3
Compare and contrast
Compare the three positions. What is the main point of disagreement? Where do the Karramiyya agree with Ash'aris, and where with Mu'tazila? Also, note the internal difference between Abu Ali al-Jubba'i and his son.
The Ash'ari view is the most unified; Mu'tazila is the most material.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهبت الأشعرية إلى أن كلام الباري تعالى واحد وهو مع وحدته أمر ونهي وخبر واستخبار ووعد ووعيد، وذهبت الكرامية إلى أن الكلام بمعنى القدرة على القول معنى واحد وبمعنى القول معان كثيرة قائمة بذات الباري تعالى، وهي أقوال مسموعة وكلمات محفوظة تحدث في ذاته عند قوله وتكلمه ولا يجوز عليها الفناء ولا العدم عندهم، وذهبت المعتزلة إلى أن الكلام حروف منظومة وأصوات مقطعة شاهداً وغائبًا لا حقيقة للكلام سوى ذلك، وهي مخلوقة قائمة بمحل حادث إذا أوجدها الباري تعالى سمعت من المحل، وكما وجدت فنيت، وشرط أبو علي الجبائي البنية المخصوصة التي يتأتى منها مخارج الحروف شاهدا وغائبا، ولم يشترط ذلك ابنه أبو هاشم في الغائب.
The Ash'aris held that the speech of the Exalted Creator is one, and it is, despite its oneness, command, prohibition, report, inquiry, promise, and threat. The Karramiyya held that speech in the sense of power over utterance is one meaning, and in the sense of utterance is many meanings subsisting in the essence of the Exalted Creator, and they are audible words and preserved expressions that occur in His essence when He speaks and says, and they do not admit of annihilation or non-existence according to them. The Mu'tazila held that speech is articulated letters and segmented sounds, both in the witnessed and the absent, and there is no reality of speech other than that, and they are created, subsisting in an originated locus, such that when the Exalted Creator brings them into existence they are heard from the locus, and as soon as they exist they perish. Abu Ali al-Jubba'i stipulated the specific structure from which the points of articulation of letters arise, both in the witnessed and the absent, but his son Abu Hashim did not stipulate that in the absent.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the three main theological schools' positions on the nature of divine speech. The author states the Ash'ari position first: God's speech is a single eternal meaning that encompasses all types of speech (commands, prohibitions, reports, etc.). Then the Karramiyya position: they distinguish between speech as power (one meaning) and speech as utterance (many meanings subsisting in God's essence, but they are audible words that occur in His essence when He speaks, and they do not perish, implying they are not eternal but also not passing away). Finally the Mu'tazila position: speech is nothing but letters and sounds, created in a locus, and they perish immediately after being heard. Within the Mu'tazila, Abu Ali al-Jubba'i required a specific physical structure (like a larynx) for the production of letters even in the absent (i.e., God), but his son Abu Hashim did not require that in the absent. This establishes the doctrinal landscape for the subsequent argument.

Key Terms
كلام
أشعرية
كرامية
معتزلة
باري
أمر ونهي
قدرة على القول
حروف منظومة
أصوات مقطعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari view denies that God's speech includes commands and prohibitions, or that it is a single meaning only in name.
Correction: The Ash'aris affirm that despite its oneness, God's speech encompasses all types (command, prohibition, etc.) because it is a single attribute that is the source of these effects.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, what is the nature of God's speech?
A) A single eternal meaning
B) Audible words that subsist in God's essence
C) Articulated letters and segmented sounds that are created and perish
D) A power over utterance

Key Takeaway
The nature of divine speech was debated: Ash'aris saw it as one eternal meaning, Karramiyya as both power and multiple utterances, and Mu'tazila as created letters and sounds.

?
The Mu'tazila claimed God's speech is created and perishes. Have you ever treated the Quran as if it were just a temporary tool? What would it cost you to now treat it as an eternal attribute of God?

The Mu'tazila Objection: Contradictory Attributes Cannot Inhere in One Speech
Presents Mu'tazila argument that command and prohibition cannot inhere in one speech due to contradictory properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that command and prohibition, being contradictory with distinct properties, cannot both inhere in one speech, and how they distinguish between relational and essential attributes.

Core Idea
Command and prohibition cannot coexist in one speech because they have mutually exclusive properties.

The Mu'tazila raise a nuanced objection against the unity of divine speech. They acknowledge that essential attributes can inhere in a single essence, but they argue that contradictory properties—like command and prohibition—are fundamentally incompatible. This lesson presents their reasoning and clarifies their distinction between relational and intrinsic attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objection
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice that they begin by conceding the possibility of essential attributes in a single essence, then state their objection.
2
Identify the key distinction
The Mu'tazila distinguish between differences of names (due to aspects) and differences of mutually exclusive properties. Understand this distinction.
They accept the former but reject the latter.
3
Focus on the argument about command and prohibition
They claim command and prohibition are not relational attributes because relational attributes change with relation, while command and prohibition are intrinsic descriptions of speech.
The passage cuts off, but the argument is clear.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Consider how this objection challenges the Ash'ari view of the unity of God's speech. What would be the response?
This sets up the next lesson on the Ash'ari rebuttal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة : إثبات صفات نفسية لذات واحدة غير مستحيل، لكن إثبات خواص مختلفة وصفات متضادة لشيء واحد هو المستحيل، ومن المعلوم أن الأمر والنهي يتضادان ولهما خاصيتان مختلفتان فإثبات ذلك لكلام واحد محال، ونحن لا تنكر اختلاف الأسامي لشيء واحد لاختلاف الوجوه والاعتبارات، لكنا ننكر اختلاف الخواص المتباينة لشيء واحد، ولا نشك أن كون الكلام أمرًا ونهيًا ليس من جملة النسب والإضافات فإن الصفات الإضافية تتحقق عند الإضافة ولا يتحقق عند رفع الإضافة، ويتطرق إليها التبدل والتغير، وليس كون الكلام أمرًا ونهيًا مما يتحقق عند الإضافة، بل هما من أخص أوصاف الكلام، سواء لاحظنا
The Mu'tazila said: Positing essential attributes for a single essence is not impossible, but positing different properties and contradictory attributes for one thing is impossible. And it is known that command and prohibition are contradictory and have two different properties, so positing them for one speech is impossible. And we do not deny the difference of names for one thing due to different aspects and considerations, but we deny the difference of mutually exclusive properties for one thing. And we have no doubt that speech being command and prohibition is not among the relations and additions, because relational attributes are realized upon relation and are not realized when relation is removed, and they are subject to change and alteration, and speech being command and prohibition is not something that is realized upon relation; rather, they are among the most specific descriptions of speech, whether we consider
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila objection against the unity of divine speech. They accept that essential attributes (sifat nafsiyya) can inhere in a single essence without impossibility. However, they argue that positing different properties (khawass) and contradictory attributes (sifat mutadadda) for one thing is impossible. Since command and prohibition are known to be contradictory and have distinct properties, they cannot both inhere in one speech. The Mu'tazila then clarify that they do not reject the difference of names for one thing due to different aspects and considerations—they accept that. What they reject is the difference of mutually exclusive properties for one thing. They further argue that command and prohibition are not relational attributes (nisab wa idafat) because relational attributes are only realized upon relation and cease when the relation is removed, and they are subject to change. In contrast, command and prohibition are among the most specific descriptions (akhass awsaf) of speech, regardless of whether we consider a relation or not. The argument is cut off at 'whether we consider' (sawa la-hazna), indicating the next page would continue the reasoning.

Key Terms
معتزلة
صفات نفسية
خواص
صفات متضادة
أمر
نهي
نسب وإضافات
أوصاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila deny that any attributes can inhere in a single essence.
Correction: They actually affirm essential attributes (sifat nafsiyya) but argue that contradictory properties like command and prohibition cannot coexist in one speech.

Checkpoint
Why do the Mu'tazila claim that command and prohibition cannot inhere in one speech?
A) Because they are relational attributes
B) Because they are mutually exclusive and have different properties
C) Because speech is not an attribute of God
D) Because they are not essential attributes

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila object to the unity of divine speech by arguing that command and prohibition are mutually exclusive properties that cannot inhere in one speech, unlike relational or merely nominal differences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God's commands and prohibitions in your life contradict each other? How does knowing that they proceed from one unified speech challenge you to trust His wisdom over your own understanding?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that contradictory properties cannot inhere in one being. Examine your own character: are there areas where you hold contradictory beliefs or actions? What would it mean for you to seek the same coherence in your faith that the debate here demands of divine speech?
?
The Mu'tazila accepted essential attributes but rejected contradictory ones. Reflect on your own perception of God's attributes: do you struggle to reconcile His mercy and justice? How does this lesson's distinction between essential and contradictory attributes help you approach that tension?

The Mu'tazilite Objection to Eternal Command
Mu'tazila object that eternal command requires a commanded being in eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that an eternal command is logically impossible because it would require addressing a non-existent being.

Core Idea
Eternal command cannot address a non-existent being.

The Mu'tazila challenge the Ash'ari doctrine of eternal speech by posing a dilemma: if God's speech is eternal and includes commands, then those commands must have been issued in eternity when no created beings existed. How can a command be given to nothing? This passage presents their first argument against the coherence of eternal command.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then the English translation. Notice the dilemma structure and the clear claim that a command requires a commanded being.
The text cuts off after the first aspect of absurdity.
2
Identify the dilemma
The Mu'tazila present a dilemma: either you judge that God's eternal speech includes command, prohibition, etc., or you do not. If you do, they claim absurdities follow. Find this in the passage.
3
Examine the first absurdity
The first absurdity is that a command requires a commanded being (مأمور) to exist. In eternity, no created beings exist, so there is no addressee. A non-existent being cannot be commanded.
This is a logical objection based on the relational nature of command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة: إذا أثبتم كلاماً أزلياً فإما أن تحكموا بأن كلام الله أمر ونهي وخبر واستخبار في الأزل، وإما أن لا تحكموا به. فإن حكمتم به فقد أحلتم من وجوه أحدها: أن من حكم الأمر أن يصادف مأموراً أولم يكن في الأزل مخاطب لأن يحث على أمر ويزجر عن آخر ويستحيل كون المعدوم متعرض مأموراً.
The Mu'tazila said: If you affirm eternal speech, then either you judge that the speech of God is command, prohibition, information, and interrogation in eternity, or you do not judge that. If you judge that, then you have fallen into impossibilities from several aspects, one of which is: that the ruling of command is that it encounters a commanded being; if not, then in eternity there was no addressee, because it urges to a thing and deters from another, and it is impossible for a non-existent being to be a commanded being subject to address.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents the beginning of a Mu'tazilite objection against the Ash'ari doctrine of eternal speech. The Mu'tazila present a dilemma: either the Ash'ari holds that God's eternal speech includes command (أمر), prohibition (نهي), information (خبر), and interrogation (استخبار) from eternity, or he does not. If he does, then they claim he falls into absurdities. The first absurdity is that a command requires a commanded being (مأمور) to exist at the time of the command. Since in eternity no created beings exist, there is no addressee. A command is supposed to urge one to do something and deter from something else, but a non-existent being (المعدوم) cannot be addressed or commanded. This is a classic Mu'tazilite argument: an eternal command to a nonexistent being is impossible. The objection is incomplete in the text; only the first of several 'aspects' (وجوه) is given. The author's response is not included in this page window, so the lesson stands as a presentation of the objection only.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الكلام الأزلي
الأمر
النهي
الخبر
الاستخبار
المأمور
المعدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the objection is about God's knowledge of future actions rather than the logical requirement of a recipient for a command.
Correction: The Mu'tazila specifically argue that a command is a relational act requiring a recipient, and since no recipient existed in eternity, an eternal command is impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazilite objection against eternal speech being command and prohibition in eternity?
A) There is no such thing as eternal speech.
B) A command requires a commanded being in existence, which did not exist in eternity.
C) God cannot speak until He creates speech.
D) The Ash'ari claim that speech is eternal is self-contradictory.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that an eternal command is impossible because a command requires a commanded being, and there were no created beings in eternity to receive it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just studied a logical argument that eternal command is impossible because there is no commanded being in eternity. How does this affect your understanding of God's commands to you? Does it make you feel that your obedience is part of a timeless plan, or does it raise doubts about how God relates to time? What step can you take today to align your actions with the commands that were always meant for you?
?
The Mu'tazila claim that addressing a non-existent being is absurd. When you consider your own life, have you ever felt 'non-existent' in your response to God's commands? What specific command have you been neglecting because you felt distant from God? Commit to one concrete act of obedience this week to become a true 'addressee' of God's command.
?
This passage shows a deep theological debate about whether God's speech can be eternal and still command temporal creatures. How does this intellectual challenge affect your personal reliance on the Qur'an as a guide? Do you find comfort in the belief that God's commands are eternal, or does it create pressure to live up to them? What is one area of your life where you need to take God's command more seriously right now?

The Mu'tazila-Ash'ari Debate on the Nature of the Quran
Presents Mu'tazila argument from consensus and Ash'ariyya response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key arguments from both the Mu'tazila and Ash'ariyya regarding whether the Quran is created or eternal, and how each side claims consensus.

Core Idea
The debate hinges on whether sensory attributes of the Quran imply its creation.

This passage presents a classic theological debate. The Mu'tazila argue from the Quran's composition and sensory accessibility that it must be created, while the Ash'ariyya respond that such a view strips the Quran of its divine status. Let's examine their arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two clear sections: the Mu'tazila argument followed by the Ash'ariyya response.
2
Identify Mu'tazila arguments
List the points the Mu'tazila make: consensus on composition, etymology of 'Qur'an', sensory accessibility, and usage of the word 'Rab' in prayers.
They use both a priori and scriptural evidence.
3
Understand the Ash'ariyya counter
The Ash'ariyya accuse the Mu'tazila of breaking consensus and argue that their view makes the Quran a human action, not God's eternal speech.
Contrast the two views on the reality of the recited Quran.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider how each position affects the doctrine of God's attributes and the status of the Quran as a miracle.
This connects to broader issues in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة : أجمع المسلمون قبل ظهور هذا الخلاف على أن القرآن كلام واتفقوا على أنه سور وآيات وحروف منتظمة وكلمات مجموعة، وهي مقروءة مسموعة على التحقيق، ولها مفتتح ومختتم، وأنه معجزة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم دالة على صدقه، ولا تكون المعجزات إلا فعلا خارقا للعادة، وكان السلف يقولون يا رب القرآن العظيم، يا رب طه يا رب يس، وإنما القرآن قرآنًا للجمع من قولهم : قرأت الناقة لبنها في ضرعها، والجمع إنما يتحقق في المفترق والكلام الأزلي لا يوصف بمثل هذه الأوصاف، ومما أجمعت عليه الأمة أن كلام الله تعالى بين أظهرنا نقرأه بألسنتنا ونمسه بأيدينا، ونبصره بأعيننا، ونسمعه بآذاننا، وعليه دلت النصوص : ﴿وَإِنْ أَحَدٌ مِّنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ اسْتَجَارَكَ فَأَجِرْهُ حَتَّى يَسْمَعَ كَلَامَ اللَّهِ﴾ [التوبة: 6] ﴿لا يَمَسُّهُ إِلَّا الْمُطَهَّرُونَ﴾ [الواقعة: 79] والصفة الأزلية كيف توصف بما وصفنا؟ قالت الأشعرية : أنتم أول من خالف هذا الإجماع، فإن عندكم كلام الله تبارك وتعالى حروف وكلمات أحدثها في محل، وكما وجدت فنيت، والذي كتبناه بأيدينا فعلنا، والذي قرأناه بألسنتنا كسبنا وكذلك نثاب على فعل ذلك ونعاقب على تركه فليس الكلام الذي بين أظهرنا كلام الله وما كان كلاما الله فليس بين أظهرنا ولا كان دليلا ومعجزة ولا قرأناه ولا سمعناه، بل الذي نقرأه مثل ذلك.
The Mu'tazila said: The Muslims agreed before the emergence of this disagreement that the Qur'an is speech, and they agreed that it consists of surahs, verses, regular letters, and collected words, and it is read and heard in reality, and it has an opening and a conclusion, and it is a miracle of the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, indicating his truthfulness, and miracles are only acts that break the customary course of events. The predecessors used to say: 'O Lord of the Great Quran, O Lord of Taha, O Lord of Ya Sin.' The word 'Qur'an' derives from 'collection' from their saying: 'The she-camel collected her milk in her udder,' and collection is only realized in what is separate. Eternal speech cannot be described with such descriptions. Among what the community agreed upon is that the speech of God Almighty is among us, we read it with our tongues, touch it with our hands, see it with our eyes, and hear it with our ears. And the texts indicate this: {And if any one of the polytheists seeks your protection, then grant him protection so that he may hear the words of Allah} [At-Tawbah: 6], {None touch it except the purified} [Al-Waqi'ah: 79]. How can an eternal attribute be described as we have described? The Ash'ariyya said: You are the first to contradict this consensus, for according to you, the speech of God, blessed and exalted, consists of letters and words that He created in a locus, and as soon as they came into existence perished. And what we have written with our hands is our act, and what we have read with our tongues is our acquisition, and we are rewarded for doing that and punished for leaving it. So the speech that is among us is not the speech of God, and what is the speech of God is not among us, nor was it a proof or a miracle, nor did we read it or hear it. Rather, what we read is like that.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila argument and the Ash'ariyya response concerning the nature of the Quran. The Mu'tazila begin by claiming consensus that the Quran is speech (kalam), consisting of organized surahs, verses, letters, and words, and that it is read and heard, has a beginning and end, and is a miracle. They support this by noting that the predecessors addressed the Quran as 'O Lord of the Quran,' implying it is something created and addressed. Etymologically, 'Qur'an' means 'collection' (of things separate), and an eternal attribute cannot be described as composed or collected. They cite the consensus that God's speech is among us, accessible to our senses, and the Qur'anic verses that refer to 'hearing' and 'touching' God's speech, asking how an eternal attribute could have such characteristics. The Ash'ariyya respond by accusing the Mu'tazila of being the first to contradict this consensus. They argue that the Mu'tazila posit God's speech as created letters and words that come into existence and then perish. On this view, what we write and recite is our own action and acquisition, not God's eternal speech. Consequently, the speech among us is not God's speech, and God's speech is not among us, nor is it a miracle that we read or hear. Thus, the Mu'tazilite position, by making the Quran created and transient, removes it from being an ongoing divine attribute and miracle.

Key Terms
معتزلة
أشعرية
كلام
قرآن
معجزة
إجماع
حروف
كلمات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that both parties agreed on the reality of the Quran but differed only in wording; in fact, they differed on whether the Quran is created or eternal, which has major theological implications.
Correction: The Mu'tazila argue the Quran is created because it has sensory attributes; the Ash'ariyya argue it is uncreated and that the recited words are not identical to God's eternal speech.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of contention between the Mu'tazila and Ash'ariyya in this passage?
A) Whether the Quran is a miracle.
B) Whether the Quran is eternal or created.
C) Whether the Quran has surahs and verses.
D) Whether the Quran can be read and heard.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila and Ash'ariyya differ on whether the Quran's sensory attributes indicate creation or not, with each side claiming consensus to support their position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran with your tongue and hear it with your ears, do you truly feel you are in the presence of God's eternal speech, or does it feel like a created object from the past? What does your answer reveal about the place the Quran holds in your daily life?
?
The Mu‘tazila argued from a consensus they believed was universal, but the Ash‘ari countered that consensus was broken. Are you willing to examine a deeply held belief—something you think everyone agrees on—and ask yourself whether it is the truth or just a comfortable tradition? What would it cost you to change?

An-Nazzam's View on Speech as a Subtle Body
Presents and critiques an-Nazzam's theory that speech is a subtle body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an-Nazzam's theory that speech is a subtle body, how he described its transmission, and why it was criticized as confused.

Core Idea
An-Nazzam's speech-as-body theory collapses into confusion and borrowed noise.

This lesson examines a historical view on the nature of human speech proposed by an-Nazzam. He attempted to explain speech as a physical, subtle body that travels from speaker to hearer. The passage presents his mechanistic description and then highlights his confusion about whether the form is singular or multiple, before delivering a sharp critique of his philosophical borrowing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to get the full picture of an-Nazzam's theory and its critique.
2
Identify an-Nazzam's definition
Note that he defines speech as a subtle body that emanates, strikes the air, and eventually reaches the intellect.
This is a physicalist account of speech.
3
Spot the confusion
Find where an-Nazzam becomes uncertain about whether the form in the air is one or many.
This confusion undermines his theory.
4
Understand the critique
The author dismisses an-Nazzam's view as mere noise borrowed from philosophers without proper understanding.
The critique is based on lack of clarity and derivation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهب النظام إلى أن الكلام جسم لطيف منبعث من المتكلم ويقرع أجزاء الهوى فيتموج الهوى بحركته ويتشكل بشكله، ثم يقرع العصب المفروش في الأذن فيتشكل العصب بشكله ، ثم يصل إلى الخيال فيعرض على الفكر العقلي فيفهم وربما يقول : الكلام حركة في جسم لطيف على شكل مخصوص، ثم تحير في أن الشكل إذا حدث في الهوى أهو شكل واحد يسمعه السامعون أم أشكال كثيرة؟ وإنما أخذ مذهبه في هذه المسألة وغيرها من المسائل من مذهب الفلاسفة غير أنه لم يفهم من كلامهم إلا ضجيجا ولم يورده نضيحًا.
An-Nazzam held that speech is a subtle body emanating from the speaker, striking the parts of the air, so the air ripples with its motion and takes shape according to its form; then it strikes the nerve spread in the ear, so the nerve takes shape according to its form; then it reaches the imagination and is presented to the rational intellect and is understood. And perhaps he says: speech is a motion in a subtle body with a specific form. Then he became confused: when the form occurs in the air, is it a single form heard by all hearers or many forms? He took his doctrine in this question and other questions from the doctrine of the philosophers, but he did not understand from their speech except noise and did not present it clearly.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents the view of an-Nazzam on the nature of human speech. According to him, speech is a subtle body (jism laṭīf) that emanates from the speaker and strikes the air, causing the air to move and take on the shape of that body. It then strikes the auditory nerve in the ear, which similarly takes shape, and finally reaches the imagination and is presented to the intellect for understanding. An-Nazzam also ventures that speech is a motion in a subtle body with a specific form. However, he became confused about whether the form that occurs in the air is a single form heard by all listeners or multiple distinct forms. The author critiques an-Nazzam by noting that he borrowed his doctrine from the philosophers but did not properly understand their speech—he only grasped noise ('ḍajīj') and did not present it clearly ('naḍīḥan'). The passage thus serves both to report a historical view and to dismiss it as confused and derivative.

Key Terms

جسم لطيف
هوى
خيال
فكر عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That speech is a physical object or substance.
Correction: The passage shows that an-Nazzam's theory fails due to confusion and lack of clarity, and the author implies speech is not a physical body.

Checkpoint
What did an-Nazzam consider speech to be?
A) An accidental attribute of the soul.
B) A subtle body emanating from the speaker.
C) An eternal divine attribute.
D) A mere convention without reality.

Key Takeaway
An-Nazzam's theory that speech is a subtle body is rejected as a confused borrowing from philosophy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
An-Nazzam's confusion led him to a theory that obscured the truth. Is there any aspect of your faith where you are content with confusion rather than seeking clarity? What step will you take today to remove that confusion?

Refuting Mu'tazila: Internal Speech from Their Own Premises
Al-Shahrastani refutes Mu'tazila by using their own concept of two inner thoughts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Shahrastani refutes the Mu'tazila denial of internal speech by demonstrating that their own concept of inner impulses and the process of reasoning both presuppose internal speech.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila admit inner impulses, which are a form of internal speech, so they cannot deny it.

Al-Shahrastani presents a clever refutation of the Mu'tazila school, which denied that God's speech is internal (kalam nafsi). He points out that their own theory of two inner impulses (khāṭirān) – one calling to good, one to evil – is itself a form of internal speech. Then he shows that even ordinary human reasoning involves internal speech. This passage uses their own premises against them, making the refutation particularly powerful.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
Notice the two distinct arguments in the passage.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage contains two arguments: (1) the Mu'tazila's own doctrine of two inner impulses shows they accept internal speech; (2) the process of reasoning itself involves internal speech.
3
Understand the first argument
Al-Shahrastani cites the Mu'tazilite doctrine that a person finds two impulses (khāṭirān) before revelation. These impulses are created and inform about 'speech of the soul' (kalām al-nafs). Thus, the Mu'tazila implicitly affirm internal speech.
This is a tuqlīd (follow-through) argument: using their own premises.
4
Understand the second argument
During reasoning, a person mentally makes and cancels statements, divides universals, and invalidates parts. This mental division is internal speech. One could fill a whole book from inner talk while the tongue is silent.
This shows internal speech is unavoidable in human cognition.
5
Reflect on the overall refutation
Ask yourself why this refutation is effective. It does not rely on scripture but on rational observation and the opponent's own views.
Consider how this connects to the broader debate on God's eternal speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن مذاهب المعتزلة أن المفكر قبل ورود السمع يجد من نفسه خاطرين؛ أحدهما يدعوه إلى معرفة الصانع وشكر المنعم وسلوك سبيل المحاسن والخيرات، والثاني يدعوه إلى خلاف ذلك ثم يختار ما فيه النجاة، ويجتنب عما فيه الهلاك، وذلك الخاطران الداعيان المحدثان المخبران عن كلام النفس وحديثها، فكيف يسوع لهم إنكار ذلك؟ وأيضًا فإن الإنسان في مراتب نظره واستدلاله يضع قولا ويرفع قولا ويقسم كليا ويبطل أحد القسمين فيتعين له الثاني، فتقسيمه هو بعينه حديث النفس، أحد القسمين هو بعينه حكم العقل، وربما أخذ القلم وكتب مجلدة من حديث فكره، وشحن ديوانًا من حديث نفسه ولسانه ساكت لا ينطق. وتعينه
And among the doctrines of the Mu'tazila is that the thinker, before the arrival of revelation, finds in himself two impulses: one calling him to knowledge of the Maker, gratitude to the Benefactor, and following the path of good deeds and virtues, and the other calling him to the opposite of that; then he chooses what contains salvation and avoids what contains destruction. Those two calling, created impulses inform about speech of the soul and its inner talk. So how can it be permissible for them to deny that? Also, a person in the stages of his consideration and inference makes a statement and cancels a statement, divides a universal and invalidates one of the two divisions, so the second becomes determined for him. His division is precisely internal speech, and the determination of one of the two divisions is precisely the judgment of reason. Perhaps he takes the pen and writes a volume from the talk of his thought, and fills a book from the talk of his soul while his tongue is silent, not speaking. And its determination.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila who deny internal speech. The author presents their own doctrine: before revelation, a person finds two inner impulses (khāṭirān) — one urging good, the other evil — and then chooses salvation. These impulses are themselves ‘speech of the soul’ (kalām al-nafs). Therefore, the Mu'tazila implicitly affirm internal speech, contradicting their denial. A further argument is given: during reasoning, a person mentally makes and cancels statements, divides a universal, invalidates one part, and determines the other. This mental division is internal speech. One could even write an entire book from this inner talk while the tongue is silent. The phrase ‘and its determination’ (wa taʿyīnuhu) likely refers to the determination of the correct division being the judgment of reason.

Key Terms
خاطران
مذهب المعتزلة
حديث النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila completely reject any inner mental content or speech.
Correction: The passage shows they admit inner impulses (khāṭirān), which are a form of internal speech (kalām al-nafs), so their denial of internal speech is inconsistent.

Checkpoint
How does the passage use the Mu'tazilite doctrine of two inner impulses (khāṭirān) against them?
A) It shows that those impulses are the same as the two parts of the soul.
B) It argues that the impulses themselves are a type of internal speech, so they cannot deny it.
C) It proves that the impulses lead to salvation or destruction.
D) It claims the impulses are created by God, not by the person.

Key Takeaway
By highlighting the Mu'tazila's own acceptance of inner impulses, Al-Shahrastani demonstrates that internal speech is undeniable even to its deniers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a moral decision, do you pay attention to the inner impulses that call you to good? Or do you ignore or rationalize them? What does your response to those impulses reveal about your true priorities?
?
The Mu'tazila acknowledged inner impulses but denied the reality of internal speech. Are there beliefs you hold in practice but deny in theory? How might this inconsistency affect your integrity before Allah?
?
Your own reasoning process—making statements, canceling them, dividing possibilities—is internal speech. When you engage in self-talk, do you recognize that you are 'speaking' to yourself? How does this awareness change the way you listen to your own thoughts?

The Mu'tazila Denial of Internal Speech
Mu'tazila deny internal speech as real speech, argue it is estimation of verbal expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that internal speech is not real speech but dependent on verbal expressions, and how they appeal to common usage.

Core Idea
Internal speech is just an echo of learned language, not real speech itself.

The Mu'tazila present a detailed objection against the view that internal speech is the real speech of Allah. They concede that thoughts occur but argue they are merely estimations based on previously learned expressions. Their argument uses empirical observations about language dependency, culminating in the example of a mute person. The author then adds support from common linguistic custom.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how they begin by conceding thoughts but then redefining them as estimations.
Focus on the flow from concession to argument.
2
Analyze their empirical proof
They give three examples: a person who doesn't know a language cannot think in it; a bilingual thinks in both; a mute devoid of expressions has no inner speech. Consider how each supports their claim.
This is the core of their reasoning.
3
Understand their conclusion
They conclude that real speech is only structured letters on the tongue. Note how they redefine 'mutakallim' as someone able to speak.
4
Reflect on the author's endorsement
The author adds that this is the common understanding of language and reason. This reinforces the Mu'tazila position here.
This is the author's own stance, not just reporting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة : نحن لا ننكر الخواطر التي تطرأ على قلب الإنسان، وربما نسميها أحاديث النفس ؛ إما مجازاً وإما حقيقة غير أنها تقديرات للعبارات التي في اللسان، ألا ترى أن من لا يعرف كلمة بالعربية لا يخطر بباله كلام العرب، ومن لا يعرف العجمية لا يطرأ عليه كلام العجم، ومن عرف اللسانين تارة تحدث نفسه بلسان العرب، وتارة بلسان العجم، فعلم على الحقيقة أنها تقديرات وأحاديث تابعة للعبارات التي تعلمها الإنسان في أول نشوئه والعبارات هي أصول لها منها تصدر وعليها ترد، حتى لو قدرنا إنساناً خالياً عن العبارات كلها أبكم لا يقدر على نطق لم نشك أن نفسه لا تحدثه بعربية ولا عجمية ولا لسان من الألسن، وعقله يعقل كل معقول وإن كان يعرى عن كل مسموع ومنقول فعلم أن الكلام الحقيقي هو الحروف المنظومة التي في اللسان. والمتعارف من أهل اللغة والعقلاء أن الذي في اللسان هو الكلام ومن قدر عليه فهو المتكلم ومن لم يقدر عليه فهو الأعجم الأبكم.
The Mu'tazila said: We do not deny the thoughts that occur to a person's heart, and we sometimes call them 'inner speech' (aḥādīth al-nafs), either metaphorically or literally, but they are estimations of the expressions (al-ʿibārāt) that are on the tongue. Do you not see that one who does not know a word in Arabic does not think of Arabic speech, and one who does not know Persian does not conceive of Persian speech, and one who knows both languages sometimes speaks to himself in Arabic and sometimes in Persian? So it is known in reality that they are estimations and inner discourses dependent on the expressions that a person learned in his early development, and expressions are their origins from which they issue and to which they recur. Even if we imagine a person completely devoid of all expressions, a mute unable to speak, we do not doubt that his soul would not speak to him in Arabic nor Persian nor any language, while his mind comprehends every intelligible even if it is stripped of everything audible and transmitted. Thus it is known that real speech (al-kalām al-ḥaqīqī) is the structured letters that are on the tongue. And the customary understanding among the people of language and the intellect is that what is on the tongue is speech, and whoever is able to do it is a speaker (mutakallim), and whoever is not able is a mute non-Arab (aʿjam abkam).
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila objection against the view that internal speech (al-kalām al-nafsī) is real speech, followed by the author's reinforcement of their conclusion by appealing to common linguistic custom. The Mu'tazila begin by conceding that they do not deny the occurrence of thoughts and inner dialogues, which they sometimes call 'inner speech' either metaphorically or literally. However, they insist that these are merely estimations (taqdīrāt) of the verbal expressions that exist on the tongue. To prove this, they offer an empirical argument: a person who does not know Arabic does not think in Arabic, and likewise for Persian; a bilingual person thinks in both languages. This shows that inner speech is dependent on previously learned expressions. The strongest evidence, they say, is a mute person who has never learned any language: such a person's soul does not produce internal speech in any language, yet he can still reason. Therefore, real speech is nothing other than the articulated letters (al-ḥurūf al-manẓūma) spoken on the tongue. After this, the author adds his own support: the common understanding of linguists and rational people is that speech is what is uttered by the tongue, and that one who can utter is called a speaker, while one who cannot is called mute. Thus, the Mu'tazila position that speech is only spoken words is affirmed.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
أحاديث النفس
تقديرات
عبارات
الكلام الحقيقي
حروف منظومة
اللسان
الأبكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Mu'tazila deny any internal thoughts altogether.
Correction: The Mu'tazila explicitly affirm thoughts and inner dialogues; they only deny that these are real speech in the strict sense, considering them estimations of verbal expressions.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila in this passage, what is the basis for internal speech?
A) It arises from the intellect independently of language
B) It consists of estimations dependent on previously learned expressions
C) It is a divine inspiration
D) It is the same for all humans regardless of language

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that internal speech is merely an estimation of spoken expressions, not real speech, and they support this with empirical evidence about language dependency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often have inner conversations with yourself. According to this passage, your inner speech is merely an echo of language you've learned. When you say 'I spoke to myself,' are you confidently asserting that this is real speech? The Mu'tazila say it's only estimation. Does that realization humble you, showing that even your most private thoughts are dependent on what you've been taught? How will you ensure that the 'expressions' stored in your mind are rooted in truth, not falsehood?
?
Imagine if you were born mute, never learning a language. This passage says you could still reason, but your soul would not speak in any language. Your worship, your supplications—how much of them depend on words? If you were stripped of all language, would your heart still communicate with Allah? What does that reveal about the essence of your connection with Him?
?
The Mu'tazila use this argument to deny that God has internal speech as an attribute. But consider your own relationship with the Qur'an: you hear it with your ears, recite it with your tongue. Is it possible that you treat the Qur'an as mere spoken letters, forgetting that it originates from an eternal internal Speech of Allah? How will you connect more deeply with the fact that every Arabic letter you recite is a manifestation of something far greater?

The Ash'ari Debt to Philosophy
The author accuses the Ash'aris of following the philosophers in their definition of speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author traces the Ash'ari definition of speech as internal utterance back to Aristotelian logic, and the theological implications of this borrowing.

Core Idea
Ash'ari internal speech is not a purely Islamic idea—it's borrowed from Greek philosophy.

The author of the Niḥāyat al-Aqdām does not merely disagree with the Ash'ari definition of speech; he charges them with adopting the philosophical framework of Aristotle. This lesson unpacks that critique, showing the logical chain that leads from defining speech as internal utterance to an anthropology that reduces the body to a mere instrument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the accusatory tone: 'And you, O assembly of Ash'aris'.
Pay attention to the technical term 'al-nuṭq al-nafsī'.
2
Identify the parallel being drawn
The author compares the Ash'ari definition of speech to the philosophers' definition of man. Find that parallel in the text. What are the two definitions being compared?
The Ash'aris defined speech as internal utterance; the philosophers defined man as rational animal.
3
Understand the logical consequence
The author claims that if the Ash'aris follow the philosophers' method, then the rational soul becomes the true human being and the body is just a tool. This is a reductio ad absurdum. Consider why this is problematic from an Islamic perspective.
This consequence undermines the unity of body and soul in traditional Islamic anthropology.
4
Reflect on the author's purpose
Why does the author mention this? He is not just criticizing; he is showing that the Ash'ari position leads to unacceptable philosophical conclusions. Ask yourself: what does this say about the Ash'ari definition of divine speech?
The author likely wants to reject internal speech altogether.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنتم يا معشر الأشاعرة انتهجتم مناهج الفلاسفة حيث حددتم الكلام بالنطق النفسي، كما حدوا الإنسان بقولهم: الحيوان الناطق، وجعلوا النطق أخص وصف الإنسان تميز به عن سائر الحيوانات وجعلوه الفصل الذاتي ويلزمكم على مساق ذلك أن تكون النفس الناطقة هي الإنسان من حيث الحقيقة والبدن يكون آلة وقالباً.
And you, O assembly of Ash'aris, have followed the paths of the philosophers, for you defined speech as internal utterance (al-nuṭq al-nafsī), just as they defined man by their statement 'the rational animal' (al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq). They made utterance (al-nuṭq) the most specific description of man by which he is distinguished from all other animals, and they made it the essential differentia (al-faṣl al-dhātī). And it follows from your course of reasoning that the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) would be the human being in reality, while the body would be a mere instrument and mold.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a critique directed at the Ash'aris, accusing them of adopting the philosophical method in their definition of speech. The author states that the Ash'aris have defined speech as 'internal utterance' (al-nuṭq al-nafsī), mirroring how the philosophers defined man as 'the rational animal' (al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq). The philosophers considered rationality (al-nuṭq) as the most specific attribute that distinguishes man from other animals and as the essential differentia (al-faṣl al-dhātī) in the definition. The author then points out the logical consequence: if the Ash'aris follow this path, then the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) would become the true human reality, and the body would be reduced to a mere instrument and mold. This is a reductio ad absurdum, implying that such a view is unacceptable within Islamic theology because it downplays the role of the body and implies a dualistic anthropology akin to philosophical traditions. The author thus rejects the Ash'ari notion of a separate essential attribute of internal speech, aligning with his earlier arguments that speech is merely conventional.

Key Terms
الأشاعرة
الفلاسفة
النطق النفسي
الحيوان الناطق
الفصل الذاتي
النفس الناطقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari definition of speech as internal utterance is a purely Islamic theological development, independent of Greek philosophy.
Correction: The author explicitly states that the Ash'aris have 'followed the paths of the philosophers' in this definition, and he ties it directly to the Aristotelian definition of man as a rational animal, showing the logical consequences that align with philosophical dualism.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what consequence follows from the Ash'ari definition of speech as internal utterance?
A) That speech is created in time
B) That the rational soul becomes the true human reality and the body a mere instrument
C) That animals also have speech
D) That God's speech is exactly like human speech

Key Takeaway
This passage reveals that the author sees the Ash'ari definition of speech as a dangerous borrowing from Greek philosophy, carrying implications that reduce the human being to a soul using a physical body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever adopted an idea from a non-Islamic source thinking it was harmless, without checking whether it leads to consequences that contradict your faith? What would you do if you discovered such an idea in your own thinking?
?
This lesson shows that the Ash'ari definition of speech is not merely a theological nuance—it carries implications for what it means to be human. Are you willing to examine your own beliefs about the human soul and body to see if they align with the Islamic holistic view, or have you unconsciously accepted a dualistic view from outside?
?
The author accuses the Ash'aris of borrowing from the philosophers. Think of one area in your life (worship, ethics, worldview) where you might be uncritically accepting ideas from the culture around you. The Necessity of Proving the Soul's Existence and Substantiality
The author states the necessity of proving the soul's existence and substantiality before proving its rational speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why proving the soul's existence and its nature as a substance is prerequisite for establishing its rational speech, and how al-Ash'ari's potential agreement with opponents undermines the soul's spiritual nature.

Core Idea
Proving the soul's existence and substantiality is the foundation for all rational speech about it.

Before we can discuss the soul's rational speech, we must first establish that it exists and that it is a substance. This passage introduces a required first step and then critiques a position that would deny the soul's spiritual substance, showing the consequences of such a denial. The author frames this as a necessary preparation for the later discussion of the soul's rational speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice that it begins with 'قالوا' (they said), indicating a quoted view that the author will critique.
2
Identify the two requirements
The passage states that proving the soul's existence and its substantiality are necessary first steps before proving its rational speech. Identify these two requirements.
3
Analyze the critique of al-Ash'ari
The author argues that if al-Ash'ari agrees with the Mu'tazila that the soul does not remain for two moments, he becomes like the Mu'tazili who agrees with naturalists that the soul is a body subject to change. Understand why this is problematic.
4
Understand the equation of terms
The passage ends by equating 'impossible' (مستحيل) with 'does not remain for two moments' (لا يبقى زمانين), both implying momentary existence. Consider how this relates to the denial of the soul's spiritual substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : ولا بد من إثبات النفس وإثبات جوهريته أولا حتى يصح إثبات النطق العقلي له ثانيا والأشعري إن وافق المعتزلة على أن نفي الروح والنفس جوهرا بلا عرض لا يبقى زمانيين وهو الحياة فقط، فهو كالمعتزلي إذا وافق الطبيعي منا على أن النفس ليس جوهرًا روحانيا لا يقبل الاستحالة، بل هو جسم قابل للكون والفساد، وعرض تابع للمزاج ، ولا فرق بين قولهم : مستحيل ؛ أي متغير حالا فحالا، وبين قولهم : لا يبقى زمانين ؛ أي موجود حالا فحالا.
They said: It is necessary to prove the soul and prove its substantiality first, so that the proof of rational speech for it becomes valid second. And al-Ash'ari, if he agrees with the Mu'tazila that denying the spirit and the soul as a substance without an accident does not remain for two moments, and it is only life, then he is like the Mu'tazili if he agrees with the naturalist among us that the soul is not a spiritual substance that does not accept transformation, but rather it is a body subject to generation and corruption, and an accident following the temperament. And there is no difference between their saying 'impossible' meaning changing state by state, and their saying 'does not remain for two moments' meaning existing state by state.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage presents a position (from an unnamed group, likely opponents) asserting that proving the soul's existence and its substantiality is a necessary first step before proving its rational speech. The author then critiques the consequences of denying the soul's spiritual substance. He notes that al-Ash'ari, if he agrees with the Mu'tazila that the soul is not a substance enduring two moments but only life, becomes like the Mu'tazili who agrees with the naturalists that the soul is not a spiritual substance but a body subject to change. The passage concludes by equating the terms 'impossible' (مستحيل) and 'does not remain for two moments' (لا يبقى زمانين), both implying momentary existence.

?

Second Mu'tazila Objection: Inconsistency Regarding Modes
Mu'tazila object that Ash'ari use of modes is inconsistent with their denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila critique that the Ash'ari argument for vision inconsistently relies on modes while denying their reality.

Core Idea
The Ash'aris cannot use modes to prove vision after rejecting them.

In this passage, the Mu'tazila press a second objection: the Ash'ari argument for beatific vision employs a division of modes (commonality and differentiation) but the Ash'ari school has rejected the reality of modes as mere words. This lesson unpacks the inconsistency and its implications for the proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection's context
Recall that the Ash'aris have explicitly denied the reality of modes (ahwal). Yet their proof for vision relies on distinguishing between common and differentiating factors—a classic mode-based analysis.
2
Identify the specific inconsistency
The Mu'tazila point out that the Ash'aris invalidated the differentiating mode but retained a common mode (existence) as the single correct cause. This selective use of modes is contradictory.
3
Examine the ontological claim
The Ash'ari doctrine holds that existence is identical to essence (e.g., existence in a substance is the substance itself). This undermines the idea that existence can be a genuine common factor uniting the witnessed and the absent.
4
Connect to the broader debate
This objection challenges the logical foundation of the entire Ash'ari proof for vision. If the proof relies on a concept (mode) they reject, the argument collapses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتراض ثان : أن تقسيم الحال إلى قضية الاشتراك والافتراق إنما يصح على مذهب مثبتي الحال، وأنتم معاشر الأشعرية قد نفيتم الحال ورددتم قضايا الخصوص والعموم والافتراق والاشتراك إلى محض العبارات، فكيف صرتم إلى إلزام أحكام الحال حتى أبطلتم قضية الافتراق، وعينتم قضية الاشتراك ثم حصرتم ذلك في قضية واحدة وهو الوجود على أن الموجودات إنما تختلف بوجودها عندكم، والوجود في الجوهر هو نفس الجوهر، وكما يتمايز الجوهر والعرض بالجوهرية والعرضية تمايزا في الوجود، وكما تمايز القديم والحادث في القدم والحدوث تمايزا في الوجود، فإن الوجود العام الذي شمل الموجودات حتى يصح أن يكون مصححا، وحتى يصح الجمع بين الشاهد والغائب بذلك الجامع.
And a second objection: That the division of the mode (al-hal) into the proposition of commonality and differentiation is only valid according to the doctrine of those who affirm modes. And you, the Ash'ari community, have negated the mode and reduced the propositions of specificity and generality, differentiation and commonality to mere expressions. So how have you come to necessitate the rulings of the mode, until you invalidated the proposition of differentiation and specified the proposition of commonality and then confined that to a single proposition, namely existence, given that existents only differ by their existence according to you? And existence in the substance is the substance itself, and just as the substance and accident are distinguished by substantiality and accidentally as a distinction in existence, and just as the eternal and the temporal are distinguished by eternality and temporality as a distinction in existence, then the universal existence that encompasses existents so that it can be a correct cause, and so that the comparison between the witnessed and the absent can be valid by that common factor.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage presents the second Mu'tazila objection against the Ash'ari argument. The Mu'tazila point out that the Ash'aris have denied the reality of modes (ahwal) — the idea that there are states intermediate between existence and non-existence — and they claim that distinctions like commonality and differentiation are mere verbal expressions. Yet, in their argument for vision, they rely on a division between what is common and what is different, and they specify existence as the common factor that serves as the correct cause. The Mu'tazila argue that this is inconsistent: if modes are mere words, then such a division has no real basis. Furthermore, the Ash'ari doctrine that existence is identical to the essence (e.g., existence in a substance is the substance itself) undermines the idea that existence can be a common cause that unites the witnessed and the absent. Thus, the objection challenges the logical coherence of the Ash'ari proof.

Key Terms
حال (mode)
قضية الاشتراك والافتراق (proposition of commonality and differentiation)
وجود (existence)
مثبتي الحال (affirmers of modes)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this objection is merely about terminology or a minor inconsistency.
Correction: It is a serious critique that strikes at the ontological foundation of the Ash'ari argument. If modes are unreal, the entire proof collapses.

Checkpoint
What inconsistency do the Mu'tazila highlight in the second objection?
A) The Ash'aris use modes while denying them
B) The Ash'aris affirm modes while rejecting existence
C) The Ash'aris deny the distinction between substance and accident
D) The Ash'aris claim existence is a mode

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila expose a logical tension: the Ash'ari proof for vision depends on a concept (mode) they officially reject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may find yourself using a concept in your reasoning that you don't fully believe in. When was the last time you did that, and what does this passage teach you about the danger of inconsistency in matters of faith? Will you examine your own arguments for hidden assumptions?
?
This objection exposes a gap between what the Ash'aris claim to deny and what they actually rely on. Are there beliefs you hold that contradict your actions or words? How would you respond if someone pointed out such a contradiction in your life?
?
The Mu'tazila force a choice: either affirm modes consistently or abandon the proof. What theological argument of yours might be undermined if you were held to the same standard of consistency? Are you willing to revise your thinking rather than cling to a convenient but flawed proof?

The Fifth Objection: Senses and the Vision of God
A fifth objection arguing that seeing God leads to absurdities if vision is a sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma posed by the fifth objection and the challenges it raises for the Ash'ari claim that God can be seen.

Core Idea
If vision is like other senses, seeing God implies touching, smelling Him—which is shirk.

This lesson examines a sophisticated objection against the Ash'ari view that God can be seen. The objector argues that if vision is one of the five senses, then either all senses perceive God (leading to absurdity) or vision alone is special, requiring a proof that does not beg the question. We will unpack the objector's reasoning and see why this objection has force in kalām debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objection is structured as a dilemma: either generalize the ruling across all senses or specify it to vision.
This is a typical kalām method of argumentation: force the opponent into an unacceptable alternative.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The first horn: if you say all senses perceive existence, then God would be hearable, smellable, etc.—which is shirk. The second horn: if you specify vision, you must provide a valid proof. Look for how the objector then attacks two possible proofs.
3
Follow the critique of the first proof
The first proposed proof is that vision does not affect or get affected by its object. The objector says this is exactly the point under dispute. Understand why this is question-begging.
4
Analyze the second proof and its refutation
The second proof claims vision is like knowledge and requires determination of the perceiver. The objector distinguishes intellectual determination from sensory determination, showing the latter leads to spatial location—impossible for God.
This shows the subtlety of kalām reasoning about divine attributes.
5
Reflect on the overall force of the objection
Consider why this objection is considered strong: it forces the Ash'ari to either accept shirk or produce a convincing proof of specification. Ask yourself how an Ash'ari might respond.
This builds critical thinking skills in theological argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتراض خامس قد تحقق أن الرؤية من جملة الحواس الخمس أفتقولون أنها كلها تتعلق بالوجود والمصحح لها الوجود، أم الرؤية خاصة تتعلق بكل موجود، فإن عممتم الحكم فقد افتتحتم أمرًا وهو التزام كونه تعالى مسموعا مشموما مطعوما ملموسا وذلك شرك عظيم، وإن خصصتم الحكم بالرؤية وجب عليكم إظهار دليل التخصيص، فإن قلتم : الدليل أنه لا يؤثر في المرئي ولا يتأثر منه، فقيل : هو نفس المتنازع فيه، وإن قلتم : هو علم أو في حكم العلم إلا أنه يقتضي تعيين المدرك، فيقال لكم التعيين قد يكون تعيين العقل، ولا شك أن الحس لا يستدعي ذلك التعيين فإن تعيين العقل كتخصيص العام، وتفصيل المجمل، وتقييد المطلق، وتحديد الشيء، ولا تختص أمثال هذه التعيينات بالرؤية والحس وإن قلتم التعيين تعيين الحس فهو يستدعي قبول الإشارة إليه، وذلك يقتضي حيزًا ومكانا وجهة ومقابلة، وهو محال.
A fifth objection: It has been established that vision is among the five senses. Do you say that all of them pertain to existence and their validating factor is existence, or is vision specifically said to pertain to every existent? If you generalize the ruling, you have initiated a matter which is the affirmation that He, exalted, is heard, smelled, tasted, and touched, and that is great shirk. And if you specify the ruling to vision, you must present a proof of specification. If you say: The proof is that it does not affect the visible nor is it affected by it, then it is said: That is the very point of dispute. And if you say: It is knowledge or in the ruling of knowledge, except that it necessitates determination of the perceiver, then it is said to you: Determination may be determination of the intellect, and there is no doubt that sense does not necessitate that determination, for determination by the intellect is like specifying the general, detailing the summary, restricting the absolute, and delimiting the thing; and examples of such determinations are not specific to vision and sense. And if you say: the determination is determination of sense, then that requires acceptance of reference to it, and that necessitates spatial location, place, direction, and facing, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents a fifth objection against the Ash'ari position that God can be seen (ru'ya). The objector argues that if vision is one of the five senses, the same ruling should apply to all senses: either all senses perceive existence (so God would be hearable, smellable, etc., implying corporeality and shirk) or only vision is specified, requiring a proof of specification. The objector then criticizes two possible proofs: (1) that vision does not affect or be affected by its object—but this is exactly what is disputed; (2) that vision is knowledge or like it, requiring determination of the perceiver—but this determination could be intellectual, not sensory, and would not be exclusive to vision. If the determination is sensory, it entails physical location and direction, which is impossible for God. Thus the objection challenges the Ash'ari claim that vision of God is possible without corporealism.

Key Terms
رؤية
مصحح
تخصيص
تعيين
مدرك
شرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is simply that God cannot be seen because He is not a body.
Correction: The objection is more subtle: it argues that if vision is treated as a sense, the Ash'ari must either absurdly generalize to all senses or provide a non-question-begging proof for specifying vision.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the fifth objection?
A) To prove that vision of God is impossible because God is not a body.
B) To argue that if vision of God is allowed, it must be based on a proof that distinguishes it from other senses.
C) To claim that all five senses perceive existence equally.
D) To reject the Ash'ari view that vision is knowledge.

Key Takeaway
This objection forces a choice between shirk and a valid proof for specifying vision—a powerful challenge in kalām.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you ever unconsciously picture Him as possessing physical traits like location or direction? This lesson exposes how such assumptions can lead to shirk without realizing it. Right now, examine your own heart: do you truly believe God is beyond all sensory limits, or do you sometimes imagine Him within the confines of your mind? Make a firm intention to purify your conception of God from even subtle anthropomorphism.
?
The objector demands a proof for specifying vision among the senses. Do you have a proof for your own hope of seeing God in the afterlife—or do you simply accept it as a comfortable belief without intellectual grounding? This lesson challenges you to either develop a sound rational argument or rely solely on revelation with full submission. Which path will you take?
?
Consider the seriousness of the accusation 'great shirk' in this objection. Have you ever attributed to God any quality that reduces Him to the level of created beings—perhaps unknowingly, through careless language or mental images? Write down one way you have done this and commit to replacing it with a proper affirmation of God's transcendence from now on.
