Refutation of Mu'tazila on Good and Evil
Criticizes Mu'tazila for attributing good and evil as essential qualities of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why classical Sunni scholars rejected the Mu'tazilite view that good and evil are essential attributes of actions, and why they instead linked moral value to knowledge and ignorance.

Core Idea
Moral values are not fixed in actions but depend on context and divine command.

The Mu'tazila argued that actions are inherently good or evil, known by reason. This passage presents a Sunni critique: actions vary with persons, times, and relations, so they cannot have unchanging essential moral qualities. The author points out that the Mu'tazila should have applied their reasoning to knowledge and ignorance instead.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the key claim: the Mu'tazila erred in attributing good and evil to essential attributes of actions.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite position being refuted
Recall that Mu'tazila believe good and evil are intrinsic to actions and known by reason independent of revelation. This passage directly challenges that by saying actions are not inherently good/evil.
3
Grasp the argument against inherent moral qualities
The passage gives a reason: actions differ with persons, times, and relations. Consider an example like killing—sometimes good (self-defense), sometimes evil (murder). Thus, actions cannot have fixed essential moral qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: وقد أخطأت المعتزلة حيث ردوا القبح والحسن إلى الصفات الذاتية للأفعال، وكان من حقهم تقرير ذلك في العلم والجهل؛ إذ الأفعال تختلف بالأشخاص والأزمان وسائر الإضافات وليست هي على صفات نفسية لازمة لها لا تفارقها البتة.
They said: The Mu'tazila erred when they attributed ugliness and beauty to the essential attributes of actions, whereas they should have established that regarding knowledge and ignorance; for actions differ according to persons, times, and all relations, and they are not upon inherent essential qualities that never depart from them at all.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage critiques the Mu'tazilite view that good and evil (ḥusn and qubh) are essential attributes of actions themselves. The author (via 'they said') states that the Mu'tazila erred (akhta'at) by placing moral value in the intrinsic qualities of actions. Instead, the correct approach would have been to apply this essential good/evil distinction to knowledge and ignorance, not to actions. The reason given is that actions vary with persons, times, and other relations; they do not have fixed essential qualities that are inseparable from them. For example, an action like killing can be good or evil depending on context. Thus, the Mu'tazila were mistaken in making moral judgments intrinsic to acts rather than relative.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
القبح
الحسن
الصفات الذاتية
الأفعال
العلم
الجهل
النسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazilite view is the only rational one—that good and evil are intrinsic to actions and known by reason alone.
Correction: This passage shows the Mu'tazilite position is flawed because actions are relative and context-dependent; they cannot have unchanging essential moral qualities.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main critique of the Mu'tazila in this passage?
A) They denied good and evil entirely
B) They wrongly made good and evil essential attributes of actions
C) They claimed actions never change in moral value
D) They rejected the role of reason

Key Takeaway
Actions do not have inherent moral qualities; good and evil depend on context, as the Mu'tazila were mistaken to think otherwise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If so, what does that imply about the nature of moral values? What does this lesson call you to reconsider about your own moral judgments?
?
When you judge an action as evil, do you typically consider the person, time, and context? Or do you instinctively feel it is evil in itself? This lesson challenges you to examine your moral intuitions. Will you commit to pausing and reflecting on context before condemning an action today?
?
This passage teaches that knowledge is inherently good and ignorance inherently evil. Do you treat the pursuit of knowledge as a moral duty? What is one concrete step you will take this week to increase your knowledge of Islam, knowing that ignorance is an evil to be eradicated?

Refutation of Ash'ariyya: Intrinsic Morality of Knowledge and Ignorance
A refutation of Ash'ariyya's denial of intrinsic moral value in knowledge and ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that knowledge and ignorance have intrinsic moral value, and why actions are only accidental in determining eternal outcomes.

Core Idea
Knowledge is inherently good; ignorance inherently evil—eternal fate hangs on them.

The Ash'ariyya claimed that good and evil are determined solely by divine command, denying any intrinsic moral properties in human acts. But the author counters that knowledge and ignorance carry their own moral weight—knowledge is always praiseworthy, ignorance always blameworthy—because eternal happiness and misery are directly tied to them. Actions, by contrast, are merely instrumental and variable. Let's examine this powerful argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the direct accusation: 'And the Ash'ariyya erred...' This sets up the refutation.
Pay attention to the terms 'knowledge' (العلم) and 'ignorance' (الجهل) as the core subjects.
2
Identify the error attributed to the Ash'ariyya
The author says the Ash'ariyya 'removed' the judgments of good and evil from knowledge and ignorance. Understand what that means: they denied that these have inherent moral qualities.
The phrase 'رفعتهما عنهما' means they lifted or removed the moral judgments from them.
3
Understand why knowledge and ignorance are inherently moral
The author states that knowledge has nothing blameworthy in its kind, and ignorance nothing praiseworthy. Reflect on why this is a strong claim—it asserts an essential, not accidental, property.
Contrast this with actions, which can be good or bad depending on context.
4
Connect to eternal consequences
Notice the causal link: eternal happiness and misery are specifically attached to knowledge and ignorance, not to actions. This anchors the intrinsic moral value in ultimate outcomes.
Actions are only 'helpers or preventers by accident' ('بالعرض')—they are not the decisive factor.
5
Reflect on the variability of actions
The text says actions vary with person and time, implying they lack fixed moral status. This contrasts with knowledge and ignorance, which are constant in their moral valuation.
This variability is a key reason why actions cannot be the basis for eternal reward or punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأخطأت الأشعرية حيث رفعتهما عن العلم الذي ليس في نوعه [ذميم، وعن الجهل الذي ليس في نوعه] حميد؛ إذ السعادة والشقاوة الأبدية مخصوصتان بهما مقصورتان عليهما، والأفعال معينات أو مانعات بالعرض لا بالذات، وتختلف بالنسبة إلى شخص وشخص وزمان وزمان.
And the Ash'ariyya erred when they removed them from knowledge, which has nothing blameworthy in its kind, and from ignorance, which has nothing praiseworthy in its kind; for eternal happiness and misery are specifically attached to them and confined to them, while actions are accidental helpers or preventers, not essential, and they vary with respect to person and person, time and time.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage critiques the Ash'ariyya position. The author states that the Ash'ariyya erred (akhta'at) by removing the judgments of good and evil (ḥusn and qubh) from knowledge and ignorance. Specifically, knowledge has nothing blameworthy (dhamīm) in its kind, and ignorance has nothing praiseworthy (ḥamīd) in its kind. The reason given is that eternal happiness (sa‘āda) and misery (shaqāwa) are specifically attached to knowledge and ignorance—they are confined to these two. Actions, in contrast, are only accidental helpers (mu‘īnāt) or preventers (māni‘āt) relative to happiness or misery, not essential causes. Moreover, actions vary with different persons and times, so their moral status is not fixed. Therefore, the Ash'ariyya were wrong to deny that knowledge and ignorance have inherent moral value.

Key Terms
الأشعرية
العلم
الجهل
السعادة
الشقاوة
الأفعال
بالعرض
بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ariyya denied any rational basis for good and evil, but the author specifies their error is about knowledge and ignorance, which have permanent moral significance.
Correction: The Ash'ariyya did not deny all rational morality; they erred in failing to recognize the intrinsic moral value of knowledge and ignorance, which are directly tied to eternal outcomes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do actions have a variable moral status?
A) Because they are commanded differently by God
B) Because they are accidental helpers or preventers, not essential, and vary with person and time
C) Because they are unrelated to eternal happiness and misery
D) Because they can be both good and evil at the same time

Key Takeaway
Knowledge and ignorance have intrinsic moral value because eternal happiness and misery depend on them directly, while actions are merely accidental factors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your eternal happiness depends more on what you know about Allah than on the rituals you perform? If so, how is your pursuit of knowledge reflecting that urgency?
?
When you encounter an area of ignorance in your deen, do you feel a spiritual discomfort—recognizing ignorance as inherently evil—or do you brush it off? What is one step you will take today to replace that ignorance with knowledge?
?
Your actions—prayer, charity—are only accidental helpers. What does that mean for your reliance on them? Are you trusting in your deeds or in the knowledge that leads to Him?

The Sabian Argument: Reason Alone Suffices
Relates Sabian addition to philosophers on good/evil and prophecy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sabian claim that celestial influences determine moral values and that human reason can independently discern good and evil, thus rejecting the need for prophecy.

Core Idea
Sabians argued that astrology determines morality, so reason needs no prophets.

The Sabians extended the philosophers' views by introducing astrological determinism into ethics. They claimed that the influence of planets and their spiritual administrators creates inherent beauty and ugliness in character and morals. Since all humans share the same rational nature, anyone with a sound intellect can perceive these values without prophetic guidance. This passage presents that argument, though the author's refutation is cut short.

Guided Path
1
Read the Sabian claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Observe how the Sabians build their argument from celestial influences to moral perception.
The passage is incomplete; it ends mid-sentence.
2
Identify the premises
Identify the key premises: (1) lower-world existents depend on planetary influences, (2) these influences produce good/evil fortune and moral qualities, (3) all human intellects are equal in species.
3
Examine the Quranic citation
Notice how the Sabians quote Quran 23:24—'He is only a human like you'—to argue that prophets are ordinary and unnecessary.
They use scripture to undermine prophecy.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion: no need for a teacher of good and evil, because sound reason and upright nature suffice. Ask yourself if this conclusion follows from the premises.
5
Reflect on the incompleteness
Consider that the passage breaks off. What might the author say next? How would a theologian respond to this rejection of prophecy?
This prepares you to anticipate the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم زادت الصابئة على الفلاسفة بأن قالوا كما كانت الموجودات في العالم السفلي مرتبة على تأثير الكواكب والروحانيات التي هي مدبرات للكواكب وفي اتصالاتها نظر نحس وسعد، وجب أن يكون في أثرها حسن وقبح في الخلق والأخلاق والعقول الإنسانية متساوية في النوع، فوجب أن يدركها كل عقل سليم وطبع قويم، ولا تتوقف معرفة المعقولات على من هو مثل ذلك العاقل في النوع (ما هَذَا إِلَّا بَشَرٌ مِثْلُكُمْ يُرِيدُ أَن يَتَفَضَّلَ عَلَيْكُمْ)، فنحن لا نحتاج إلى من يعرفنا حسن الأشياء وقبحها، وخيرها وشرها ونفعها وضرها، وكما كنا نستخرج
Then the Sabians added to the philosophers by saying: Just as the existents in the lower world are arranged according to the influence of the planets and the spiritual beings that are the administrators of the planets, and in their conjunctions there is consideration of evil and good fortune, so there must be in their effect beauty and ugliness in character, morals, and human intellects are equal in species. So every sound intellect and upright nature must perceive them, and the knowledge of intelligibles does not depend on someone who is like that rational person in species (He is only a human like you, he wants to gain superiority over you), so we have no need for someone to teach us the beauty and ugliness of things, their good and evil, their benefit and harm, and as we used to extract...
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage presents the Sabian position as an addition to the philosophers. The Sabians claimed that the existence of beings in the sublunary world is arranged according to the influence of planets and the spiritual beings (rūḥāniyyāt) that govern them. They argued that planetary conjunctions produce good or bad fortune (sa‘d and naḥs), and this results in beauty and ugliness (ḥusn and qubh) in character, morals, and human intellects, which are equal in species. Therefore, every sound intellect and upright nature can perceive these moral values without needing a prophet. They cited the Quranic verse (23:24) as evidence that the prophet is only a human like them, seeking superiority. Their conclusion: humans do not need anyone to teach them good and evil, benefit and harm. The passage is incomplete, cutting off at 'and as we used to extract' (kammā kunnā nastakhrij), presumably continuing with a refutation or further argument.

Key Terms
الصابئة
الفلاسفة
الكواكب
الروحانيات
نحس
سعد
حسن
قبح
العقل السليم
الطبع القويم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Sabian view is purely philosophical, but it includes astrological determinism and rejection of prophecy.
Correction: The Sabians blend astrological causation with rationalism, explicitly denying the need for prophets, as shown in their use of Quran 23:24.

Checkpoint
What was the Sabian argument's conclusion regarding prophets?
A) Prophets are necessary because astrology is unreliable
B) No prophet is needed because sound reason can discern good and evil on its own
C) Prophets are needed to interpret planetary influences
D) Only philosophers can be prophets

Key Takeaway
The Sabians argued that celestial determinism makes moral knowledge universally accessible to reason, thereby eliminating the need for prophetic guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever rely solely on your own reasoning to determine what is good and evil, without turning to revelation? In a current dilemma, are you acting as if prophets are unnecessary?
?
The Sabians argued that human intellect is equal and sufficient—but why do you still find yourself confused about moral decisions? Could it be that your reason is not as sound as you think?
?
If you truly believed that moral knowledge comes only from divine guidance, how would your daily decision-making change? What is one area where you need to stop relying on your own judgment and submit to revelation?

Criticism of the Ash'ariyya on Knowledge and Ignorance
The author criticizes the Ash'ariyya for removing moral values from knowledge and ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's critique that the Ash'ariyya mistakenly detached good and evil from knowledge and ignorance, and how eternal outcomes are tied to these states rather than to actions.

Core Idea
The Ash'ariyya wrongly separated moral values from knowledge and ignorance.

The author directly challenges the Ash'ariyya on a foundational point of moral theology. He asserts that they erred by removing moral attributions from knowledge and ignorance, when in fact eternal happiness and misery are uniquely tied to these two states. Actions, he argues, are merely accidental means, varying by person and time.

Guided Path
1

2

3
Examine the reasoning

4
Understand the role of actions

The phrase 'بالعرض لا بالذات' is key.
5

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأخطأت الأشعرية حيث رفعتهما عن العلم الذي ليس في نوعه [ذميم، وعن الجهل الذي ليس في نوعه] حميد ؛ إذ السعادة والشقاوة الأبدية مخصوصتان بهما مقصورتان عليهما، والأفعال معينات أو مانعات بالعرض لا بالذات، وتختلف بالنسبة إلى شخص وشخص وزمان وزمان.

Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage criticizes the Ash'ariyya for detaching good and evil from knowledge and ignorance. The author asserts that eternal happiness and misery are tied to knowledge and ignorance, not to actions per se. Actions only have accidental effects, not essential. Thus, the Ash'ariyya's moving of moral values away from knowledge and ignorance is an error.

The Sabian Position: Reason as Sufficient for Morality
The author presents the Sabian extension of the philosophers' view that reason alone can know good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sabian claim that human reason, unaided by revelation, can independently discern good and evil.

Core Idea
The Sabians claimed that sound reason alone can know moral truths.

The Sabians extended the philosophers' view, arguing that since human intellects are equal in species, they can deduce good and evil without prophets. This lesson presents their argument and the Quranic verses they cited to support their rejection of prophetic authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Sabian Premise
Notice how the Sabians base their argument on the influence of planets and the equality of human intellects.
The planetary influence is a key part of their worldview.
2
Understand the Conclusion
They conclude that every sound mind can independently know good and evil, so no lawgiver is needed.
This directly challenges the necessity of prophethood.
3
Examine the Quranic Citations
The Sabians quote verses from Surah al-Mu'minun and Surah al-Taghabun to argue against following prophets.
They use these verses to claim that prophets are just humans like us.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم زادت الصابئة على الفلاسفة بأن قالوا كما كانت الموجودات في العالم السفلي مرتبة على تأثير الكواكب والروحانيات التي هي مدبرات للكواكب وفي اتصالاتها نظر نحس وسعد، وجب أن يكون في أثرها حسن وقبح في الخلق والأخلاق والعقول الإنسانية متساوية في النوع، فوجب أن يدركها كل عقل سليم وطبع قويم، ولا تتوقف معرفة المعقولات على من هو مثل ذلك العاقل في النوع ( ما هَذَا إِلَّا بَشَرٌ مِثْلُكُمْ يُرِيدُ أَن يَتَفَضَّلَ عَلَيْكُمْ ﴾ [المؤمنون: ٢٤]، فنحن لا نحتاج إلى من يعرفنا حسن الأشياء وقبحها، وخيرها وشرها ونفعها وضرها، وكما كنا نستخرج بالعقول من طبائع الأشياء منافعها ومضارها، كذلك نستنبط من أفعال نوع الإنسان حسنها وقبحها، فنلابس ما هو حسن منها بحسب الاستطاعة، ونجتنب قبح منها بحسب الطاقة، فلا نحتاج إلى شارع متحكم على عقولنا بما ولا يهتدي ( أَبَشَرِّ يَهْدُونَنَا ﴾ [التغابن : ٦]، ﴿ وَلَبِنْ أَطَعْتُم بَشَرًا مِثْلَكُمْ إِنَّكُمْ إِذًا لَّخَسِرُونَ ) [المؤمنون: ٣٤]، فهذه مقالة القوم، ولها شرح ذكرناه في كتابنا الموسوم بالملل والنحل).
Then the Sabians added to the philosophers by saying: Just as the existents in the lower world are arranged according to the influence of the planets and the spiritual beings that manage the planets, and in their conjunctions there is consideration of misfortune and good fortune, it must be that in their effect there is beauty and ugliness in creation, morals, and human intellects that are equal in species. Therefore, it must be that every sound intellect and upright nature perceives them, and knowledge of intelligibles does not depend on one who is like that rational being in species. (They say:) 'This is only a human like you, wanting to be superior to you.' (Qur'an 23:24) So we do not need someone to inform us of the goodness and ugliness of things, their good and evil, their benefit and harm. Just as we extract by reason from the natures of things their benefits and harms, likewise we deduce from the actions of the human species their goodness and ugliness. So we engage in what is good among them according to ability, and avoid the ugly according to capacity. Thus we do not need a lawgiver who controls our reason. (They cite:) 'Should a human guide us?' (Qur'an 64:6) and 'If you obey a human like yourselves, you will indeed be losers.' (Qur'an 23:34). This is the doctrine of those people, and its explanation we have mentioned in our book titled 'al-Milal wa al-Nihal'.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage presents the Sabian position, which extends the philosophers' argument. The Sabians claim that the universe is influenced by planets and spiritual beings, and that human intellects are equal in species, so any sound mind can discern moral values independently. They reject the need for prophets or lawgivers, citing Quranic verses to support their denial. The author then notes that he has explained this further in his book al-Milal wa al-Nihal.

Key Terms
الصابئة
الفلاسفة
الكواكب
العقول
الحسن
القبح
الشارع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sabians believed that moral knowledge is innate to all humans.
Correction: The Sabians argued that human intellects are equal in species and can deduce morality through reason from the nature of actions, not that it is innate.

Checkpoint
What is the central claim of the Sabians presented in this passage?
A) Good and evil are known only through revelation.
B) All human intellects are equal and can independently know good and evil.
C) Moral values are determined by planetary conjunctions.
D) Prophets are needed to explain moral values.

Key Takeaway
The Sabians claimed that human reason, being equal across individuals, can independently know good and evil, thus eliminating the need for prophetic guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

How does your heart react to submitting your intellect to revelation? Commit today to one specific act of obedience that goes against your own preference.

The Reincarnationist Position on Good and Evil
The author presents the reincarnationist view that moral values are determined by reward and punishment in previous lives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Tanasukhiyya view that moral values are not intrinsic but are merely the effects of reward and punishment from past actions, and why they reject reason and revelation as arbiters of good and evil.

Core Idea
For the reincarnationists, good and evil are nothing but the echoes of past deeds.

This passage introduces a radically different perspective on ethics from a sect known as the Tanasukhiyya (reincarnationists). They argue that what we call 'good' and 'evil' are not objective qualities knowable by reason or revelation, but simply the results of a cosmic law of requital. An action is 'good' if it is a reward for a previous good deed, and 'evil' if it is a punishment. This challenges the mainstream Islamic view that good and evil are known through divine law and human reason. Let's read their argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Get a sense of the flow of the argument.
The Persian phrase 'kard va badasht' means 'action and reaction'.
2
Identify the key components of the Tanasukhiyya view
Notice how they start with human distinctiveness (choice, speech, reason), then describe the cycle of ascent/descent based on deeds, and finally conclude that moral value is defined by requital.
3
Analyze the conclusion and its implications
The Tanasukhiyya explicitly reject reason and law as sources of moral judgment. Ask yourself: how does this contrast with the Islamic position? What problems does this create?
The author presents this view critically; it is not endorsed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزادت التناسخية على الصابئة بأن قالت : نوع الإنسان لما كان موصوفا بنوع اختيار في أفعاله مخصوصا بنطق وعقل في علومه وأحواله، ارتفع عن الدرجة الحيوانية استسخارًا لها، فإن كانت أعماله على مناهج الدرجة الإنسانية ارتفعت إلى الملكية أو إلى النبوة، وإن كانت على مناهج الدرجة الحيوانية انخفضت إلى الحيوانية أو إلى أسفل، وهو أبدا في أحد أمرين ؛ إما فعل الجزاء أو جزاء على فعل كما يقولون كرد و باداشت، فما بالنا نقول : محتاج في أفعاله وأحواله إلى شخص مثله يقبح ويحسن ، فلا العقل يحسن ويقبح ولا الشرع، لكن حسن أفعاله جزاء أفعال غيره، وقبح أفعاله كذلك، وربما يصير حسنها وقبحها صورا على حسن أفعال غيره.
And the reincarnationists (al-Tanasukhiyya) added to the Sabians by saying: Since the human species is described with a kind of choice in its actions, distinguished by speech and reason in its sciences and states, it has risen above the animal level as a subjugation of them. If his deeds follow the paths of the human level, he ascends to the angelic level or to prophecy. And if they follow the paths of the animal level, he descends to the animal level or lower. He is forever in one of two matters: either an act of requital or a requital for an act, as they say 'kard va badasht' (action and reaction). So why do we say: He needs in his actions and states a person like him who pronounces something ugly or beautiful? For neither reason pronounces beauty or ugliness, nor does the Law. Rather, the beauty of his actions is the requital for the actions of others, and the ugliness of his actions likewise. And perhaps their beauty and ugliness become forms upon the beauty of the actions of others.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage presents the Tanasukhiyya (reincarnationists) view that humans are in a cycle of rebirth based on their deeds. They reject the need for a prophet to determine good and evil because moral values are not intrinsic but are merely results of past actions. Actions are either punishments or rewards. The author is critical of this position.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Tanasukhiyya deny any moral distinctions altogether, or that they are complete skeptics about good and evil.
Correction: They do not deny moral distinctions; rather, they redefine them. For the Tanasukhiyya, good and evil are real but not intrinsic—they are determined by the consequences of past actions in a cycle of rebirth. An action is good if it is a reward for a previous good deed, and evil if it is a punishment.

D) The intrinsic nature of the action.

Key Takeaway
The Tanasukhiyya view holds that moral good and evil are not inherent or known by reason/law but are simply the consequences of past actions in a cycle of reincarnation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Tanasukhiyya claim that all your present suffering or happiness is merely the requital for others' past actions. If you truly believed that every hardship you face today is a punishment for a past life, how would you respond to the injustice you see around you? Would you feel more resigned or more compelled to change? Examine your own heart: can you accept that moral value is nothing but an echo of other people's deeds?
?
The passage says that a person's deeds cause them to ascend or descend to different levels of existence. Think of a single action you performed today. According to this system, does it move you toward the angelic or the animal? What does that reveal about your own personal scale of good and evil? Are you comfortable with the idea that your eternal destiny is determined by a mechanical law of requital rather than by a personal relationship with your Creator?
?
The Tanasukhiyya argue that neither reason nor divine law can define good and evil; only the cycle of requital does. Have you ever assumed, without reflection, that something is 'obviously' good or evil? Where does that assumption come from? Are you willing to test it against this passage's challenge? Or do you find yourself instinctively rejecting their view? What does that instinct say about where you truly place your moral anchor?

The Sabian Doctrine: Reason Alone Determines Good and Evil
Sabian claim that reason alone suffices for moral knowledge, with Quranic support.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sabian claim that human reason, by examining natural properties of things, can discern good and evil without the need for divine revelation.

Core Idea
Reason, not revelation, is sufficient for moral knowledge according to the Sabians.

This passage presents one of the classical theological positions that denied the necessity of revealed law. The Sabians argued that the natural world itself provides all the guidance humans need, and that reason alone can determine right from wrong. As you read, note how they use both logical argument and Quranic verses to support their view.

Guided Path
1
Read the Sabian argument
Read the passage carefully. The Sabians claim that from the natures of things we know benefits and harms, and similarly we deduce good and evil from human actions.
Notice the parallel between natural properties and human actions.
2
Identify their conclusion
The Sabians conclude that we do not need a lawgiver. Find where they state this explicitly.
3
Examine their use of Quranic verses
The Sabians quote Quran 64:6 and 23:34. What is the purpose of these quotations? How do they support the idea that following a human prophet is unnecessary?
The verses question following human guides.
4
Consider the author's remark
The author states that this is the doctrine of the Sabians and refers to his own book for a fuller explanation. Why do you think the author includes this note?
This passage is a report, not the author's own view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالعقول من طبائع الأشياء منافعها ومضارها، كذلك نستنبط من أفعال نوع الإنسان حسنها وقبحها، فنلابس ما هو حسن منها بحسب الاستطاعة، ونجتنب قبح منها بحسب الطاقة، فلا نحتاج إلى شارع متحكم على عقولنا، ولا يهتدي (أَبَشَرٌ يَهْدُونَنَا) [التغابن: ٦]، (وَلَئِنْ أَطَعْتُم بَشَرًا مِثْلَكُمْ إِنَّكُمْ إِذًا لَّخَاسِرُونَ) [المؤمنون: ٣٤]، فهذه مقالة القوم، ولها شرح ذكرناه في كتابنا الموسوم بالملل والنحل.
By intellects from the natures of things (we know) their benefits and harms, likewise we deduce from the actions of the human type their goodness and badness, so we engage in what is good among them according to ability, and avoid what is bad among them according to capacity, so we do not need a lawgiver who imposes upon our intellects, and they do not follow (a human who guides us?) [Quran 64:6], (and if you obey a human like yourselves, then you will indeed be losers) [Quran 23:34]. This is the doctrine of the people, and we have an explanation of it in our book titled al-Milal wa al-Nihal.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage presents the Sabian doctrine, a position that denies the necessity of revealed law. The author begins by stating the Sabian claim: from the natural properties of things, human reason can discern benefits and harms. Similarly, from human actions, reason deduces good and evil. The Sabians argue that people can then pursue good according to ability and avoid evil according to capacity, thus having no need for a lawgiver who dictates to their intellects. They support this with Quranic verses that question following a human guide and warn against obeying a mere human. The author notes that this is the doctrine of the Sabians and refers to his own book, al-Milal wa al-Nihal, for a fuller explanation. The passage sets up the first of several views that will be refuted.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is the personal belief of the author.
Correction: The author is reporting the Sabian doctrine, not endorsing it. He will later refute it.

D) The teachings of prophets

Key Takeaway
The Sabians argued that reason alone, by examining natural and human actions, can determine good and evil, making revelation unnecessary.

The Tanasukhiyya Doctrine: Reincarnation as Moral Cause and Effect
Describes the Tanasukhiyya addition of reincarnation to Sabian ideas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Tanasukhiyya belief that reincarnation based on actions replaces the need for revealed law and a separate afterlife.

Core Idea
Reincarnation makes moral actions automatic cosmic consequences.

The Tanasukhiyya (reincarnationists) extended the Sabian trust in reason by adding a theory of soul migration driven by one's deeds. According to them, the moral quality of actions determines whether a soul rises to angelic status or descends to animality, all within a perpetual cycle of cause and effect. This passage presents their argument against the necessity of a divine lawgiver and a future reckoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the Tanasukhiyya argument. Notice the key terms.
The passage is from a doxographic work, so it summarizes a sect's view.
2
Identify the core claim
The Tanasukhiyya say human actions determine the next existence, and there is no afterlife reward or punishment. Good deeds lead to angelic or prophetic status, bad deeds lead to animality or lower.
Note the use of Persian terms 'kard u badāsht' for action and requital.
3
Understand the rejection of law and reason
They claim neither reason nor revealed law defines good and evil; instead, goodness or badness is simply recompense for prior deeds. This eliminates the need for a human lawgiver.
4
Reflect on the implications
This doctrine makes morality a natural process of cause and effect, removing the need for prophets, divine commands, and a final judgment day.
Consider how this contrasts with orthodox Islamic teachings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزادت التناسخية على الصابئة بأن قالت: نوع الإنسان لما كان موصوفا بنوع اختيار في أفعاله مخصوصا بنطق وعقل في علومه وأحواله، ارتفع عن الدرجة الحيوانية استسخارًا لها، فإن كانت أعماله على مناهج الدرجة الإنسانية ارتفعت إلى الملكية أو إلى النبوة، وإن كانت على مناهج الدرجة الحيوانية انخفضت إلى الحيوانية أو إلى أسفل، وهو أبدا في أحد أمرين: إما فعل الجزاء أو جزاء على فعل كما يقولون كرد و باداشت، فما بالنا نقول: محتاج في أفعاله وأحواله إلى شخص مثله يقبح ويحسن، فلا العقل يحسن ويقبح ولا الشرع، لكن حسن أفعاله جزاء أفعال غيره، وقبح أفعاله كذلك، وربما يصير حسنها وقبحها صورا حيوانية، وربما يصير الحسن والقبح في الحيوانية أفعالا إنسانية، وليس بعد هذا العالم عالم جزا يحكم فيه ويحاسب ويثاب ويعاقب.
And the reincarnationists added to the Sabians by saying: the human species, since it is described with a kind of choice in its actions and distinguished by speech and intellect in its sciences and states, rose above the animal degree, subjugating it. So if its deeds are according to the ways of the human degree, it rises to the angelic degree or to prophethood; and if they are according to the ways of the animal degree, it descends to animality or below. And it is always in one of two states: either the act of recompense or recompense for an act, as they say 'kard u badāsht'. So why do we say that he is in need in his actions and states of a person like him who declares good and evil? Thus neither reason declares good and evil nor the law, but rather the goodness of his actions is recompense for the actions of others, and the badness of his actions likewise. And perhaps their goodness and badness become animal forms, and perhaps goodness and badness in animality become human actions. And there is no after this world a world of recompense in which judgment is passed and reckoning is taken and reward and punishment are given.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage describes the Tanasukhiyya (reincarnationist) addition to the Sabian view. They agree with the Sabians on the sufficiency of reason but add a theory of soul transmigration based on moral actions. The argument: humans, possessing choice, speech, and intellect, have risen above animals. Their actions determine their next existence: if good, they rise to angelic or prophetic status; if bad, they descend to animality or lower. The process is perpetual recompense (kard u badāsht, Persian for 'action and requital'). Thus, there is no need for a human lawgiver to declare good and evil; the moral quality of actions is simply the natural result of previous deeds. Even the distinction between good and evil can become embodied in animal forms or human forms, and there is no separate afterlife of reward and punishment. This doctrine eliminates the role of revealed law by making morality a matter of cosmic cause and effect.

Key Terms
تناسخية
جزاء
استسخار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Tanasukhiyya reject morality entirely and believe in random reincarnation.
Correction: They actually ground morality in a system of cause and effect: good actions lead to higher states, bad actions to lower states. They do not deny moral distinctions but reinterpret them as natural consequences.

Checkpoint
According to the Tanasukhiyya, what determines whether a soul rises to angelic status or descends to animality?
A) Divine decree
B) The teachings of prophets
C) The nature of its actions in the current life
D) Random chance

Key Takeaway
The Tanasukhiyya replace revealed judgment with a natural cycle of recompense where actions determine one's next state, eliminating the need for prophets and an afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act as if your good deeds automatically cancel out your bad ones, without turning to God for forgiveness? How does the Tanasukhiyya's automatic cycle mirror a heart that ignores divine mercy and judgment? Examine one area where you rely on moral balancing rather than sincere tawbah.
?
If you truly believed there was no final judgment, would your moral decisions change? Be honest: is your obedience rooted in love for God or merely in fear of punishment? Identify one act of worship you do primarily out of habit or expectation of reward, and commit to renewing your intention for Allah alone.
?
The Tanasukhiyya claim reason alone can determine good and evil. In what area of your life do you rely on your own reasoning instead of God's revelation to decide what is right? This week, pick one situation where you will actively submit your judgment to the Qur'an and Sunnah, even if it contradicts your initial logic.

The Barahima Dilemma: Reason vs. Revelation
Barahima present a dilemma against prophetic necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Barahima's logical argument against the necessity of prophets and why it challenges the concept of revelation.

Core Idea
If reason alone can judge all commands, prophets become redundant.

The Barahima, a school of thought often associated with Indian traditions, presented a sharp challenge to the need for prophets. Their argument is a simple but powerful dilemma: if a prophet's teaching is rational, human reason already has access to it; if it is irrational, it cannot be accepted. This passage presents that dilemma in its starkest form, forcing us to consider what role prophecy plays if reason is sufficient.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the English translation. Notice the structure of the argument as a conditional ('if... then...').
The Barahima are building on a previous argument from reincarnationists.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The argument splits into two possibilities: either the prophet's command is rational or it is not. Trace how each horn leads to the conclusion that prophets are unnecessary.
3
Understand the underlying assumption
The dilemma assumes that reason is the sole arbiter of truth and acceptability. Consider why this assumption is problematic from an Islamic perspective.
This connects to broader debates about the role of revelation.
4
Reflect on the challenge to revelation
If accepted, this argument would deny the need for any divine law. Think about how a Muslim theologian would respond to preserve the necessity of prophethood.
Sanusi and other scholars address this later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزادت البراهمة على التناسخية بأن قالوا: نحن لا نحتاج إلى شريعة وشارع، فإن ما يأمر به النبي لا يخلو إما أن يكون معقولا أو لا يكون معقولا، فإن كان معقولا فقد استغنى بالعقل عن النبي، وإن لم يكن معقولا لم يكن مقبولا.
And the Brahmins added to the reincarnationists by saying: we do not need a law and a lawgiver, for what the prophet commands is either rational or not rational. If it is rational, then reason suffices without the prophet; and if it is not rational, it is not acceptable.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage presents the Barahima (Brahmins) argument, which builds on the previous views. They assert that there is no need for any religious law or lawgiver. Their reasoning is a simple dilemma: whatever a prophet commands is either rational or irrational. If it is rational, human reason alone is sufficient to know it, so the prophet is superfluous. If it is irrational, it cannot be accepted because reason rejects it. In either case, prophethood is unnecessary. This argument challenges the very concept of revelation by making reason the sole criterion for truth and morality. The Barahima thus deny the need for any prophetic mission.

Key Terms
براهمة
شريعة
شارع
معقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Barahima simply reject prophets without reason.
Correction: The Barahima offer a sophisticated logical dilemma that challenges the need for revelation on rational grounds, claiming that either reason suffices or the command is unacceptable.

Checkpoint

C) Prophets are only needed for spiritual matters, not for morality
D) Prophets are impossible because no one can be sinless

Key Takeaway
The Barahima argue that prophets are unnecessary because either reason is sufficient or the command is irrational and thus unacceptable.

The Dialectical Link Between Denying God's Speech and Action
Author shows dialectical relationship between denying God's speech and action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the surprising symmetry between denying God's speech and denying human acquisition, and how each leads to a form of compulsion or free will that actually mirrors the other.

Core Idea
Denying God's speech makes you Jabriyya; denying human action makes you Qadariyya — but each shares the other's vice.

In this passage, the author unveils a subtle insight about two major theological sects: the Jabriyya (compulsionists) and the Qadariyya (free-will advocates). He shows that denying different divine attributes can lead to positions that, from a certain angle, are identical. This lesson will walk you through that dialectical dance, helping you see how these seemingly opposite doctrines are connected.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's repeated pattern: 'Whoever denies X has denied Y and becomes...'
The author uses parallel constructions for both cases.
2
Identify the two denials
The passage describes two denials: denial of God's speech (qawl) and denial of human acquisition (kasb). For each, note the consequence regarding Jabriyya and Qadariyya.
3
Understand the flipped perspective
The author then says the Qadariyya are Jabriyya from one angle and the Jabriyya are Qadariyya from another. Ask yourself: why would that be?
This is the 'subtle point' that the author wants you to grasp.
4
Reflect on the interdependence
Consider how the denial of one divine attribute (speech) leads to a kind of compulsion, and denial of another (human action) leads to a kind of free will, but from a different perspective they swap. Reflect on why these attributes are so deeply connected.
This shows that the divine attributes are not isolated; they form a coherent whole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكثيرا ما نقول: مَن نفى قول الله فقد نفى الفعل فصار من أوحش الجبرية؛ أعني أثبت جبرًا على الله تعالى وجبرًا على العبد، ومن نفى إكساب العباد فقد نفى قول الله صار من أوحش القدرية؛ أعني قدرًا على الله وقدرًا على العبد، والقدرية جبرية من حيث نفي الكلام والقول والأمر، والجبرية قدرية من حيث نفي الفعل والكسب والمأمور به، فلينتبه لهذه الدقيقة.
And we often say: Whoever denies the speech (qawl) of God has denied the action (fiʿl) and becomes one of the ugliest of the Jabriyya; I mean he affirms compulsion (jabr) upon God Almighty and compulsion upon the servant. And whoever denies the acquisition (kasb) of the servants has denied the speech of God and becomes one of the ugliest of the Qadariyya; I mean he affirms power (qadr) upon God and power upon the servant. And the Qadariyya are Jabriyya from the perspective of denying speech, word, and command, and the Jabriyya are Qadariyya from the perspective of denying action, acquisition, and the commanded thing. So pay attention to this subtle point.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical observation where the author demonstrates a surprising symmetry between two theological positions. He states that whoever denies God's speech (qawl) – i.e., that God speaks or has an attribute of speech – has also denied God's action (fiʿl) and thus falls into the worst kind of Jabriyya (compulsionism). This is because by denying speech, one effectively affirms compulsion (jabr) upon God (since God is compelled to not speak?) and upon the servant (since the servant has no command to follow?). Conversely, whoever denies the acquisition (kasb) of servants – i.e., the Ashʿarite concept that humans acquire their actions – has denied God's speech and becomes one of the ugliest Qadariyya (free-will advocates). This means they affirm power (qadr) upon God and upon the servant. Then comes the flipping insight: the Qadariyya are actually Jabriyya from the perspective of denying speech, word, and command, while the Jabriyya are Qadariyya from the perspective of denying action, acquisition, and the commanded thing. So each sect, from a different angle, shares a vice with the other. The author concludes by urging attention to this subtle point (dāqiqa), indicating its importance for understanding the interdependency of divine attributes.

Key Terms
قول
فعل
جبرية
قدرية
كسب
جبر
قدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think Jabriyya and Qadariyya are completely opposite and unrelated sects.
Correction: The author shows they share a common ground: each denies an aspect that the other denies from a different angle, making them mirror images in terms of their denials.

Checkpoint
According to the author, from what perspective are the Qadariyya considered Jabriyya?
A) From the perspective of denying action and acquisition.
B) From the perspective of denying speech, word, and command.
C) From the perspective of affirming divine transcendence.
D) From the perspective of affirming human free will.

Key Takeaway
The denial of God's speech and the denial of human acquisition are interconnected, revealing that Jabriyya and Qadariyya are not polar opposites but share common ground in their rejections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking 'God made me do it' or 'I had no choice'? How does this passage reveal that such an excuse might actually imply a denial of God's speech and action in your life? What will you do the next time you are tempted to blame God for your actions?
?
In moments of self-reliance, do you unconsciously act as if your own will is completely independent of God's command? How does this passage warn you that denying God's speech in practice leads to a subtle form of Qadariyya? What concrete step will you take today to acknowledge God's command over your choices?
?
This passage shows that Jabriyya and Qadariyya are not as opposed as they seem—both stem from denying something about God. Where in your own thinking might you be unwittingly denying a divine attribute? Are you willing to examine your beliefs for hidden extremes, even if it is uncomfortable?

The Mutual Implications of Denying Speech and Action
The author shows mutual implications between denying God's speech and action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how denying God's speech and human action are not separate errors but mutually implicating extremes.

Core Idea
Denying God's speech and human action lead to a surprising mutual entanglement of Jabriyya and Qadariyya.

This passage presents a subtle argument about two opposing theological schools—Jabriyya and Qadariyya. The author shows that each extreme position actually implies the other when you examine the denial of God's speech versus human action. Pay close attention to how the logic unfolds, as the conclusion may challenge your assumptions about these groups.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'Whoever denies X becomes Y.' Let the author's argument sink in.
2
Identify the two conditional statements
Find the two 'whoever' clauses: one about denying God's speech leading to Jabriyya, and one about denying acquisition (kasb) leading to Qadariyya. Note the terms used for each.
The first says 'min nafa qawl Allah faqad nafa al-fi'l' (denying speech denies action).
3
Notice the reversal
Read the final sentence: 'al-Qadariyya Jabriyya min hayth ... wa al-Jabriyya Qadariyya min hayth ...' This reverses the initial classification. Ask yourself: why would each group be considered the opposite in a different respect?
This is the subtle point (dāqiqa) the author emphasizes.
4
Reflect on the mutual implication
Consider how denying God's speech forces compulsion on both God and humans, while denying human acquisition forces independent power on both. The two positions are not simple opposites but intertwined.
The author warns us to pay attention to this subtlety.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكثيرا ما نقول: من نفى قول الله فقد نفى الفعل فصار من أوحش الجبرية؛ أعني أثبت جبرا على الله تعالى وجبرا على العبد، ومن نفى كسب العباد فقد نفى قول الله صار من أوحش القدرية؛ أعني قدرا على الله وقدرا على العبد، والقدرية جبرية من حيث نفي الكلام والقول والأمر، والجبرية قدرية من حيث نفي الفعل والكسب والمأمور به، فلينتبه لهذه الدقيقة.
And we often say: Whoever denies God's speech has denied action, so he becomes of the most heinous Jabriyya; I mean he affirms compulsion upon God and compulsion upon the servant. And whoever denies the acquisition (kasb) of servants has denied God's speech, so he becomes of the most heinous Qadariyya; I mean he affirms power upon God and power upon the servant. The Qadariyya are Jabriyya in terms of denying speech, word, and command, and the Jabriyya are Qadariyya in terms of denying action, acquisition, and that which is commanded. So pay attention to this subtle point.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is a classification and argument. The author presents a reciprocal relationship: denying God's speech leads to the extreme form of Jabriyya because it implies compulsion on God and the servant. Conversely, denying human acquisition (kasb) leads to extreme Qadariyya because it implies independent power for both. Interestingly, the author then states that Qadariyya are actually Jabriyya in one respect (denying speech) and Jabriyya are Qadariyya in another (denying action). This reveals a mutual entanglement. The key is that both positions are extreme and mutually inclusive. The author urges the reader to pay attention to this subtle point (dāqiqa).

Key Terms
الجبرية
القدرية
قول الله
الكسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Jabriyya and Qadariyya are simple opposites with no overlap.
Correction: This passage shows that each position implies the other in a different respect: Qadariyya are Jabriyya in denying speech, and Jabriyya are Qadariyya in denying action.

Checkpoint

A) Jabriyya
B) Qadariyya
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Denying God's speech and denying human action are not separate errors but lead to a mutual entanglement of Jabriyya and Qadariyya, a subtle point the author urges us to understand.

Refutation of the Philosophers' Division of Existence
Refutes the philosophers' division of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' division of existence into good and evil is logically invalid according to al-Shahrastani.

Core Idea
The philosophers' own definitions make their division self-contradictory.

The philosophers claimed existence contains pure good, pure evil, and mixed. But as al-Shahrastani points out, they define good as existence and evil as non-existence. This leads to a logical problem: how can existence contain non-existence? Let's examine his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author begins by questioning what 'good' and 'evil' mean.
2
Identify the philosophers' definitions
The author states that for the philosophers, 'good' applies to every existent and 'evil' to every non-existent. Understand this metaphysical usage.
This is key to the refutation.
3
Spot the contradiction
If evil is non-existence, then saying existence includes pure evil means existence includes non-existence — which is impossible. The division becomes a tautology and a contradiction.
Think about how this makes the division invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الجواب عن مقالة الفلاسفة قولهم : إن الوجود قد اشتمل على خير محض وشر محض، وخير وشر ممتزجين فهو كلام من لم يتحقق الخير والشر ما هو، فما المعنى بالخير؟ أولا فإن الخير يطلق على كل موجود عندكم، وعلى هذا الشر يطلق على كل معدوم وعلى هذا لم يستمر قولكم : الوجود يشتمل على خير مطلق فكأنكم قلتم : اشتمل الوجود على الوجود ، وهو تكرار غير مفيد ولم يستتب قولكم على شر مطلق، فإن الشر المطلق هو المعدوم ، والوجود كيف يشتمل على العدم؟ فلم يصح التقسيم رأسًا
As for the answer to the statement of the philosophers, their saying that existence has included pure good and pure evil, and good and evil mixed, it is the speech of one who has not verified what good and evil are. So what is meant by 'good'? Firstly, for you, 'good' is applied to every existent, and on this basis 'evil' is applied to every non-existent. On this basis, your saying that existence includes absolute good does not hold, for it is as if you said: existence includes existence, which is a useless repetition. And your saying about absolute evil does not hold, for absolute evil is non-existence, and how can existence include non-existence? Thus the division is not valid at all.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' division of existence into three categories: pure good, pure evil, and mixed. The author (al-Shahrastani) points out that the philosophers themselves define good as whatever exists and evil as whatever does not exist. Therefore, claiming that existence includes evil is contradictory, because evil, being non-existence, cannot be included in existence. The division collapses into a tautology (existence includes existence) and a contradiction (existence includes non-existence). The author concludes the division is invalid.

Key Terms
خير (good)
شر (evil)
وجود (existence)
عدم (non-existence)
تقسيم (division)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' division refers to moral good and evil.
Correction: In this passage, the author points out that the philosophers use 'good' to mean existence and 'evil' to mean non-existence, not moral qualities.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the philosophers' division of existence into good and evil invalid?
A) Because it implies a contradiction between existence and non-existence.
B) Because good and evil are subjective.
C) Because the philosophers did not intend a logical division.
D) Because the division is based on revelation not reason.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' division of existence into good and evil collapses because their own definitions make it self-contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says the philosophers 'have not verified what good and evil are.' Have you truly verified your understanding of good and evil from revelation, or do you rely on cultural or personal feelings? This lesson demands that you ground your moral framework in the truth. What specific step will you take this week to learn the Islamic definitions of khayr and sharr from the Quran and Sunnah?

If the philosophers had been honest with themselves, they would have seen their division collapse. Where might you be holding onto a comfortable but false belief because you haven't examined its foundations? Pray for the courage to dismantle it and rebuild on truth.

Refuting Astrology: Nature vs Moral Command
Author refutes Sabian astrological determinism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Sabian claim by distinguishing between natural and moral qualities and locating the real dispute in divine command.

Core Idea
Astrology may affect nature, but morality comes only from God's command.

The Sabians believed planets caused moral good and evil. In response, the author makes a strategic concession: even if planets influence natural qualities, they cannot determine moral value. He then shifts the debate to where it belongs—the divine command that assigns reward and punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the author's initial concession and the shift to the real point of contention.
The passage is concise; every phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the rhetorical strategy
The author concedes potential planetary influence on natural qualities (taslīm al-musāhalah). This is a dialectical move to isolate the actual disagreement.
Concession does not mean agreement; it's for argument's sake.
3
Distinguish natural vs moral qualities
Natural qualities (khalqah) like physical beauty or ugliness are not disputed. Moral qualities (amr) are based on divine command and are the real subject of debate.
This distinction is central to the refutation.
4
Apply the conclusion
Understand that God's commands (ifʿal, lā tafʿal) determine moral value, and recompense in the afterlife is based on obedience, not planetary positions.
This upholds divine transcendence and moral responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن مقالة الصائبة نقول : أنتم منازعون في إثبات تأثيرات الكواكب في الأجسام السفلية كل المنازعة ولنسلم لكم ذلك تسليم المساهلة، فالسعد والنحس لم يؤثر إلا في الحسن و القبح من حيث الخلقة، وذلك غير متنازع فيه، وإنما النزاع بيننا في الحسن والقبح من حيث الأمر، ولا شك أن الله تعالى حكم في أفعال العباد بافعل ولا تفعل، وعلى ذلك الحكم جزى في الدار الآخرة.
As for the answer to the statement of the Sabians, we say: You dispute completely the proof of the influences of the planets on sublunary bodies. But let us concede that to you as a lenient concession. So good fortune and bad fortune only affect the beautiful and ugly in terms of nature (khalqah), and that is not disputed. Rather, the dispute between us is concerning the beautiful and ugly in terms of command (amr). There is no doubt that God Almighty has decreed concerning the actions of servants 'do' and 'do not', and according to that decree He will recompense in the hereafter.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a concise refutation of the Sabian belief in astrological influences. The author first notes that the Sabians themselves disagree about proving planetary effects on sublunary bodies (الأجسام السفلية). Then, for the sake of argument (تسليم المساهلة), he concedes that planets may influence natural qualities like physical beauty or ugliness (الحسن والقبح من حيث الخلقة). However, the real point of contention is moral goodness and badness that arises from divine command (الحسن والقبح من حيث الأمر). The author asserts that God has commanded actions with 'do' and 'do not', and recompense in the afterlife is based on obedience or disobedience, not on planetary alignment.

Key Terms

أجسام سفلية
سعد
نحس

قبح
خلقة
أمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author denies all planetary influence, but he actually concedes influence on natural qualities and restricts the disagreement to moral value from divine command.
Correction: The author does not deny planetary influence on natural qualities; he only denies that they determine moral value, which is based on divine command.

Checkpoint

A) Whether planets exist
B) Whether planets influence natural qualities
C) Whether moral goodness and badness derive from divine command
D) Whether recompense occurs after death

Key Takeaway
The Sabian claim is refuted by distinguishing natural qualities (possibly influenced by planets) from moral qualities (based solely on divine command).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Mu'tazilite Argument for Divine Purpose and Benefit
Presentation of the Mu'tazilite argument that God acts for wisdom and benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that God must act for the benefit of His creatures, and the internal debate among them about whether the most beneficial act is obligatory on God.

Core Idea
The wise act for a purpose; God, being all-wise, must act for our benefit.

The Mu'tazilites developed a rational argument about divine purpose. They argued that a wise agent never acts pointlessly; since God is perfectly wise and cannot benefit Himself, He must act to benefit His creation. This passage presents that argument and introduces a debate about the extent of that benefit. Understanding this view is essential for grasping the Ash'ari refutation that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for the argument. Then read the literal translation to ensure you understand the meaning.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Analyze the logical structure
Break the argument into premises: (1) The wise acts only for a purpose. (2) The wise either benefits himself or others. (3) God cannot benefit Himself, so He benefits others. (4) Every act therefore contains benefit. Trace this reasoning.
Notice how the Mu'tazilites derive the conclusion from a general principle.
3
Note the internal disagreement
The passage then presents a disagreement: some Mu'tazilites say God must also do the most beneficial, while others say no, because there is no limit to what is more beneficial. Understand the two positions.
This shows that even within the school there was not full consensus.
4
Reflect on the purpose in the text
Remember that this passage is presented by an Ash'ari author for refutation. Ask yourself how an Ash'ari might respond to this argument.
This will prepare you for the later refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة : الحكيم لا يفعل فعلا إلا لحكمة وغرض، والفعل من غير غرض سفة وعبث، والحكيم من يفعل أحد أمرين؛ إما أن ينتفع أو ينفع غيره، ولما تقدس الرب تعالى عن الانتفاع تعين أنه إنما يفعل لينتفع غيره، فلا يخلو فعل من أفعاله من صلاح، ثم الأصلح هل تجب رعايته؟ قال بعضهم: تجب كرعاية الصلاح، وقال بعضهم لا تجب؛ إذ الأصلح لا نهاية له، فلا أصلح إلا وفوقه ما هو أصلح منه، ولهم كلام في اللطف وتردد في وجوب ذلك.
And the Mu'tazilites said: The wise does not do an act except for a wisdom and purpose, and acting without purpose is folly and frivolity. The wise does one of two things: either he benefits himself or benefits others. Since the Lord Most High is transcendent above deriving benefit, it is determined that He only acts to benefit others. So no act of His is devoid of benefit (ṣalāḥ). Then, regarding the most beneficial (aṣlaḥ): is observance of it obligatory? Some of them said: It is obligatory like observance of benefit. And some of them said: It is not obligatory, because the most beneficial has no limit; there is no most beneficial except that above it is what is more beneficial. And they have discourse on grace (luṭf) and hesitation regarding its obligation.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite argument. The author first states their premise: a wise agent (ḥakīm) does not act without purpose; purposeless action is foolish. The wise agent either seeks benefit for himself or for others. Since God is transcendent above receiving benefit, it follows that He acts to benefit others. Therefore, every divine act must contain some benefit (ṣalāḥ) for creatures. The Mu'tazilites then debate whether the most beneficial (aṣlaḥ) is obligatory upon God. Some say it is obligatory just as benefit is obligatory; others say it is not, because there is no limit to what is more beneficial—for any supposed most beneficial, there could be something more beneficial. The passage also mentions their discussion on grace (luṭf) and their hesitation on its obligation. Note: the author is reporting these views for refutation, as the principle title indicates.

Key Terms
حكيم
غرض
صلاح
أصلح
لطف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the Ash'ari author agrees with the Mu'tazilite position.
Correction: This passage is presented for refutation; the author does not endorse the Mu'tazilite view.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilites, why must every divine act contain benefit?
A) Because God is wise and cannot act foolishly
B) Because God needs to benefit Himself
C) Because creation demands it
D) Because prophets said so

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites argued that God's wisdom necessitates that every act benefits creatures, though they disagreed on whether God must always do the most beneficial act.

The Mu'tazilite View: God Acts for a Purpose
Mu'tazilites establish God acts for the benefit of others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that God, as a wise agent, must act for a purpose that benefits His creatures.

Core Idea
A wise agent acts for a purpose; God is wise, so He acts for our benefit.

The Mu'tazilites begin their discussion of divine action with a general principle: a wise person does nothing without a purpose. Since acting without purpose is foolish, and God is wise, His actions must have meaning. In this lesson, we examine how they apply this principle to God, concluding that every divine act contains benefit (salah) for creation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of a wise agent
The passage states that a wise person acts only for a wisdom and purpose. Recognize that acting without purpose is considered foolish.
This is a rational premise.
2
Identify the two options for a wise agent
A wise agent either benefits himself or benefits others. Notice that these are the only possibilities given.
3
Apply to God
Since God is transcendent and does not seek personal benefit, He must act to benefit others. Trace the logic: God is wise -> He acts for a purpose -> not for Himself -> therefore for others.
This is key to Mu'tazilite theology.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Every act of God contains benefit (salah) for His creatures. This sets the stage for the debate about the most beneficial (al-Aslah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة : الحكيم لا يفعل فعلا إلا لحكمة وغرض، والفعل من غير غرض سفه وعبث والحكيم من يفعل أحد أمرين ؛ إما أن ينتفع أو ينفع غيره ، ولما تقدس الرب تعالى عن الانتفاع تعين أنه إنما يفعل لينتفع غيره فلا يخلو فعل من أفعاله من صلاح ثم الأصلح هل تجب رعايته ؟ قال بعضهم: تجب كرعاية الصلاح وقال بعضهم لا تجب ؛ إذ الأصلح لا نهاية له، فلا أصلح إلا وفوقه ما هو أصلح منه ، ولهم كلام في اللطف وتردد في وجوب ذلك.
And the Mu'tazilites said: The wise person does not perform an act except for a wisdom and purpose, and an act without purpose is foolishness and frivolity. The wise person does one of two things: either he benefits himself or benefits others. Since the Lord Almighty is exalted above benefiting Himself, it is determined that He only acts to benefit others. So not one of His acts is devoid of benefit (salah). Then, regarding the most beneficial (al-aslah): is its observance obligatory? Some of them said: it is obligatory like the observance of benefit (al-salah). And some of them said: it is not obligatory, because al-aslah has no end; there is no most beneficial except that there is something more beneficial above it. And they have discussion about divine grace (al-lutf) and hesitation concerning its obligation.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite view on divine action. It begins with a general principle: a wise agent acts only for a purpose; acting without purpose is foolish. The wise agent does one of two beneficial actions: either he benefits himself or others. Since God is transcendent and does not benefit Himself, He must act to benefit others. Therefore, every act of God contains benefit (salah) for His creatures. The passage then introduces a disputed point among the Mu'tazilites: is God obligated to choose the most beneficial (al-aslah) for His creatures? Some say yes, just as He is obligated to provide benefit; others say no, because there is no ultimate limit to what is most beneficial—for any given good, a better one can be conceived, so obligation would be impossible. Finally, the passage mentions their discussion about 'lutf' (divine grace) and their hesitation on whether it is obligatory. This shows internal diversity within the Mu'tazilite school.

Key Terms
غرض
حكيم
صلاح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's actions are arbitrary and without purpose.
Correction: The Mu'tazilites argue that as a wise agent, God must act for a purpose that benefits His creatures.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilites, why must God's actions benefit others?
A) Because God is imperfect without doing so.
B) Because a wise agent acts for a purpose, and God cannot benefit Himself.
C) Because the Quran explicitly states so.
D) Because of consensus.

Key Takeaway
God's wisdom necessitates that every divine act serves a purpose and benefits His creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Mu'tazilite Debate on al-Aslah and al-Lutf
Mu'tazilites debate the obligation of al-Aslah and discuss al-lutf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the internal Mu'tazilite debate about whether God is obligated to choose the most beneficial (al-Aslah) for His creatures, and their discussion of divine grace (al-lutf).

Core Idea
Some Mu'tazilites say al-Aslah is obligatory; others say it's not due to boundless degrees of benefit.

After establishing that every divine act contains benefit, the Mu'tazilites differed on whether God must choose the most beneficial option (al-Aslah). This lesson explores their arguments and also touches on the concept of divine grace (lutf). The diversity of opinions within the school shows the complexity of their theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions on al-Aslah
The passage notes that some Mu'tazilites consider al-Aslah obligatory, while others disagree. Read carefully to understand the reasoning behind each.
2
Understand the objection to obligation
The argument against obligation is that there is no ultimate limit to what is most beneficial; for any given good, a better can be conceived, so obligation would be impossible.
This reflects a concern about infinite regress.
3
Consider the mention of al-lutf
The passage ends with discussion of divine grace (lutf) and hesitation about its obligation. Note that lutf is an additional concept related to helping faith.
This shows further internal debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة : الحكيم لا يفعل فعلا إلا لحكمة وغرض، والفعل من غير غرض سفه وعبث والحكيم من يفعل أحد أمرين ؛ إما أن ينتفع أو ينفع غيره ، ولما تقدس الرب تعالى عن الانتفاع تعين أنه إنما يفعل لينتفع غيره فلا يخلو فعل من أفعاله من صلاح ثم الأصلح هل تجب رعايته ؟ قال بعضهم: تجب كرعاية الصلاح وقال بعضهم لا تجب ؛ إذ الأصلح لا نهاية له، فلا أصلح إلا وفوقه ما هو أصلح منه ، ولهم كلام في اللطف وتردد في وجوب ذلك.
And the Mu'tazilites said: The wise person does not perform an act except for a wisdom and purpose, and an act without purpose is foolishness and frivolity. The wise person does one of two things: either he benefits himself or benefits others. Since the Lord Almighty is exalted above benefiting Himself, it is determined that He only acts to benefit others. So not one of His acts is devoid of benefit (salah). Then, regarding the most beneficial (al-aslah): is its observance obligatory? Some of them said: it is obligatory like the observance of benefit (al-salah). And some of them said: it is not obligatory, because al-aslah has no end; there is no most beneficial except that there is something more beneficial above it. And they have discussion about divine grace (al-lutf) and hesitation concerning its obligation.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite view on divine action. It begins with a general principle: a wise agent acts only for a purpose; acting without purpose is foolish. The wise agent does one of two beneficial actions: either he benefits himself or others. Since God is transcendent and does not benefit Himself, He must act to benefit others. Therefore, every act of God contains benefit (salah) for His creatures. The passage then introduces a disputed point among the Mu'tazilites: is God obligated to choose the most beneficial (al-aslah) for His creatures? Some say yes, just as He is obligated to provide benefit; others say no, because there is no ultimate limit to what is most beneficial—for any given good, a better one can be conceived, so obligation would be impossible. Finally, the passage mentions their discussion about 'lutf' (divine grace) and their hesitation on whether it is obligatory. This shows internal diversity within the Mu'tazilite school.

Key Terms
أصلح
لطف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazilites agreed that God must provide the most beneficial.
Correction: There was a significant disagreement; some denied its obligation due to the unbounded nature of benefits.

Checkpoint
Why do some Mu'tazilites reject the obligation of al-Aslah?
A) Because it would contradict God's mercy.
B) Because al-Aslah has no end; there is always a more beneficial option.
C) Because the Quran does not mention it.
D) Because it would limit God's freedom.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites disagreed on whether God must provide the most beneficial; the objection highlights the conceptual problem of an infinite hierarchy of goodness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek the 'best' in your life, do you ever feel trapped in never being satisfied? What does this lesson's argument about the endless hierarchy of benefit reveal about your own desires? Will you today thank God for what you have rather than resent what could be better?
?
The Mu'tazilites hesitated about whether God must grant grace. Do you ever assume that God owes you ease in obedience—that He must make faith easy for you? Where in your life have you felt entitled to divine help, and what does this lesson teach you about God's sovereignty over giving or withholding?
?
If you truly believe that God is not obligated to give you the absolute best, how does that change your response when things don't go your way? Can you still trust His wisdom without believing He owes you the optimal outcome? What would it look like to surrender that expectation today?
