Mu'tazilite Views on Divine Beneficence: Two Schools and Their Proof
Shahrastani presents two Mu'tazilite schools on divine beneficence and their shared proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two Mu'tazilite positions on the obligation of divine beneficence (ṣalāḥ and aṣlaḥ) and the rational proof they share.

Core Idea
For Mu'tazilites, God's wisdom necessitates observing the most beneficial for creation, but they differ on when this obligation begins.

This passage from Shahrastani introduces the Mu'tazilite doctrine of divine beneficence. Two schools—Baghdad and Basra—agree that God must act for the best, but they disagree on whether this obligation holds from the beginning of creation or only after rational beings exist. Their common proof is grounded in God's wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's presentation of the two schools come through.
Notice that the passage ends abruptly; the argument continues on the next page.
2
Identify the Baghdad position
The Baghdad school holds that it is obligatory in wisdom to create the world and capable beings, then to maximize their well-being in every way. Even eternal punishment is beneficial for the damned.
Consider why they think hellfire is 'most beneficial' for the inhabitants.
3
Identify the Basra position
The Basra school holds that initial creation is a free favor without obligation, but once God creates rational beings and imposes obligations, He must remove all impediments and observe the most beneficial.
Note the shift: obligation begins only after creation and taklīf.
4
Examine the common proof
Both schools argue from God's wisdom: the wise Maker does not act in a way that invites questioning; He removes impediments, charges within capacity, and provides recompense. Thus all acts are beneficial and most beneficial.
This proof ties together the two positions despite their differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة : نحن على طريقين في وجوب رعاية الصلاح والأصلح، فشيوخنا من بغداد حكموا بأن الواجب في الحكمة لخلق العالم، وخلق من يكون قابلا للتكليف، ثم استصلاح حاله بأقصى ما يقدر من إكمال العقل والأقدار على النظر والفعل وإظهار الآيات وإزاحة العلل وكل ما ينال العبد في الحال والمال من البأساء والضراء والفقر والغنى والمرض والصحة والحياة والموت والثواب والعقاب فهو صلاح له حتى تخليد أهل النار في النار صلاح لهم وأصلح، فإنهم لو أخرجوا منها لعادوا لما نهوا عنه، وصاروا إلى شر من الأول، وشيوخنا من البصرة صاروا إلى أن ابتداء الخلق تفضل وإنعام من الله تعالى من غير إيجاب عليه، لكنه إذا خلق العقلاء وكلفهم وجب عليه إزاحة عللهم من كل وجه، ورعاية الصلاح والأصلح في حقهم بأتم وجه وأبلغ غاية. قالوا: والدليل على المذهبين أن الصانع حكيم، والحكيم لا يفعل فعلا يتوجه عليه سؤال ويلزم حجة، بل يزيح العلل كلها فلا يكلف نفسا إلا وسعها، ولا يتحقق الوسع إلا بإكمال العقل والإقدار على الفعل، ولا يتم الغرض من الفعل إلا بإثبات الجزاء ( وَلِتُجْزَى كُلُّ نَفْسٍ بِمَا كَسَبَتْ ) فأصل التخليق والتكليف صلاح والجزاء صلاح وأبلغ ما يمكن في كل صلاح هو الأصلح، وزيادات الدواعي والصوارف والبواعث والزواجر في الشرع، وتقدير ألطاف بعضها خفي وبعضها جلي، فأفعال الله تعالى اليوم لا تخلو من صلاح وأصلح ولطف، وأفعال الله تعالى غدًا على سبيل الجزاء؛ إما ثواب أو عوض أو تفضل، فالصلاح ضد
The Mu'tazilites said: We are on two ways regarding the obligation of observing the beneficial and the most beneficial. Our elders from Baghdad ruled that what is obligatory in wisdom is: to create the world, and to create one capable of being charged, then to seek his well-being to the utmost extent possible by completing the intellect and enabling him to consider and act, and manifesting signs, and removing impediments, and everything that befalls the servant in present circumstances and property of hardship, harm, poverty, wealth, illness, health, life, death, reward, and punishment is beneficial for him, even the eternal abiding of the people of the Fire in the Fire is beneficial and most beneficial for them, because if they were brought out of it they would return to what they were forbidden and become worse than before. And our elders from Basra held that the beginning of creation is a favor and bounty from God Almighty without any obligation upon Him, but when He creates the rational beings and charges them, it becomes obligatory upon Him to remove their impediments from every aspect and to observe the beneficial and the most beneficial in their case in the most complete manner and farthest reach. They said: The proof for both schools is that the Maker is wise, and the wise does not do an act that would be subject to a question and necessitate an argument, but rather removes all impediments, so He does not charge a soul except within its capacity, and capacity is not realized except by completing the intellect and enabling action, and the purpose of the act is not achieved except by establishing recompense (and that every soul may be recompensed for what it earned). So the origin of creation and charging is beneficial, and the recompense is beneficial, and the utmost possible in every benefit is the most beneficial, and the additions of motives, deterrents, incentives, and preventives in the Law, and the estimation of subtle graces some of which are hidden and some manifest. So the actions of God Almighty today are not free from benefit, most beneficial, and grace, and the actions of God Almighty tomorrow in the way of recompense: either reward, compensation, or bounty. So benefit is opposite...
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite doctrine of divine beneficence (ṣalāḥ and aṣlaḥ) as held by two schools. The author reports the positions verbatim. The Baghdad school holds that it is obligatory in wisdom for God to create the world and beings capable of obligation, and then to provide for their well-being to the maximum degree. Everything that happens—good and ill—is beneficial for the servant, even eternal punishment in Hell, because if the damned were taken out they would sin again and become worse. The Basra school, on the other hand, asserts that the initial creation is a free favor and bounty without obligation, but once God creates rational beings and imposes obligations, He must remove all impediments and observe the most beneficial for them. The common proof is that the Maker is wise, and a wise agent does not act in a way that invites questioning or necessitates argument; rather, He removes impediments, charges within capacity, and provides recompense. Therefore, from the beginning of creation to the final recompense, all acts are beneficial and the most beneficial, including subtle graces. The passage ends abruptly mid-sentence, likely continuing on the next page.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Mu'tazilites uniformly hold that God must do the best in all circumstances.
Correction: In fact, the Basra school says the initial creation is a free grace without obligation; the obligation to observe the most beneficial begins only after God creates rational beings and imposes obligations.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Two Mu'tazila Schools on al-Salah wa al-Aslah
Two Mu'tazila schools on al-salah wa al-aslah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two Mu'tazila positions on whether God is obligated to do what is best and most beneficial (al-salah wa al-aslah) and how the Baghdad and Basra schools differ.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila disagreed among themselves on God's obligation to create the best.

This passage presents two schools within Mu'tazila theology concerning the question of whether God must always act in the best and most beneficial way for His creatures. The Baghdad school holds that from the very beginning, God is obligated to create the world and ensure optimal conditions, even for the inhabitants of Hell. The Basra school, however, distinguishes between the initial creation—which is a free favor—and the subsequent care of rational beings after obligation is imposed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two schools
Read the Arabic and English translation carefully. Note that the Mu'tazila are presenting two distinct views from their elders in Baghdad and Basra.
The passage begins with 'قالت المعتزلة : نحن على طريقين'.
2
Understand the Baghdad position
The Baghdad school obligates God to do what is best in creating, charging with obligations, and even in punishment. Everything that happens is al-salah (benefit) for the individual.
3
Understand the Basra position
The Basra school sees initial creation as a favor without obligation, but after creating rational beings and imposing taklif, God must then observe al-salah wa al-aslah and remove impediments.
This is a subtle but important difference.
4
Compare the two positions
Ask yourself: How do the two schools differ on the starting point of obligation? What does each consider wajib (obligatory) upon God?
This comparison clarifies the doctrinal nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة : نحن على طريقين في وجوب رعاية الصلاح والأصلح، فشيوخنا من بغداد حكموا بأن الواجب في الحكمة لخلق العالم، وخلق من يكون قابلا للتكليف ، ثم استصلاح حاله بأقصى ما يقدر من إكمال العقل والأقدار على النظر والفعل وإظهار الآيات وإزاحة العلل وكل ما ينال العبد في الحال والمال من البأساء والضراء والفقر والغنى والمرض ،والصحة والحياة والموت والثواب والعقاب فهو صلاح له حتى تخليد أهل النار في النار صلاح لهم وأصلح، فإنهم لو أخرجوا منها لعادوا لما نهوا عنه، وصاروا إلى شر من الأول، وشيوخنا من البصرة صاروا إلى أن ابتداء الخلق تفضل وإنعام من الله تعالى من غير إيجاب عليه، لكنه إذا خلق العقلاء وكلفهم وجب عليه إزاحة عللهم من كل وجه، ورعاية الصلاح والأصلح في حقهم بأتم وجه وأبلغ غاية.
The Mu'tazila said: We are on two paths regarding the obligation of observing the best and most beneficial (al-salah wa al-aslah). Our elders from Baghdad ruled that what is obligatory in wisdom is to create the world, and to create one who is capable of being charged with obligations (taklif), then to make his condition best by the utmost possible completion of intellect and powers for speculation and action, and the manifestation of miracles, and the removal of impediments, and everything that befalls the servant in present circumstances and wealth of affliction and adversity, poverty and wealth, illness and health, life and death, reward and punishment — all that is benefit (salah) for him, even the eternal abiding of the people of Hellfire in the Fire is benefit for them and most beneficial (aslah), for if they were brought out of it they would return to what they were forbidden and become worse than before. And our elders from Basra held that the beginning of creation is a favor (tafaddul) and grace (in'am) from God, may He be exalted, without any obligation upon Him, but when He creates rational beings and charges them with obligations, it becomes obligatory upon Him to remove their impediments from every aspect, and to observe the best and most beneficial regarding them in the most complete manner and the utmost degree.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage presents the two positions of the Mu'tazila on the issue of al-salah wa al-aslah (the best and most beneficial). It is a classification passage. The author first introduces the two schools: Baghdad and Basra. The Baghdad school obligates God to always do what is best and most beneficial from the very beginning, including creating the world, choosing those capable of taklif, and ensuring all circumstances, even punishment, are for benefit. They argue that eternal punishment is itself beneficial for the damned because if they were released they would sin again. The Basra school differs: the initial creation is a gratuitous favor (tafaddul) and grace (in'am) from God without obligation, but once God creates rational beings and charges them with obligations, then it becomes obligatory (wajib) upon Him to remove impediments and observe the best and most beneficial for them to the fullest extent. The key difference is whether obligation applies to the initial act of creation and taklif or only to subsequent care.

Key Terms
صلاح
أصلح
تكليف
تفضل
إنعام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazila obligate God to do the best in all actions without exception.
Correction: The Basra school holds that the initial creation is a favor without obligation; obligation begins only after creating rational beings and imposing taklif.

Checkpoint
What is the difference between the Baghdad and Basra schools of Mu'tazila on the obligation of al-salah wa al-aslah?
A) Baghdad obligates initial creation and taklif as necessary; Basra holds initial creation is a favor, but obligation arises after taklif.
B) Both schools maintain absolute obligation on God to do the best in every action.
C) Basra obligates initial creation; Baghdad obligates after taklif.
D) Neither school accepts any obligation on God.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila split on whether God must always do the best from the start (Baghdad) or only after creating and obligating rational beings (Basra).

?

Refutation of Mu'tazila Doctrine as Anthropomorphism
Author refutes Mu'tazila doctrine, accusing them of anthropomorphism in actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari critique that the Mu'tazila's method of reasoning about God's actions is a form of anthropomorphism (tashbih).

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's reasoning about God's actions is actually a form of anthropomorphism.

This passage presents a sharp Ash'ari critique of the Mu'tazila. The author accuses them of having no real basis for their doctrine of al-salah wa al-aslah except for flawed analogy. The lesson explores why this accusation is serious and how it connects to the core issue of tashbih (anthropomorphism).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's direct accusation sink in.
The author is concise and severe in tone.
2
Identify the two missing bases
The author lists two things the Mu'tazila lack: a rational basis (mustanad ʿaqlī) and scriptural support (mustarawḥ sharʿī). Notice that only custom and exaggeration remain.
3
Understand 'custom' and 'analogy of the unseen'
The Mu'tazila rely on custom (ʿāda) in the witnessed world and then apply analogy to God. Ask yourself: why is this problematic for the author?
Consider that God's actions are not bound by human customs.
4
Reflect on the accusation of anthropomorphism
The author explicitly calls them 'anthropomorphists in actions' (mushabbiha fī al-afʿāl). Think about how imposing human moral obligations on God is likening Him to creation.
This is a classic Ashʿarī critique of Muʿtazilī rationalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس للمعتزلة فيما ذكروه مستند عقلي ولا مستروح شرعي إلا مجرد اعتبار العادة في الشاهد وتشنيعا معقولا، ثم اعتبار الغائب بالشاهد وهم في الحقيقة مشبهة في الأفعال.
And the Mu'tazila have no rational basis nor scriptural support for what they mentioned, except merely consideration of custom in the witnessed world and an intellectual exaggeration, then analogy of the unseen from the witnessed, and they are in reality anthropomorphists (mushabbiha) in actions.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is the author's refutation of the Mu'tazila's entire doctrine of al-salah wa al-aslah. It is a critique and rebuttal passage. The author states that the Mu'tazila have no rational (ʿaqlī) basis nor any scriptural (sharʿī) support for their claims. Their only method is mere consideration of custom (ʿāda) in the witnessed world (shāhid) and an intellectual exaggeration (tashnīʿ maʿqūl). Then they apply analogy of the unseen (ghāʾib) from the witnessed (shāhid) – a method the author considers invalid. Consequently, the author levels a serious accusation: that the Mu'tazila are in reality anthropomorphists (mushabbiha) in actions (fī al-afʿāl). This means they liken God's actions to human actions by imposing human moral obligations (wujūb al-ṣalāḥ) on God, which is a form of tashbīh (anthropomorphism). This is a classic Ashʿarī critique of Muʿtazilī rationalism.

Key Terms
مستند عقلي
مستروح شرعي
اعتبار العادة
الغائب بالشاهد
مشبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila rely solely on reason, making their doctrine rational and sound.
Correction: The author argues that their reason is not sound; it is based on flawed analogy from human custom to God, which is a form of anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint
What does the author accuse the Mu'tazila of in their method of reasoning?
A) Fatalism
B) Anthropomorphism in actions
C) Denial of divine attributes
D) Incoherence of the Quran

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's method of analogizing God's actions to human actions is a form of anthropomorphism rejected by Ash'ari theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazila thought they were defending God's justice, but they fell into a subtle form of shirk in actions. Is there an area of your life where you expect God to behave as you would if you were in His place? What would it cost you to let go of that expectation and simply trust His decree?
?
This lesson warns that reasoning from human experience to God is dangerous. When you face a trial, how often do you try to understand it by human logic rather than turning to revelation? Will you make a commitment today to stop judging God's actions by your limited perspective and instead submit wholly to His command?

Fifth Ash'ari Objection: Iblis and the Prophet's Death
Ash'ari objection using Iblis and Prophet's death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari objection using Iblis and the Prophet's death exposes an inconsistency in the Mu'tazila definition of 'soundness' (صلاح).

Core Idea
Consistency demands that divine actions be judged by God's wisdom, not human benefit.

The Ash'ari objection continues by testing the Mu'tazila principle on specific, concrete cases. If 'soundness' means maximising benefit for creatures, then why did God let Iblis live (causing immense harm) but take the Prophet's life (ending immense benefit)? This passage sets up the dilemma to show that the Mu'tazila cannot consistently apply their own criterion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the two cases presented and the rhetorical question at the end.
The passage cuts off mid-sentence; the argument is incomplete.
2
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two specific cases: the postponement of Iblis (Satan) and the death of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him). For each, it asks whether the action was 'soundness' (صلاح) or 'corruption' (فساد).
3
Understand the dilemma
The objection implies a contradiction: if Iblis's survival is considered 'sound' despite leading people astray, then why was the Prophet, who brought great benefit, not kept alive? This challenges the Mu'tazila principle that God must always do what is best for creatures.
4
Reflect on the target
The target is the Mu'tazila's definition of 'soundness' as what maximises benefit for creatures. The objection shows that this definition cannot account for these real divine actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها قولهم: نحن فرضنا الكلام في إنظار إبليس وإمهاله، أكان صلاحا له وللخلق أم كان فسادا؟ وفي إماتة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم هل كان صلاحا له وللخلق أم فسادا؟ فإن كان تبقية إبليس صلاحا مع إضلاله الخلق فهلا كان تبقية
And among them their saying: We have discussed the postponement and delay of Iblis, was it soundness for him and for creation or was it corruption? And regarding the death of the Prophet, peace be upon him, was it soundness for him and for creation or corruption? For if the survival of Iblis is soundness despite his leading creation astray, then why was not the survival
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This objection raises specific cases to test the Mu‘tazila principle. The Ash‘ariyya ask: consider the postponement of Iblis (Satan) – was that sound for him and for creation or corrupt? Similarly, consider the death of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) – was that sound or corrupt? If the survival of Iblis is considered 'sound' despite his leading people astray, then why was the Prophet taken while he was beneficial? The objection implies inconsistency: if soundness is defined by benefit to creatures, then Iblis's continued existence harms many while the Prophet's death ends great benefit. The text cuts off before completing the argument, but the point is clear that the Mu‘tazila cannot consistently apply their own criterion.

Key Terms
إنظار
إبليس
إماتة
النبي
صلاح
فساد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this objection questions God's wisdom or actions.
Correction: The objection does not question God's actions; it challenges the Mu'tazila's human-centric definition of 'soundness' by showing it leads to contradictions.

Checkpoint
What two cases are presented in this objection?
A) The creation of angels and the death of Iblis
B) The postponement of Iblis and the death of the Prophet
C) The birth of the Prophet and the creation of Hell
D) The punishment of sinners and the reward of believers

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari objection uses concrete cases to show that the Mu'tazila definition of 'soundness' leads to contradictions and is therefore untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read about Iblis being allowed to continue leading people astray, does that disturb your sense of how a just and loving God should act? How do you reconcile that with your personal trust in God's wisdom right now?
?
Think of a situation in your own life where God's plan seemed the opposite of what was 'best' for you or others. Does this lesson challenge you to question your assumptions about what is truly beneficial? Will you commit to surrendering your definition of 'good' to God's, even when it hurts?
?
The Prophet's death is presented as a case where God's action seems contrary to human benefit. Are you willing to accept that God's decisions for your life—including losses and disappointments—are rooted in a wisdom that may not align with your understanding? What is one specific area where you need to stop measuring God's actions by your own measure of 'soundness'?

First Objection: Entailment of Obligating al-Salah on Humans
The author presents a necessary entailment from the Ash'ari obligation of al-salah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection (luzum) against the Ash'ari doctrine of God's obligation to do what is best, and why the analogy between God and humans leads to inconsistency.

Core Idea
Obligating God to do what's best implies humans must also be obligated — an inconsistency.

This lesson examines a key objection raised against the Ash'ari view that God must do what is best (al-salah wa al-aslah). The objection argues that if this obligation holds for God, then by analogy it should also hold for humans. Yet the Ash'aris do not hold humans to that standard, exposing a logical flaw.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: the author first states the entailment, then explains the analogical reasoning, and finally points out the inconsistency.
The author uses the phrase 'لازم' (necessary entailment) to signal a logical consequence.
2
Identify the analogical reasoning
The objection uses qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogizing the unseen to the witnessed). The Ash'ari position on God (unseen) is used to derive a rule for humans (witnessed).
This analogical method is common in kalam.
3
Grasp the inconsistency
The author notes that by consensus (bil-ittifaq) humans are not obligated to observe al-salah and al-aslah in all actions. This contradicts the entailment. Additionally, the objection highlights that if hardship (nasb) could break the analogy, it would also break the principle of al-salah itself.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فألزمت الأشعرية عليهم التزامات منها قولهم: إذا أوجبتم على الله تعالى رعاية الصلاح والأصلح في أفعاله، فيجب أن توجبوا علينا رعاية الصلاح والأصلح في أفعالنا، حتى يصح اعتبار الغائب بالشاهد، ولم يجب علينا رعايتهما بالاتفاق إلا بقدر ما، والتعرض للنصب والتعب، والنصب لو كان فاصلا الشاهد والغائب لكان فاصلا في أصل الصلاح.
The Ash'aris are compelled to accept entailments, among them their statement: If you make it obligatory upon God Almighty to observe what is best and most beneficial (al-salah wa al-aslah) in His actions, then you must make it obligatory upon us to observe what is best and most beneficial in our actions, so that the unseen (al-gha'ib) can be analogized to the witnessed (al-shahid). But we are not obliged to observe them by consensus except to a certain extent, and we are exposed to hardship and fatigue, and if hardship were a separator between the witnessed and the unseen, it would be a separator in the very principle of what is best.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection (luzum) against the Ash'ari doctrine that God is obligated to do what is best (al-salah wa al-aslah) for His creation. The author argues that if this obligation holds, then by analogy (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid) humans should also be obligated to do what is best in all actions. However, the Ash'aris do not hold this, which is inconsistent. Moreover, the obligation would expose humans to hardship (nasb) and fatigue (ta'b), which contradicts the purpose of al-salah. If hardship were a valid basis to distinguish between the unseen (God) and the witnessed (humans), it would negate the very principle of al-salah itself.

Key Terms
الصلاح
الأصلح
الغائب
الشاهد
اعتبار
النصب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Ash'ari view is that only God is obligated to do what is best, and humans are entirely free from any such obligation.
Correction: The objection shows that this view is inconsistent: if the obligation is based on a principle of goodness that applies universally, it should apply to humans too; otherwise the principle is arbitrary.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue that the Ash'ari doctrine of al-salah entails for human actions?
A) Humans must also observe al-salah and al-aslah in all their actions.
B) Humans are released from any obligation regarding al-salah.
C) God's actions are not analogous to human actions at all.
D) Hardship proves that al-salah is not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The first objection shows that applying the principle of al-salah to God but not to humans is inconsistent, especially given the problem of hardship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused yourself from a difficult duty because it seemed too hard, while expecting God to make things easy for you? What does this passage reveal about that double standard? Repent and commit to consistency in what you expect from yourself and from God.
?
When you consider your own life, do you hold others to a standard you do not meet yourself? The author uses logical analogy to expose inconsistency. Ask yourself: In which relationships am I applying a heavier burden on others than on myself? Write down one area and change it today.
?
The Ash'aris obligate God to always choose the best for creation, but they do not obligate humans. Do you ever assume that God should always give you what is best, while you make choices that are far from best for yourself? Reflect on this asymmetry and resolve to align your actions with what you expect from God.

Second Objection: Supererogatory Acts Would Be Obligatory
The author presents a concise objection: supererogatory acts are beneficial, so they should be obligatory, contradicting the Ash'ari position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the Ash'ari principle that obligation is based on benefit (salah), specifically that supererogatory acts would then be obligatory like the obligatory acts.

Core Idea
If supererogatory acts are beneficial, they must be obligatory — but they are not, so the Ash'ari definition fails.

This lesson introduces a sharp objection against the Ash'ari position that 'obligation' is defined by benefit (salah). The objection targets supererogatory acts of devotion (nawafil), which are widely considered beneficial but not obligatory. If the Ash'ari principle is correct, these acts should be obligatory — a conclusion that is not accepted. Let's examine the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and its English translation. Notice the concise argument.
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument is: supererogatory acts are beneficial (premise), therefore they should be obligatory (conclusion). The objection uses the Ash'ari's own definition against them.
3
Consider the broader implication
If the Ash'ari definition of obligation leads to supererogatory acts becoming obligatory, then the definition must be flawed or incomplete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن القربات من النوافل صلاح فليجب وجوب الفرائض.
And among them is that supererogatory acts of devotion (al-qurubat min al-nawafil) are beneficial (salah), so they should be obligatory like the obligatory acts (al-fara'id).
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection. The author argues that if supererogatory acts of worship (nawafil) are considered beneficial (salah) according to the Ash'ari principle, then they should be as obligatory as the obligatory acts (fara'id). Since this is not the case, the Ash'ari position is inconsistent.

Key Terms
القربات
النوافل
الصلاح
الفرائض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection claims that all beneficial things are obligatory.
Correction: The objection does not claim that all beneficial things are obligatory, but that the Ash'ari criterion for obligation (salah) would improperly elevate supererogatory acts to obligatory status.

Checkpoint
What does the second objection conclude about supererogatory acts based on the Ash'ari view of salah?
A) They are not beneficial.
B) They should be obligatory like the fara'id.
C) They are optional despite being beneficial.
D) They are more beneficial than obligatory acts.

Key Takeaway
The second objection forces the Ash'ari to reconsider whether benefit (salah) alone can define obligation, since supererogatory acts are beneficial yet not obligatory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume that whatever benefits you in this life is a command from Allah? Does this objection expose an unexamined habit in your own reasoning about your religious duties? Will you now test your assumptions against revealed texts before declaring something obligatory?
?
When you perform a supererogatory prayer or fast, does it ever feel 'optional' or less weighty? This objection shows that if benefit alone defined obligation, even those acts would be demanded of you. Do you take them for granted, or do you perform them with the same urgency as obligatory acts?
?
The Ash'ari definition leads to an absurdity. But what about your own personal definition of what is 'important' in Islam? Have you elevated beneficial worldly matters to the status of obligations, while neglecting supererogatory acts that might be more beneficial for your afterlife? What will you change today?

Third Objection: Eternal Punishment as Best for the Damned?
The author presents an objection and offers counterarguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection to the Ash'ari claim that God always does what is best for His creatures, focusing on the case of eternal punishment.

Core Idea
If God must do what's best, then Hell's punishment must be best for the damned—an implausible conclusion.

This lesson presents a key objection raised against the Ash'ari doctrine of al-aslah (God's doing the best). The objection argues that if God always does what is most beneficial for His creatures, then the eternal punishment of the damned in Hell must itself be beneficial for them. The author counters by offering alternative divine actions that would be even more beneficial, thus challenging the consistency of the Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the original Arabic and its literal English translation. Notice the structure: it first states the objection, then presents the counterargument.
2
Identify the objection's logical flow
The objection says: if God always does what is best (aslah) for creatures, then eternal punishment must be beneficial (salah) for the damned. Why would that be a problem? Consider the implications.
The Ash'ari rejoinder about returning to sin is dismissed.
3
Examine the alternative scenarios
The author lists three things God could do that would be more beneficial: cause death, deprive of intellect, or forgive entirely. Understand why each is considered 'more beneficial' (aslah).
This undermines the claim that eternal punishment is the best option.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها القضاء بأن خلود أهل النار في النار يجب أن يكون صلاحًا لهم، وقولهم بأنهم لو ردوا لعادوا لما نهوا عنه لا يغني فإن الله تعالى لو أماتهم أو سلب عقولهم وقطع عقابهم كان أصلح لهم، ولو غفر لهم ورحمهم وأخرجهم من النار؛ إذ لم يتضرر بكفرهم وعصيانهم كان أصلح لهم.
And among them is the judgment that the eternal stay of the inhabitants of the Fire in the Fire must be beneficial (salah) for them, and their statement that if they were returned they would return to what they were forbidden does not suffice, for if God had caused them to die or deprived them of their intellects and terminated their punishment, it would have been more beneficial (aslah) for them, and if He had forgiven them, had mercy on them, and removed them from the Fire—since He is not harmed by their disbelief and disobedience—it would have been more beneficial for them.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection. The author argues that if God always does what is best for His creatures, then the eternal punishment of the damned in Hell must be beneficial (salah) for them, which is counterintuitive. The Ash'ari response that the damned would revert to sin if sent back is insufficient, because God could instead annihilate them, deprive them of intellect, or forgive them entirely—all of which would be more beneficial (aslah). Thus the Ash'ari position fails.

Key Terms
الخلود
أهل النار
الصلاح
الأصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is that eternal punishment is unjust.
Correction: The objection is not about justice; it is about the logical consistency of the Ash'ari principle that God always does what is best for His creatures. The point is that the principle leads to the counterintuitive claim that eternal punishment is the best option for the damned.

Checkpoint
According to the third objection, what would be more beneficial for the damned than eternal punishment?
A) Returning them to the world.
B) Giving them another chance.
C) Death, deprivation of intellect, or forgiveness.
D) Increasing their punishment.

Key Takeaway
The third objection shows that the Ash'ari principle of al-aslah leads to an implausible conclusion regarding eternal punishment, because alternative divine actions would be more beneficial for the damned.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God always chooses what is best for you, would you ever fear His punishment? How does this lesson challenge your assumption about what is 'best' for you?
?
The objector says God could forgive the damned because He is not harmed by their sins. Are there sins in your life you assume are too great for God to forgive? What would it mean to take God's 'not being harmed' seriously?
?
If you were to stand before God and He offered you alternatives to eternal punishment—annihilation, loss of intellect, or forgiveness—which would you choose? Why does your answer reveal about your current state of faith?

The Objection from Gratitude
The author argues that if God's actions are obligatory, they deserve no thanks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection that if God's actions are obligatory, they cannot deserve gratitude, and the implications for the doctrine of divine obligation.

Core Idea
If God must act, His acts are debts, not gifts — no thanks for debts.

This lesson presents the fourth objection against the Ash'ari position that God is obligated to do what is best. The objection argues that if God's beneficial acts are required, they are like a debt He must pay, and a debtor merits no gratitude for paying what is owed. The objection draws an analogy with a servant's obedience and reward.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the analogy with debt repayment.
2
Explore the analogy
The passage compares God's obligatory actions to a servant's obligatory obedience. If a servant fulfills an obligation, their reward is a debt, not a favor. Similarly, if God's acts are obligatory, they are not gifts but debts, so He deserves no thanks.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the implication: if God's goodness is obligatory, then gratitude becomes meaningless. This challenges the idea that God is morally required to act in a certain way.
This is a key objection in the debate about divine obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن كل ما فعله الرب تعالى من الصلاح لو كان حتما عليه لما استوجب بفعل ما شكرًا أو حمدًا، فإنه في فعله قضى ما وجب عليه، وما استوجب عبد بطاعته ثوابًا وتفضيلا فإنه في طاعته قضى ما وجب عليه، ومن قضى دينه لم يستوجب شيئًا آخر.
And among them is that if every act of benefit (salah) done by the Lord Almighty were obligatory upon Him, then He would not deserve thanks or praise for doing it, for in doing it He fulfilled what was incumbent upon Him. And if a servant deserves reward and favor for his obedience, then in his obedience he has fulfilled what was incumbent upon him. And whoever pays his debt does not deserve anything else.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection. The author argues that if God is obligated to do what is best, then His actions become like a debt He must pay. In that case, He does not deserve gratitude (shukr) or praise (hamd) for them, because He is merely fulfilling an obligation. Similarly, if a servant's obedience is obligatory, then the reward for that obedience would also be a debt, not a favor. The analogy is drawn to a debtor who pays what he owes: he merits no additional thanks.

Key Terms
الصلاح
حتما
الشكر
الحمد
الثواب
الدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if God does good deeds, He always deserves gratitude regardless of whether they are obligatory or voluntary.
Correction: The objection shows that if the actions are obligatory, they are like repaying a debt, and one does not receive thanks for fulfilling an obligation. Only voluntary gifts merit gratitude.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth objection conclude about God's merit for thanks if His actions are obligatory?
A) He deserves more thanks.
B) He deserves no thanks or praise.
C) He deserves thanks only for voluntary acts.
D) It does not affect gratitude.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection contends that if God's actions are obligatory, they are like a repaid debt, and thus God would not deserve thanks or praise for them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you thank Allah for a blessing, do you ever feel deep down that He 'owed' it to you because of your righteousness? How does this passage expose that attitude as a failure to recognize His free generosity?
?
Imagine if every good deed you did was something you were forced to do — would you expect a reward for it? How does this reshape your view of the reward Allah promises for voluntary obedience?
?
If you truly believed that Allah's acts are obligatory, would your gratitude toward Him remain the same? What does your answer reveal about how you understand your relationship with your Creator?

Fifth Objection: Iblis vs. the Prophet
Author compares preservation of Iblis with death of the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fifth objection uses a comparison between Iblis and the Prophet to challenge the doctrine that God always does what is best (al-aslah).

Core Idea
Is preserving Satan better than preserving the Prophet?

The fifth objection presents a rhetorical dilemma: if God preserves Satan despite his misguidance, why would He take the life of the Prophet who guided humanity? This argument aims to show that the Ash'ari principle of always doing what is best leads to a contradiction. The passage directly compares two cases to force the opponent into an absurd conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the rhetorical question.
Notice the parallel structure: 'if preservation of Iblis is beneficial... then why not preservation of the Prophet?'
2
Identify the two cases being compared
Identify that the author compares the preservation (بقاء) of Iblis with the death (إماتة) of the Prophet, and questions the inconsistency in God's actions.
3
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
The objection assumes the opponent's principle (God always does what is best) and shows it leads to contradictory judgments. Ask yourself: why does this undermine the doctrine?
The key is that the comparison is not about the actual goodness of each event, but about the logical consequence of the doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها قولهم: نحن فرضنا الكلام في إنظار إبليس وإمهاله، أكان صلاحًا له وللخلق أم كان فسادًا؟ وفي إماتة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم هل كان صلاحًا له وللخلق أم فسادًا؟ فإن كان تبقية إبليس صلاحًا مع إضلاله الخلق فهلا كان تبقية النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم صلاحًا مع هدايته للخلق، وكيف صار الأمر بالضد من ذلك؟
And among them is their statement: We posit the discussion about the respite of Iblis and his delay—was it beneficial (salah) for him and for creation or was it corruption (fasad)? And regarding the death of the Prophet (peace be upon him)—was it beneficial for him and for creation or corruption? If the preservation of Iblis is beneficial despite his misguidance of creation, then why was the preservation of the Prophet (peace be upon him) not beneficial despite his guidance of creation? And how did the matter become the opposite?
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection. The author poses a rhetorical question: If God's preservation of Iblis (Satan) is considered beneficial despite Satan leading people astray, then preserving the Prophet Muhammad (who guides people) should be even more beneficial. Yet the Prophet died, while Iblis remains. This inconsistency undermines the Ash'ari claim that God always does what is best, because the opposite seems to occur.

Key Terms
إنظار إبليس
إمهاله
صلاح
فساد
تبقية
النبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection claims that Iblis's preservation is actually good.
Correction: The objection does not assert that Iblis's preservation is truly beneficial. It is a reductio ad absurdum: it assumes the opponent's principle and shows it leads to a contradiction, not that the premise is true.

Checkpoint
What comparison does the fifth objection make to challenge the Ash'ari view?
A) Comparing Iblis and the Prophet in terms of their benefits.
B) Comparing the death of a sinner and a believer.
C) Comparing different types of benefit.
D) Comparing God's treatment of angels and humans.

Key Takeaway
The fifth objection uses a comparison between Iblis and the Prophet to expose a logical contradiction in the doctrine of al-aslah.

The Harm of Duty with Foreknowledge of Loss
Author presents sixth objection using analogy of a father and son.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if God foreknows a person's damnation, then imposing duty cannot be for their benefit and is instead harmful.

Core Idea
Foreknowledge of loss makes duty an act of harm, not benefit.

This lesson presents the sixth objection against the Ash'ari position that duty (taklif) is for the servant's benefit. The author argues that if God knows a person will disbelieve and be damned if he reaches maturity, then imposing duty on him cannot be beneficial—rather, it would be harmful. A vivid analogy from everyday life illustrates the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
2
Identify the key premise
Identify the hidden premise: that the purpose of duty is to expose the believer to eternal reward. The objection challenges this by considering cases where the outcome is known to be loss.
The passage states 'if the Lord knows that if He had taken the servant before puberty... he would have been saved'.
3
Analyze the analogy
Study the father-and-son analogy. The author gives two scenarios: one with trade money, one with a sword. Ask yourself how these map onto God and the servant.
The analogy argues that such action would be unwise and even harmful.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that God must be considered to have 'acted badly'—a strong accusation against the Ash'ari view. Recognize that this does not deny foreknowledge but the claim of optimal benefit.
The objection is against the consistency of divine wisdom.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the analogy's point. Then reflect on how an Ash'ari might respond.
This prepares for the next lesson on the response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها قولكم إنكم حسبتم التكليف لتعريض المكلف للثواب الدائم، وإذا علم الرب أنه لو اخترم العبد قبل البلوغ وكمال العقل لكان ناجيا، ولو أمهله وسهل له النظر لعند وكفر وجحد، فكيف يستقيم أن يقال: أراد به الصلاح والأصلح؟ ومن المعلوم أن المقصود من التكليف عند القوم الصلاح والتعريض لا معنى الدرجات التي لا تنال إلا بالأعمال، فيجب أن يكون الرب مسيئًا للنظر لمن خلقه وأكمل عقله وكلفه مع العلم بأنه يهلك ويخسر، ولزم من حيث الحكمة أن يكون عن إرادته، والتعريض لمن هذه حاله بالنفع والثواب، وفي العادة كل من عرف من حال ولده أنه لو سافر واتجر في مال يعطيه لهلك وخسر لا يحسن من حيث العقل أن يبعثه لأجل التجارة ويعطيه المال لأجل الخسارة، ولو علم من ولده أنه لو أعطاه سيفا وسلاحًا ليقاتل عدوا من أعدائه لقتل نفسه ويبقى السلاح العدوه، لم يكن من الحكمة أن يعطيه السلاح ويبعثه للقاء عدوه البتة، بل لو فعل ذلك كان ساعيًا في هلاك ولده.
And among them is your statement that you considered the duty (taklif) to be for exposing the duty-bound to eternal reward. But if the Lord knows that if He had taken the servant before puberty and before the completion of intellect, he would have been saved, and if He had given him respite and facilitated reflection for him, he would have become stubborn, disbelieved, and denied, then how can it be consistent to say that He intended what is best and most beneficial (al-salah wal-aslah) for him? And it is known that the purpose of duty according to the [Ash'ari] people is benefit and exposure, not the meanings of degrees that are only attainable through deeds. So the Lord must be considered to have acted badly in regard to the one He created, completed his intellect, and imposed duty upon him while knowing that he will perish and lose. And it follows from the perspective of wisdom that it would be contrary to His will, and the exposure of one in this state to benefit and reward [is problematic]. And in custom, anyone who knows from his son's condition that if he traveled and traded with money he gave him, he would perish and lose, it is not intellectually good to send him for trade and give him money for loss. And if he knew from his son that if he gave him a sword and weapon to fight an enemy, he would kill himself and the weapon would remain with the enemy, it would not be wise to give him the weapon and send him to meet his enemy at all. Rather, if he did that, he would be contributing to his son's destruction.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection, which is the longest. The author argues that if God knows that a particular person will disbelieve and be damned if he reaches maturity and is given the duty (taklif), then imposing that duty on him cannot be beneficial (salah). Instead, it would be harmful, because the person would have been better off dying before puberty. The author uses an analogy: just as a father would not give his son money for a business that will ruin him, or give him weapons to fight an enemy that will result in his own death, so God, knowing the outcome, would not impose a duty that leads to damnation if He truly intended what is best. Thus the Ash'ari position is inconsistent with divine wisdom.

Key Terms
التكليف
التعريض
الثواب
الرب
الخسارة
الهلاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection denies God's foreknowledge or claims God does not know the future.
Correction: The objection does not deny foreknowledge; it argues that foreknowledge combined with the claim that duty is for the servant's best benefit is logically inconsistent.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth objection, what is the problem with imposing duty on someone God knows will be lost?
A) It contradicts God's mercy.
B) It is not beneficial but harmful, so the claim of benefit is false.
C) It proves God is not all-knowing.
D) It shows that duty is only for punishment.

Key Takeaway
The sixth objection exposes an inconsistency in the Ash'ari view: if God knows a person will be damned, then imposing duty is not beneficial but harmful, undermining the claim that duty is for the servant's best.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you knew that a specific action would lead you away from Allah, would you still do it? How does this lesson challenge your own assumptions about what is truly beneficial for you?
?
The author likens imposing duty on a person known to be damned to a father giving a sword to a son who will be killed. When have you felt that a trial you faced was like that sword—seeming to lead to loss? What does this passage demand you reconsider about Allah’s wisdom in your own trials?
?
Do you ever doubt that Allah's commands are for your benefit, especially when you see people around you falling into sin after being given guidance? How does this passage force you to examine the depth of your trust in divine wisdom, even when outcomes seem harmful?

The Seventh Objection: Divine Knowledge and Intending Benefit
The author concludes that divine knowledge prevents intending benefit for a known rebel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the seventh objection uses an analogy from sending a messenger to challenge the Ash'ari view that God intends benefit for all, given His foreknowledge of disbelief.

Core Idea
God's foreknowledge of failure logically precludes intending benefit.

This lesson explores the seventh objection against the Ash'ari position. The author draws a parallel between God sending a messenger He knows will disobey and God intending benefit for someone He knows will disbelieve. If foreknowledge prevents willing the messenger's delivery, it should also prevent willing benefit for the future disbeliever. This challenges the coherence of universal divine intent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first a premise about a messenger, then an application to a disbeliever.
The passage ends with a comma, suggesting the argument continues, but the core analogy is complete.
2
Identify the analogy
The author compares two cases: (1) God sends a messenger He knows will not deliver, (2) God intends benefit for someone He knows will disbelieve. In both, foreknowledge should prevent the intention.
This analogy is the heart of the objection.
3
Reflect on the logical force
Ask yourself: why does this analogy undermine the Ash'ari claim that God intends good for all? The objection argues that foreknowledge of failure makes intending benefit incoherent.
This is a key challenge in the debate over qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن مذهبهم: أن الرب تعالى لو علم أنه لو أرسل رسولا إلى خلقه وكلفه الأداء عنه مع علمه بأنه لا يؤدي فإن علمه به يصرفه عن إرادته الأداء عنه، فكذلك لو علم أنه يكفر ويهلك وجب أن يصرفه عن إرادته الخير والصلاح له،
And from their doctrine: that if the Lord Almighty knew that if He sent a messenger to His creation and charged him with delivering on His behalf while knowing that he would not deliver, then His knowledge of him would turn him away from willing the delivery from him. Similarly, if He knew that he would disbelieve and perish, it must turn Him away from willing good and benefit for him.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objection. The author draws an analogy: if God sends a messenger whom He knows will not fulfill his mission, that knowledge would prevent God from actually intending the messenger to deliver. Likewise, if God knows that a person will disbelieve and be destroyed, that knowledge should prevent God from intending good and benefit (al-khayr wa al-salah) for that person. This challenges the Ash'ari view that God intends benefit for all, because foreknowledge of failure would logically preclude that intention.

Correction: The objection is not about God's power but about the coherence of intending benefit when failure is foreknown.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh objection, what effect does God's knowledge of a person's future disbelief have on His intention of benefit?
A) It increases His intention.
B) It has no effect.
C) It should turn Him away from intending benefit.
D) It only affects the person's free will.

Key Takeaway
This objection argues that divine foreknowledge of disbelief logically prevents God from intending benefit for that person, challenging the coherence of universal divine intent.

?

The First Mu'tazili Objection: Foreknowledge and the Goodness of Taklif
Mu'tazili objection with three supporting analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili argument that if God knows a servant will disbelieve, imposing taklif cannot be considered good or beneficial, along with the analogies used to support this claim.

Core Idea
If God knows you'll fail, why would He test you?

In this lesson, we encounter a powerful objection from the Mu'tazila against the Ash'ari understanding of divine obligation (taklif). They argue that if God already knows that a particular person will disbelieve and be punished, then it would be inconsistent for Him to intend that person's good by obligating him. The objection is illustrated through three everyday analogies drawn from father-son relationships. This passage forces us to reflect on how divine foreknowledge relates to justice and wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Read the opening part of the passage where the Mu'tazili objection is presented. Notice how they establish the purpose of taklif (exposure to reward) and then point out the contradiction when God knows the outcome.
2
Identify the three analogies
Find the three analogies used: the son sent to trade, the son given a sword, and the rope lowered to a drowning person. Understand how each analogy illustrates the same point: if the outcome is known to be harmful, the action cannot be considered wise or good.
The analogies are in the latter part of the passage.
3
Understand the underlying premise
Reflect on the Mu'tazili assumption that taklif must be for the servant's benefit (al-salāh/al-aslah) and that God's knowledge of the outcome makes the act of obligating counterproductive.
This premise is contested by Ash'aris.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this objection challenges the Ash'ari view that God's will and wisdom are not constrained by human concepts of benefit, and that taklif is a test regardless of foreknowledge.
This leads to deeper discussions on divine justice and predestination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها قولكم إنكم حسبتم التكليف لتعريض المكلف للثواب الدائم، وإذا علم الرب أنه لو اخترم العبد قبل البلوغ وكمال العقل لكان ناجيا، ولو أمهله وسهل له النظر لعند وكفر وجحد ، فكيف يستقيم أن يقال : أراد به الصلاح والأصلح؟ ومن المعلوم أن المقصود من التكليف عند القوم الصلاح والتعريض لا معنى الدرجات التي لا تنال إلا بالأعمال، فيجب أن يكون الرب مسيئًا للنظر لمن خلقه وأكمل عقله وكلفه مع العلم بأنه يهلك ويخسر، ولزم من حيث الحكمة أن يكون عن إرادته، والتعريض لمن هذه حاله بالنفع والثواب، وفي العادة كل من عرف من حال ولده أنه لو سافر واتجر في مال يعطيه لهلك وخسر لا يحسن من حيث العقل أن يبعثه لأجل التجارة ويعطيه المال لأجل الخسارة، ولو علم من ولده أنه لو أعطاه سيفا وسلاحًا ليقاتل عدوا من أعدائه لقتل نفسه ويبقى السلاح للعدو، لم يكن من الحكمة أن يعطيه السلاح ويبعثه للقاء عدوه البتة، بل لو فعل ذلك كان ساعيًا في هلاك ولده. وهذا بمثابة من أدلى حبلًا إلى غريق ليخلص به نفسه، مع العلم بأنه يخنق نفسه فقد أساء النظر له، بل قصد به هلاكه
And among them is your saying: You considered taklif to be for exposing the obligated person to eternal reward. And if the Lord knows that if He would have taken the servant before puberty and completion of intellect, he would have been saved, and if He would have given him respite and made reflection easy for him, he would have turned astray and disbelieved and denied, then how can it be correct to say: He intended good and the most beneficial? And it is known that the purpose of taklif according to the people is good and exposure, not the meaning of ranks that are only attained through deeds. So it must be that the Lord is doing badly by the one He created, completed his intellect, and obligated him, while knowing that he will perish and lose. And it follows, from the standpoint of wisdom, that He would be against His will, and exposing someone of this state to benefit and reward. And customarily, whoever knows from the condition of his son that if he travels and trades with the money he gives him, he will perish and lose, it is not good, from the standpoint of reason, to send him for the sake of trade and give him the money for the sake of loss. And if he knew from his son that if he gave him a sword and weapons to fight an enemy from his enemies, he would kill himself and the weapon would remain for the enemy, it would not be from wisdom to give him the weapon and send him to meet his enemy at all. Rather, if he did that, he would be striving for the destruction of his son. And this is like one who lowers a rope to a drowning person to save himself, while knowing that he will strangle himself — he has done badly by him, rather, he intended his destruction.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage presents a Mu'tazili objection against the Ash'ari view of divine predestination. It argues that the purpose of taklif (religious obligation) is to expose the servant to reward. However, if God knows that a particular servant would disbelieve and be punished if He allowed him to reach maturity, then it would be inconsistent for God to intend good (al-salāh) and the most beneficial (al-aslah) for him. The objection uses two analogies: first, a father who knows his son would lose money if he sends him on a business trip would not be considered wise to do so; second, a father who gives his son a sword knowing he would be killed by the enemy would be causing his son's destruction. The passage then adds a third analogy: lowering a rope to a drowning man while knowing he will strangle himself is not a good deed but an intent to destroy. The point is that, according to the Mu'tazila, God's foreknowledge of disbelief should prevent Him from imposing obligations that lead to harm.

Key Terms
تكليف
صلاح
أصلح
ثواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the Ash'aris deny God's foreknowledge or that He knows future disbelief.
Correction: Both Ash'aris and Mu'tazila affirm God's foreknowledge. The disagreement is about the implications for the goodness of taklif.

Checkpoint
What is the role of the analogies in the Mu'tazili objection?
A) To prove that God does not exist.
B) To show that taklif is impossible if God knows the outcome.
C) To illustrate that a wise person would not cause harm if he knows the outcome.
D) To argue that children should not be obligated.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that if God knows a person will disbelieve, imposing taklif is not good or wise, using analogies from human experience to support their point.

?

?

?
The analogy of the father giving a sword to his son who will misuse it—can you think of a time when you blamed Allah for a test that seemed destructive? How does this passage challenge you to reconsider your view of divine wisdom?

Second Objection: God's Knowledge Prevents Willing Good for the Disbeliever
The author presents a second Mu'tazili argument that God's knowledge of disbelief prevents Him from willing good for the servant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili argument that God's foreknowledge of disbelief prevents Him from willing good for the disbeliever.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of future evil would contradict His willing good.

The Mu'tazilites argue that if God knows someone will disbelieve, He cannot genuinely will their good because that would be inconsistent with His knowledge. This passage uses an analogy with a messenger who God knows will not convey the message: just as God would not will the messenger to act, He would not will good for a known disbeliever. This reasoning supports their doctrine that God must always act for the best (al-aslah).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Let the argument's structure become clear: an analogy between a messenger and a disbelieving servant.
2
Identify the analogy
Notice how the argument draws a parallel between God sending a messenger (whom He knows will not convey) and God willing good for a servant (whom He knows will disbelieve).
The same reasoning applies: knowledge of failure turns God away from willing the act.
3
Understand the implication
The Mu'tazilites conclude that if God knows a servant will disbelieve, He cannot will good for them—this supports their view that God must do what is best for the believer, not the disbeliever.
This is a key argument in the debate over divine justice and predestination.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
Consider why this argument matters: it challenges the Ash'ari view that God can will good for someone He knows will reject it.
The disagreement hinges on whether God's will must align with His knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن مذهبهم : أن الرب تعالى لو علم أنه لو أرسل رسولا إلى خلقه وكلفه الأداء عنه مع علمه بأنه لا يؤدي فإن علمه به يصرفه عن إرادته الأداء عنه، فكذلك لو علم أنه يكفر ويهلك وجب أن يصرفه عن إرادته الخير والصلاح له
And from their school: That the Lord, exalted, if He knew that if He sent a messenger to His creation and obligated him to convey from Him, while knowing that he would not convey, then His knowledge of it would turn him away from willing the performance from him. Similarly, if He knew that he would disbelieve and perish, it would be necessary that it would turn Him away from willing good and benefit for him.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage presents another Mu'tazili argument. It draws a parallel between sending a messenger and obligating a servant. If God knows that a messenger will not fulfill his duty of conveying the message, then His knowledge would prevent Him from willing that the messenger perform the task. Likewise, if God knows that a servant will disbelieve and be destroyed, His knowledge should prevent Him from willing good and benefit for that servant. The argument is that God's foreknowledge of evil consequences necessitates that He cannot genuinely intend good for that individual, because it would be futile or contradictory. This is another way to argue that God must act for the best (al-aslah) and cannot knowingly cause harm.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilis argue that God's foreknowledge of disbelief would prevent Him from willing good for that person, supporting their doctrine that God must act for the best.

Third Objection: Obligation Leading to Community Corruption
The author presents a Mu'tazili objection based on communal harm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili argument that obligating someone whom God knows will cause communal corruption is morally reprehensible.

Core Idea
If God knows taklif causes community harm, it becomes unjust.

In this passage, the author presents a third Mu'tazili objection against the Ash'ari view of taklif. The argument moves from individual harm to communal harm: if God knows that imposing obligation on a specific person will lead to corruption of the community, then that obligation is morally repugnant. This challenges the notion that God can will anything without regard for consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to grasp the exact wording of the argument.
2
Identify the conditional and conclusion
Notice the condition: 'if God knows that obligating a servant will cause community corruption.' The conclusion: 'then His obligation is reprehensible.'
The reason is given in the last clause.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The Mu'tazila hold that God must do what is best (al-aslah). Causing communal corruption violates this, making the obligation qabih.
This argument extends the previous ones by considering societal impact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن مذهبهم : أن الرب إذا علم أن في تكليفه عبدا من العباد فساد الجماعة، فإنه يقبح تكليفه ؛ لأنه استفساد لمن يعلم أنه يكفر عند تكليفه.
And from their school: That the Lord, if He knows that in obligating a servant from the servants there is corruption of the community, then His obligation is reprehensible, because it is corrupting someone whom He knows will disbelieve upon his obligation.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage presents a third Mu'tazili argument focusing on communal harm. The idea is that if God knows that imposing religious obligations on a particular servant will lead to corruption within the community (فساد الجماعة), then such an obligation becomes morally repugnant (قبيح). The reason given is that it amounts to 'corrupting' (استفساد) the person whom God knows will disbelieve when he is obligated. This argument extends the previous ones by considering not just the individual's fate but also the broader societal impact. The Mu'tazila hold that God must do what is best (al-aslah) for all creation, and causing communal corruption violates this principle.

استفساد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this argument applies to all obligations.
Correction: It specifically targets cases where God knows the outcome will be communal corruption.

A) Because God should not create anyone who will cause corruption.
B) Because it is corrupting someone God knows will disbelieve when obligated.
C) Because the person does not have free will.
D) Because the community will reject the obligation.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that if God knows that an obligation will lead to communal corruption, that obligation is morally reprehensible because it involves corrupting someone destined to disbelieve.

The Objection of Suffering and the Two Children Dilemma
The author presents an objection, gives the opponent's answer, refutes it, and poses a dilemma about two children.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Mu'tazili objection that God's ability to grant reward freely obviates the need for trials, and how the two-children example challenges the Mu'tazili principle of al-aslah.

Core Idea
If reward can be given freely, why hardship? The answer reveals the reality of divine sovereignty.

The Mu'tazila argued that since God can simply grant reward without any effort, imposing religious obligations and trials seems purposeless. The author exposes the flaw in this reasoning, then flips the argument against them, ending with a powerful analogy of two children that questions human notions of fairness. Let's explore this dense objection and response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the first part of the passage where the Mu'tazili objection is stated and their answer is given. Notice their reasoning that receiving what one deserves is more pleasant than accepting a favor.
The opponent here is the Mu'tazila, who believed God must always do what is best (al-aslah).
2
Understand the refutation
The author rejects the Mu'tazili answer, calling it the speech of one who does not know God. He argues that the servant should not disdain God's favor, and that God could have created them in Paradise without obligation.
The author emphasizes God's absolute sovereignty: 'His is the creation and the command.'
3
Follow the counter-argument
The author turns the Mu'tazili logic against them: if more hardship leads to greater reward, why did God not withhold all divine assistances to make the reward even more abundant?
This reductio ad absurdum exposes the flaw in their reasoning.
4
Examine the two-children dilemma
Read the two-children example carefully. One child dies before puberty and is saved from disbelief; the other reaches maturity, disbelieves, and is punished. Consider what objection this raises against the Mu'tazili view.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the intended dilemma is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الإلزامات: أن القوم قضوا بأن الرب تعالى قادر على التفضل بمثل الثواب، فأي غرض في تعريض العباد للبلوى والمشاق ؟ قالوا : الغرض فيه أن استيفاء المستحق أهنأ وألذ من قبول التفضل، وهذا كلام من لم يعرف الله حق معرفته، وكيف يستنكف العبد وهو مخلوق مربوب من قبول فضل الله تعالى، فلو خلق الخلق وأسكنهم الجنة كان حسنًا منه، ولو خلقهم في الدنيا ثم أماتهم من غير تكليف كان حسنًا ( أَلَا لَهُ الخَلْقُ وَالْأَمْرُ تَبَارَكَ اللَّهُ رَبُّ الْعَالَمِينَ ) [الأعراف: ٥٤]، أليس لو خلقهم وكلفهم وقطع عنهم الألطاف كانوا أبلغ في الاجتهاد وأحمل للمشاق فكان الثواب لهم أكثر والالتذاذ بما يستحقونه من العوض أشد ، فهلا قطع عنهم الألطاف بأسرها لتكون اللذة في الثواب أوفر. ثم نلزمهم فرض الكلام في طفلين ؛ أحدهما اخترمه قبل البلوغ لعلمه بأنه لو بلغ لكفر، فصار الصلاح في حقه الاخترام حتى لا يستوجب عقاب النار ، والآخر أوصله إلى البلوغ والتكليف فكفر ، فيقول : يا رب
And among the necessary implications: That the people judged that the Lord, exalted, is capable of bestowing favor with the like of reward, so what purpose is there in exposing servants to trial and hardships? They said: The purpose is that receiving what one deserves is more pleasant and delicious than accepting favor. And this is the speech of one who does not know God truly. How can the servant, who is a created and controlled being, be disdainful of accepting the favor of God, exalted? For if He created the creation and settled them in Paradise, it would be good from Him. And if He created them in the world then caused them to die without obligation, it would be good: 'Indeed, His is the creation and the command; blessed is Allah, Lord of the worlds' [Al-A'raf: 54]. Indeed, if He created them, obligated them, and withheld from them the divine assistances (alṭāf), they would be more striving in effort and more bearing of hardships, so the reward would be greater for them and the enjoyment of what they deserve of compensation would be more intense. So why did He not withhold from them all the divine assistances so that the pleasure in the reward would be more abundant? Then we oblige them with a hypothetical case of two children: one of them He took before puberty because He knew that if he reached puberty he would disbelieve, so the good in his case was the taking before puberty so that he does not deserve the punishment of the Fire; and the other He brought to puberty and obligation, and he disbelieved, so he says: O Lord...
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage presents a final set of arguments. First, it states the Mu'tazili objection (الإلزام) that since God is capable of granting reward as a favor (tafaḍḍul), there is no need to expose servants to trials and hardships through taklif. The opponent (the Mu'tazila) answers that receiving reward as something deserved (mustaḥaqq) is more pleasant than accepting a favor. The author refutes this, calling it the speech of one who does not know God, arguing that the servant should not disdain God's favor; indeed, God could have created them in Paradise without obligation, and that would be good. The author then turns the argument around: if the purpose is greater reward through hardship, then why did God not withhold all divine assistances (alṭāf) to make the reward even greater? Finally, the author poses a dilemma about two children: one who dies before puberty (and is saved) and another who reaches maturity and disbelieves (and is punished). The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing with an objection from the disbeliever against God's justice. The overall point is to challenge the Mu'tazili view that God must always act for the best (al-aslah).

Key Terms

صلاح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the author's response implies that God is indifferent to human suffering or that He could have prevented it but chose not to.
Correction: The author is not denying God's mercy; rather, he is asserting that God's actions are not bound by human standards of what is best. God can do what He wills, and all His actions are good.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the two children example in the passage?
A) To show that God treats all children equally.
B) To challenge the Mu'tazili view that God must do what is best for each individual.
C) To prove that predestination is unjust.
D) To argue that all children should be obligated.

Key Takeaway
God's sovereignty is absolute; He is not obligated to follow human standards of what is best.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship in your religious duties, do you ever feel that you 'deserve' an easier path? How does the author's rebuke of those who refuse God's favor challenge that sense of entitlement?
?
The dilemma of the two children shows that God's knowledge determines each person's fate before they act. Are you truly at peace with the fact that your ultimate salvation depends entirely on God's will and not on your own efforts? How does that change how you view your daily obedience?
?
If God had created you directly in Paradise without any test, would you have been content with that? Or do you, like the Mu'tazila, feel a need to 'earn' your reward? What does that desire for self-justification reveal about your view of God's grace?
