The Reductio Argument Against al-Aṣlaḥ
Reductio against Mu'tazila using child deaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila principle of al-aṣlaḥ leads to the absurd demand that God kill potential believers before they disbelieve.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila view forces God to cause early deaths to avoid eternal punishment.

The author presents a powerful reductio ad absurdum against the Mu'tazila claim that God must always do what is most beneficial for His creatures. He imagines a child who died before puberty questioning why God did not cause him to die even earlier to avoid the risk of eternal punishment, and then two hypothetical children whose fates invert expectations. This argument exposes the absurd implications of the principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the scenario. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the two examples: the complaining child and the two children.
The language is argumentative; notice the direct speech.
2
Identify the first reductio example
The first example is a child who died before puberty objecting to God: 'Why did You not cause me to die even earlier so that I would not face obligation and punishment?' This shows that if God must always do the most beneficial, He should have killed the child earlier.
This is a rhetorical objection attributed to the child.
3
Analyze the second reductio example
The second example contrasts two children: one dies before puberty though he would have believed, the other lives to puberty but becomes a disbeliever. The objector asks why God did not kill the second child before puberty. This reveals that the principle requires God to preemptively kill potential disbelievers.
The absurdity is that God would have to intervene contrary to actual outcomes.
4
Understand the reductio's conclusion
The passage then states the logical consequence: one who holds al-aṣlaḥ must demand that God cause premature death for those He knows will disbelieve, and spare those He knows will believe. This shows the principle leads to a self-defeating requirement.
This sets up the critique in the following lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هلا اخترمتني قبل البلوغ كما اخترمت أخي حتى لا يتوجه عليَّ تكليف يوجب عقاب الأبد. وطفلين آخرين اخترم أحدهما قبل البلوغ وهو ابن كافر، وقد علم أنه لو بلغ لآمن وأصلح، والآخر أوصله إلى البلوغ وهو ابن مسلم فكفر وأفسد، فيقول: هلا اخترمتني حتى لا أستوجب عقاب الأبد، وبالجملة من أوجب رعاية الصلاح والأصلح يوجب اخترام الأطفال الذين علم الله تعالى منهم الكفر بعد البلوغ حتى يوجد كافر في العالم، وإبقاء الأطفال الذين علم الله تعالى منهم الإيمان حتى لا يتحقق إلا الإيمان والمؤمن في العالم والصلاح يدور على المعلوم كيف كان ثم ما من أصلح إلا وفوقه أصلح والاقتصار على مرتبة واحدة كالاقتصار على الصلاح، فلا معنى للأصلح ولا نهاية له ولا يمكن في العقل رعايته.
Why did You cause me to die before puberty as You caused my brother to die, so that no obligation would be directed at me that would necessitate eternal punishment? And two other children: one died before puberty and he was the son of a disbeliever, and it was known that if he had reached puberty he would have believed and become righteous, and the other was allowed to reach puberty and he was the son of a Muslim, then he disbelieved and became corrupt. So he says: Why did You not cause me to die so that I would not deserve eternal punishment? In summary, whoever makes it obligatory to observe benefit (al-ṣalāḥ) and the more beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) necessitates the premature death of children whom God knows will disbelieve after puberty, so that only a disbeliever exists in the world, and the preservation of children whom God knows will believe, so that only faith and the believer exist in the world. But benefit revolves around what is known, however it is. Then there is no more beneficial except that above it is more beneficial, and restricting to one level is like restricting to benefit. So there is no meaning to 'the more beneficial', no end to it, and it is not possible in reason to observe it.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila principle that God must always do what is most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) for His creatures. The author presents a reductio ad absurdum. First, he quotes an imagined objection from a child who died before puberty, asking why God did not cause him to die earlier to avoid the obligation of religious law that would lead to eternal punishment. Then he describes two hypothetical children: one dies before puberty, the son of a disbeliever, but would have believed and done good had he lived; the other lives to puberty, the son of a Muslim, but becomes a disbeliever and corrupt. The objector asks why God did not let him die earlier. The author then shows the logical consequence of the Mu'tazila view: one who holds that God must observe benefit (al-ṣalāḥ) and the more beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) would be forced to require the premature death of children whom God knows will disbelieve after puberty, so that only disbelievers exist, and the preservation of those whom He knows will believe, so that only believers exist. But benefit is relative to what God knows. Moreover, there is no limit to what is 'more beneficial'; every level has a higher one. Thus restricting to one level is arbitrary, like restricting to mere benefit. Hence the concept of 'the more beneficial' is meaningless, endless, and cannot be rationally required of God.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some hold that the Mu'tazila principle of al-aṣlaḥ is a rational necessity that God must always act for the greatest benefit of each individual.
Correction: This passage shows that the principle leads to absurd consequences, such as requiring God to preemptively kill children to avoid future punishment, which contradicts observable reality and divine wisdom.

Checkpoint
In the reductio argument, what does the child who died before puberty complain?
A) That he was not allowed to live longer to do good deeds.
B) That God did not cause him to die even earlier to avoid obligation and punishment.
C) That his brother was treated better than him.
D) That he was not given a chance to repent.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila principle of al-aṣlaḥ, if applied consistently, would demand that God cause the premature death of potential disbelievers, which is an absurd and self-defeating requirement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you were the child who died before puberty in the example, would you still complain? What does your answer reveal about your view of God's justice and mercy in your own life?
?
The Mu'tazila principle seems compassionate, yet it leads to an absurd conclusion. Are there areas in your life where you demand that God act according to your idea of what is best for you? How does this reductio make you reconsider that demand?

The Impossibility of Observing al-Aṣlaḥ
Conceptual critique of al-aṣlaḥ as meaningless by infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of 'the more beneficial' (al-aṣlaḥ) is meaningless and cannot be rationally required of God.

Core Idea
There is no limit to 'more beneficial', so requiring it is arbitrary.

Having shown the absurd practical consequences of the Mu'tazila principle, the author now attacks its conceptual foundation. He argues that benefit is relative to God's knowledge, and that for every level of 'more beneficial' there is a higher one. Thus restricting to any particular level is as arbitrary as restricting to mere benefit, making the concept of al-aṣlaḥ indefinable and impossible to observe.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Focus on the Arabic from 'والصلاح يدور على المعلوم كيف كان' to the end. Notice the claim that benefit revolves around what God knows, and that there is no ultimate 'more beneficial'.
This is a conceptual argument, not an empirical one.
2
Understand the relativity of benefit
The author says 'benefit revolves around what is known, however it is.' This means that what is beneficial depends on God's knowledge of outcomes, not on a fixed standard. Therefore, the Mu'tazila cannot claim an objective scale of benefit.
This undercuts the idea that one can determine what is 'most' beneficial.
3
Grasp the infinite regress argument
The passage states: 'there is no more beneficial except that above it is more beneficial.' This means that for any proposed level of benefit, a higher level can be conceived. Hence there is no ultimate 'most beneficial' — the concept is endless.
This is a logical point: if 'more beneficial' is always possible, then 'the most beneficial' does not exist.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that restricting to one level is like restricting to mere benefit, and that 'the more beneficial' has no meaning, no end, and cannot be rationally required of God. This dismantles the Mu'tazila obligation.
This completes the refutation.

Mu'tazila Definition of Tawfīq
Presents the Mu'tazila definition of tawfīq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila definition of tawfīq (divine guidance) as a set of external divine acts that guide rational beings towards knowledge of God.

Core Idea
Tawfīq is God's external guidance: signs, intellect, messengers, and books.

This lesson introduces the theological concept of tawfīq (divine guidance) as defined by the Mu'tazila. The author first notes that various groups hold extreme views on this topic, then presents the Mu'tazila position. Understanding this definition is crucial for appreciating the broader debate on divine guidance and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Observe the rhythm and key terms.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the definition may continue beyond this excerpt.
2
Identify the two parties
Notice the author's reference to 'the two parties' (al-farīqayn) — the groups with extreme views. The truth is between them.
'The two parties' likely refer to the Mu'tazila and the Ash'ariyya, but the text only gives the Mu'tazila definition.
3
Examine the Mu'tazila definition
List the components of tawfīq as given: manifestation of signs, creation of intellect, hearing, and sight, sending messengers, revealing books.
These are all external acts of God, not internal predestination.
4
Understand the purpose
The text states these acts are a grace (luṭf) from God to awaken the rational and bring them closer to knowledge of Him.
This aligns with the Mu'tazila emphasis on human reason and responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللفريقين في التوفيق والخذلان والشرح والطبع وأمثالها كلام على طرفي الغلو والتقصير والحق بينهما دون الجائز منهما. قالت المعتزلة : التوفيق من الله تعالى إظهار الآيات في خلقه الدالة على وحدانيته وإبداع العقل والسمع والبصر في الإنسان، وإرسال الرسل، وإنزال الكتب؛ لطفا منه تعالى، وتنبيها للعقلاء من غفلتهم، وتقريبا للطرق إلى معرفته،
And for the two parties there is speech concerning tawfīq (divine guidance) and khidhlān (abandonment), and al-sharḥ (expansion) and al-ṭab‘ (seal) and the like, on the extremes of exaggeration and deficiency, and the truth is between them, apart from what is excessive from them. The Mu'tazila said: Tawfīq from God Almighty is the manifestation of signs in His creation that indicate His oneness, and the creation of intellect, hearing, and sight in man, and the sending of messengers, and the revelation of books, as a grace (luṭf) from Him, and an awakening of the rational from their heedlessness, and a bringing near of the paths to knowledge of Him.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage introduces the theological discussion of divine guidance (tawfīq) and abandonment (khidhlān), along with related concepts like expansion (sharḥ) and sealing (ṭab‘). The author notes that both extremes of exaggeration and deficiency exist among the groups, and the truth lies between them. Then he presents the Mu'tazila definition of tawfīq: they consider it to be God's acts of displaying signs of His oneness in creation, creating the faculties of intellect, hearing, and sight in humans, sending prophets, and revealing scriptures. These are described as a grace (luṭf) from God, meant to awaken rational people from heedlessness and draw them closer to knowledge of Him. The passage is cut off at the end of the page, so the definition may continue in subsequent pages.

?
Prophets and revealed books are also tawfīq from God. How seriously do you take the Quran as a personal guide for your life? Have you treated it as a mere text, or as an awakening call? This lesson calls it a bringing near of paths to knowledge of Him — are you walking those paths?

Refutation of Mu'tazila on Granting the Listener Time
Refutes the Mu'tazila claim that a prophet must grant time if asked.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why granting time to every listener would logically undermine prophethood, according to the author's reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
If prophets always granted time, no prophecy would ever be established.

The Mu'tazila argued that a prophet must grant time if a listener asks to reflect. But the author shows that this seemingly merciful principle leads to absurd consequences that destroy the very basis of prophethood. By following the logic, we see that the only consistent outcome is that the listener would end up fighting and killing the prophet.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila claim
The Mu'tazila claim that if a listener asks for time (استمهال), the prophet must grant it (إمهال). This sounds reasonable, but the author will show its logical failure.
2
Follow the reductio: step by step
Trace what happens if we accept the claim. The author presents a chain of absurdities: prophethood never established, perpetual waiting, a quarrel, and ultimately killing.
Notice how each step follows necessarily from the previous one.
3
See the absurd conclusion
The argument forces the Mu'tazila to say that the first duty of a rational person is to kill the prophet. This is impossible, so the original claim is false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum – showing a claim leads to a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا كما زعمت المعتزلة أن المستمع إذا استمهل وجب على النبي إمهاله، فيلزمهم أن لا تثبت لنبي رسالة ولا يستمر لرسول دعوة. فإن كل عاقل إذا استمهل وأمهل تعطلت النبوة في الحال، وصار منتظراً مدة الإمهال حتى يعود إليه الدعوة، وإن يلزم العود إليه وبعد لم تثبت عنده نبوته، بل يعود جبريل عليه السلام ويقول: {قم فأنذر} فيقوم إلى المستجيب فيقول: أنت أمهلتني وأنا بعد في مهلة النظر، والنظر طريق المعرفة، فتريد تصدني عن الطريق، فأنت قبل معرفتي من أنت؟ مضل ضال قاطع للطريق، فيتخاصمان ويقتلان، ويؤول الإلزام إلى النبي قولاً وفعلاً، فيلزم المعتزلة القول بأن أول واجب على العاقل قتال النبي عليه السلام وقتله، وهذا مما لا محيص لهم عنه.
Not as the Mu'tazila claimed that if the listener asks for time, it is obligatory for the prophet to grant him time. This would necessarily imply that no prophet has his message established, and no messenger has a call that continues. For if every rational person who asks for time is given time, prophethood would be nullified immediately, and he would become waiting for the period of postponement until the call returns to him, and the return to him would be necessary, but after that, his prophethood would not be established with him. Rather, Gabriel, peace be upon him, would return and say: 'Arise and warn.' Then he would go to the respondent and say: 'You gave me time, and I am still in the period of reflection, and reflection is the path of knowledge. You want to block me from the path. So, before my knowledge, who are you? A misguider, misguided, obstructer of the path.' Then they would quarrel and kill each other, and the obligation would lead to the prophet, in word and deed. Thus it becomes necessary for the Mu'tazila to say that the first duty upon the rational person is to fight the prophet, peace be upon him, and kill him. And there is no escape for them from this.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position that if a listener asks for time to think, the prophet must grant it. The author argues that this claim leads to absurd consequences, making it false. First, if every listener who asks for time must be given time, then no prophet's message would ever be established; the call would be perpetually delayed. Second, he portrays a hypothetical scenario: a listener asks for time, the prophet gives it, and then the listener remains in a state of reflection. When Gabriel comes again to the prophet to command him to warn, the listener argues that the prophet is blocking him from the path of knowledge (reflection). This leads to a quarrel and mutual killing, ultimately obligating the listener to fight and kill the prophet. Thus, the Mu'tazila are forced to say that the first duty of a rational person is to kill the prophet—a clear impossibility. This reductio ad absurdum shows that the original claim (that the prophet must grant time) is invalid. The author asserts that the prophetic method described earlier (not granting unnecessary delay) is correct.

Key Terms
استمهال (asking for time)

Absurdity of Granting Respite in Prophetic Call
al-Shahrastani refutes the Mu'tazila position on granting respite to listeners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Shahrastani refutes the Mu'tazila position on granting respite to listeners by showing it leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
If prophets must grant time to consider, prophethood would be endlessly suspended.

In this passage, al-Shahrastani takes aim at a specific Mu'tazila claim: that a prophet must delay calling whenever a listener asks for time to think. He demonstrates that this seemingly reasonable request would actually destroy the very possibility of prophethood, creating an infinite regress and even justifying killing the prophet. Read on to see his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the tone of refutation and the logical structure.
The author uses a reductio ad absurdum argument.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila position
Find the statement that describes the Mu'tazila claim: that if a listener asks for respite, the prophet must grant it.
3
Follow the hypothetical scenario
The author paints a scenario: if respite is granted, the listener says he is still considering, and this leads to accusations and even killing the prophet. Understand how this absurd outcome refutes the original claim.
The central point: granting respite would make prophethood impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مفحمتان، وإنما لم يلزم تكليف ما لا يطاق إذا لم يكن المستمع متمكنا من الاستماع والنظر والتدبر في كل ما يستمعه ويعقله، حتى لو أنكره ظهر عناده وبطل استرشاده، هذا هو طريق الدعوة النبوية والحجة العقلية، لا كما زعمت المعتزلة أن المستمع إذا استمهل وجب على النبي إمهاله، فيلزمهم أن لا تثبت لنبي رسالة ولا يستمر لرسول دعوة. فإن كل عاقل إذا استمهل وأمهل تعطلت النبوة في الحال، وصار منتظرا مدة الإمهال حتى يعود إليه الدعوة، وإن يلزم العود إليه وبعد لم تثبت عنده نبوته، بل يعود جبريل عليه السلام ويقول: قم فأنذر فيقوم إلى المستجيب فيقول: أنت أمهلتني وأنا بعد في مهلة النظر، والنظر طريق المعرفة، فتريد تصدني عن الطريق، فأنت قبل معرفتي من أنت مضل ضال قاطع للطريق فيتخاصمان ويقتلان، ويؤول الإلزام إلى النبي قولا وفعلا، فيلزم المعتزلة القول بأن أول واجب على العاقل قتال النبي عليه السلام وقتله، وهذا مما لا محيص لهم عنه.
Two compelling arguments, and indeed the obligation of what is not within capacity does not apply if the listener is not capable of listening, considering, and reflecting upon everything he hears and understands, such that if he denies it, his obstinacy becomes apparent and his seeking guidance is nullified. This is the way of prophetic call and rational proof, not as the Mu'tazila claimed that if the listener asks for respite, the prophet must grant him respite. So it follows from them that no prophet's message can be established and no messenger's call can continue. For every rational person, if he asks for respite and is granted it, prophethood becomes suspended immediately, and he becomes a waiter for the duration of the respite until the call returns to him. And the return to him would be necessary after his prophethood had not been established with him. Rather, Gabriel, peace be upon him, returns and says: 'Arise and warn.' So he arises to the respondent and says: 'You granted me respite, and I am still in the period of consideration, and consideration is the path of knowledge. So you want to block me from the path? So you, before my knowledge of you, are you a misguider, misguided, a cutter of the path?' So they dispute and kill each other, and the obligation leads to the prophet in word and deed. So it necessitates that the Mu'tazila say that the first duty of the intelligent person is to fight and kill the prophet, peace be upon him. And there is no escape for them from this.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position that when a prophet calls people to Islam, if the listener asks for time to consider (istimhal), the prophet must grant it. The author argues that if this were accepted, prophethood would be suspended indefinitely because every listener could ask for respite. He illustrates with a hypothetical scenario: Gabriel commands the prophet to warn, but the listener claims he is still in the period of consideration and that the prophet's call would be blocked, leading to conflict and even killing the prophet. So the Mu'tazila would have to say that the first duty of a rational person is to kill the prophet, which is absurd. Thus their position is refuted.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila position that prophets must grant time for consideration is self-defeating because it would make prophethood impossible and lead to the ultimate contradiction: that the first duty of a rational person would be to kill the prophet.

Mu'tazila on Obligatory Reflection Before Revelation
Author critiques Mu'tazila for inconsistency in their argument for obligatory reflection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila's argument for obligatory reflection before revelation is self-defeating according to the author.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's own logic backfires on the prophet's claim.

This passage presents a sharp critique of the Mu'tazila. They argued that rational reflection is obligatory before revelation, based on a rational calculus of two thoughts. The author turns their own argument against them, showing it leads to inconsistent conclusions about belief in a prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Notice the flow of the critique.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila's original argument
Identify the Mu'tazila premise: a rational person has two thoughts (one for reflection, one against), and chooses the safer path. They conclude reflection is obligatory before revelation.
Reflection before revelation means using reason alone.
3
Follow the author's counter-application
See how the author applies the same logic to the prophet's claim: the report is either true or false, and the same two thoughts should occur. Believing is safer than disbelieving.
The author argues that belief is the rational safe choice.
4
Note the final inconsistency about the Qur'an
The author highlights that the Mu'tazila consider the Qur'an like human speech, which undermines their argument for its inimitability, thus making their position inconsistent.
This reveals a crack in their overall doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن العجب أن المعتزلة قالوا بوجوب النظر قبل ورود الشرع، وبنوا ذلك على أن العاقل المفكر لا يخلو عن خاطرين يطريان على قلبه؛ أحدهما يدعوه إلى النظر حتى يعرف الصانع فيشكر فيثاب على شكره، والثاني يمنعه عن ذلك فيختار بعقله أحق الطريقين بالأمن، ويرفض أحقهما بالخوف، فيقال لهم: إذا تحدى النبي بالرسالة، وأخبر أنه رسول الله كان خبره محتملاً للصدق والكذب، فهلا طرأ الخاطران في تصديقه وتكذيبه، ثم يحصل الخبر بالصدق أولى، فإن فيه الأمن إذ لو كان صادقًا فكذبه خسر ولو كان كاذبًا فصدقه لم يخسر، فإنه إن يكن كاذبًا فعليه كذبه وفي طلب المعجزة منه خطر آخر، وهو جعل الخبر أولى بالكذب فكأنه يكذبه في الحال إلى أن تظهر المعجزة فيصدقه في ثاني الحال، وعند المعتزلة القرآن من جنس كلام العرب، فإنهم يقدرون على مثله قدرتهم على كلامهم، فلم يستمر لهم التمسك بإعجاز القرآن.
And it is surprising that the Mu'tazila said that reflection (al-nazar) is obligatory before the arrival of the Law (al-shar'), and they built that on the premise that the rational, thinking person is never free from two thoughts that occur to his heart: one calls him to reflection in order to know the Maker, so that he may be grateful and be rewarded for his gratitude, and the other prevents him from that, so he chooses with his intellect the safer of the two paths and rejects the more frightening of the two. So it is said to them: When the prophet challenges with the message and informs that he is the messenger of God, his report is susceptible to truth and falsehood. Why then do the two thoughts not occur concerning believing him and disbelieving him? Then the report based on truth would be preferable, because in it there is safety—for if he is truthful and you belie him, you lose, and if he is lying and you believe him, you do not lose. For if he is lying, his lie is against him. And in seeking a miracle from him there is another risk, which is to deem the report more likely false, as if you belie him immediately until the miracle appears, then you believe him in the second moment. And according to the Mu'tazila, the Qur'an is of the same kind as the speech of the Arabs, for they are able to produce its like just as they are able to produce their own speech, so they could not consistently hold to the inimitability (i'jāz) of the Qur'an.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the Mu'tazila's position on obligatory reflection before revelation. The author begins by expressing surprise at the Mu'tazila's claim that rational reflection is obligatory even before divine law arrives. They base this on the idea that a rational person inevitably faces two conflicting thoughts: one urging him to reflect so as to know the Creator and be rewarded, and another dissuading him. He then chooses the safer path. The author then turns this argument against them: when a prophet claims to be a messenger, his report can be either true or false. By the same logic, the two thoughts should occur regarding belief and disbelief. The author argues that believing the report is safer: if the prophet is true and you deny him, you lose; if he is false and you believe him, you do not lose. Seeking a miracle before belief is riskier because it effectively denies the prophet until the miracle appears. Finally, the author notes that the Mu'tazila consider the Qur'an to be like human speech and claim humans can produce its like, which undermines their own argument for the Qur'an's inimitability.

Key Terms
النظر
الشرع
خاطران
الصدق
الكذب
المعجزة
إعجاز القرآن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila require reflection before any claim, including the prophet's.
Correction: The author shows their own logic would make belief in the prophet the safer choice, contradicting their requirement for reflection before accepting revelation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what happens when the Mu'tazila apply their principle of the two thoughts to the prophet's claim?
A) They find that believing is safer.
B) They find that disbelief is safer.
C) They find that seeking a miracle is safer.
D) They find no difference between truth and falsehood.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's argument for obligatory reflection before revelation is inconsistent because it would require a similar reflection before accepting a prophet's claim, which their own view on the Qur'an undermines.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you hear a clear command from the Qur'an or Sunnah, do you instinctively weigh it with your own reasoning before obeying? What would it mean for you to accept the prophet's message as the safer path, even when your mind raises doubts?
?
The Mu'tazila's over-reliance on reason led them to deny the miraculous nature of the Qur'an. Are there areas in your life where your rationalizations have led you to downplay the power of Allah's words? Repent and commit to reading the Qur'an with submission, not suspicion.

The Mu'tazila Argument Against Intercession for Grave Sinners
The author presents the Mu'tazila proof that intercession is only for obedient believers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that intercession is only for obedient believers and not for unrepentant grave sinners.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila restrict intercession to the obedient; the unrepentant sinner has no share.

This passage presents the Mu'tazila proof that intercession is limited to obedient believers. They argue that grave sinners who die without repentance merit eternal Fire, and thus cannot be saved by intercession. Their reasoning combines theological principles and Qur'anic proof texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the logical structure: claim, reasons, then evidence.
2
Identify the core claim
Find the sentence that states the Mu'tazila position on intercession. What is it limited to?
They say it is 'for the obedient believers.'
3
Understand the rationale
The passage gives two reasons: the fasiq merits Fire (and wrath) and does not deserve the name 'faith'. Trace each reason.
The first reason is based on divine wrath; the second on the definition of faith.
4
Examine the Qur'anic evidence
List the verses cited. How do they support the Mu'tazila position? Note also the argument from the absence of a third category in the Qur'an.
The verses threaten eternal punishment; the two groups imply no middle group.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشفاعة فقد قالت المعتزلة : إنها للمطيعين من المؤمنين بناء على مذهبهم أن الفاسق إذا خرج من الدنيا من غير توبة خلد في النار ؛ لأنه قد استوجب النار بفسقه، ومن دخل النار كان مغضوبًا عليه، ومن كان مغضوب عليه لا يدخل الجنة، وأيضا فإنه في حال الفسق ما استحق اسم الإيمان ؛ لأن الإيمان عبارة عن خصال محمودة يستوجب المؤمن بها المدح والثناء والفاسق لا يستوجب المدح، وقد أخلى أركان إيمانه بخروجه عن الطاعة واستدلوا على ذلك بآيات من الكتاب ؛ منها قوله تعالى : ( ومن يعص الله ورسوله ويتعد حدوده يدخله نارا خالدًا فيها ) [النساء : ١٤]، وقوله تعالى : ( بلى من كسب سيئة وأحاطت به خطيئته فأولئك أصحاب النار هم فيها خالدون [البقرة : ۸۱]، وقوله تعالى : ( ومن يقتل مؤمنًا متعمدا فجزاؤه جهنم خالدا فيها [النساء : ٩٣]. وأيضًا فإن الرب تعالى ذكر حال الأشقياء وحال السعداء، وربط الخلود بمكانهما ، ولم يذكر في القرآن قسما ثالثًا فقال : ( فأما الذين شقوا ﴾ [هود : ١٠٦] وأما الذين سعدوا ﴾ [هود : ۱۰۸] ، وفي تفصيل الفريقين ( فريق في الجنة وفريق في السعير).
And as for intercession, the Mu'tazila have said: It is for the obedient believers, based on their doctrine that the grave sinner (fasiq), if he leaves the world without repentance, will abide eternally in the Fire; because he has merited the Fire due to his sin, and whoever enters the Fire is subjected to wrath, and whoever is subjected to wrath does not enter Paradise. Also, in the state of sin he does not deserve the name of faith; because faith is an expression of praiseworthy qualities by which the believer merits praise and commendation, and the grave sinner does not merit praise, and he has emptied the pillars of his faith by his departure from obedience. And they adduced for that verses from the Book: among them His saying, Exalted: 'And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger and transgresses His limits, He will make him enter a Fire, abiding eternally therein' [An-Nisa: 14]; and His saying, Exalted: 'Nay, whoever earns evil and his sin has encompassed him, those are the companions of the Fire; they will abide therein eternally' [Al-Baqara: 81]; and His saying, Exalted: 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding eternally therein' [An-Nisa: 93]. And also, the Lord, Exalted, mentioned the state of the wretched and the state of the blessed, and tied eternal abiding to their places, and He did not mention in the Qur'an a third category, so He said: 'As for those who are wretched...' [Hud: 106] and 'As for those who are blessed...' [Hud: 108], and in the detailing of the two groups: 'A group in Paradise and a group in the Blaze.'
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila position on intercession (shafa'a). They argue that intercession is only for obedient believers, not for grave sinners (fussaq) who die unrepentant. Their reasoning: the fasiq merits eternal Fire due to his sin; once in the Fire, he is under divine wrath, which precludes entry to Paradise; and in his sinful state he does not deserve the name 'believer' because faith entails praiseworthy qualities he lacks. They support this with four Qur'anic arguments: (1) verses threatening eternal punishment for disobedience, transgression, encompassing sin, and intentional murder; (2) the fact that the Qur'an divides people only into the wretched (eternal Fire) and the blessed (eternal Paradise), with no third category. Thus, the Mu'tazila conclude that intercession cannot benefit the fasiq.

Key Terms
الشفاعة
المعتزلة
الفاسق
الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila completely reject the concept of intercession.
Correction: They affirm intercession but limit it to obedient believers, not to unrepentant grave sinners.

Checkpoint

B) Because faith is a set of praiseworthy qualities he lacks.
C) Because he has never performed any good deeds.
D) Because he will eventually repent.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that intercession is exclusively for obedient believers because the unrepentant grave sinner merits eternal Fire and cannot be called a believer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Or does the possibility of intercession drive you to more sincere obedience? Examine your heart: are you relying on intercession to save you while you continue in known disobedience without repentance?
?
The Mu'tazila insist that the unrepentant grave sinner cannot be saved. Does that thought cause you to examine your own major sins? Have you genuinely repented from sins you know are grave, or do you assume Allah will forgive you without any change in your life?
?
If intercession is only for the obedient, what does that say about your daily commitment to obedience? Are you living as one who expects intercession, or are you indifferent to your own sins? What specific sin will you begin to work on today, starting right now?

The Khawarij Argument: Grave Sinners as Unbelievers
The author presents the Khawarij argument that grave sinners are unbelievers, citing Iblis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Khawarij position that committing a major sin renders one an unbeliever, and their proof from the story of Iblis.

Core Idea
The Khawarij use Iblis to prove that a single major sin makes one a kafir.

The Khawarij were known for their extreme position on sin, going beyond other sects. In this passage, the author presents their argument that a person who commits a major sin (kabira) is not merely a sinner but an outright unbeliever (kafir). Their proof is drawn from the story of Iblis, whom they see as an example of a knowledgeable believer thrown into eternal punishment for a single act of disobedience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: first the claim, then the proof.
The passage is short but packed.
2
Identify the claim
The Khawarij claim that the perpetrator of a major sin (sahib al-kabira) is a kafir. Note that this goes beyond the Mu'tazila who only consider them fasiq.
This is a key difference.
3
Analyze their proof
They use the story of Iblis. Iblis was knowledgeable and obedient but committed one major sin (refusing to prostrate). As a result, Allah cursed him, declared him a kafir, and made him eternally in Hell. The Khawarij reason by analogy that any major sinner should be treated similarly.
Think about the logic: does a single sin negate all faith?
4
Reflect on the implications
This position leads to takfir of many Muslims. Consider the theological consequences.
This is a radical view rejected by mainstream Sunni Islam.
5
Review the key terms
Make sure you understand the Arabic terms: الخوارج, الكبيرة, التكفير, إبليس.
These are central.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزادت الخوارج عليهم بأن كفروا صاحب الكبيرة، واستدلوا عليه بقصة إبليس؛ إذ كان عارفا بالله مطيعا له غير أنه ارتكب كبيرة وهو الامتناع من السجود لآدم عليه السلام، فاستوجب اللعن والتكفير والتخليد في النار.
And the Khawarij added upon them by declaring the perpetrator of a major sin an unbeliever, and they adduced for it the story of Iblis; for he was knowledgeable of Allah and obedient to Him, but he committed a major sin, namely refraining from prostrating to Adam, peace be upon him, so he merited the curse, declaration of disbelief, and eternal abiding in the Fire.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage presents the Khawarij position on grave sins. The Khawarij go further than the Mu'tazila: they declare the perpetrator of a major sin (kabira) an unbeliever (kafir). Their proof is the story of Iblis (Satan). Iblis was knowledgeable of Allah and obedient, but his one sin of refusing to prostrate to Adam led to his being cursed, declared an unbeliever, and eternally punished. Thus, any major sin, by analogy, results in takfir (declaration of disbelief) and eternal punishment.

Key Terms
الخوارج
الكبيرة
التكفير
إبليس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila and Khawarij have the same view on grave sinners.
Correction: The Mu'tazila only consider the grave sinner a fasiq (neither believer nor unbeliever), while the Khawarij declare him a kafir (unbeliever).

Checkpoint
How do the Khawarij justify declaring a grave sinner an unbeliever?
A) By citing verses about obedience to rulers.
B) By using the example of Iblis, who was punished for a single sin.
C) By arguing that no sin is great enough to remove faith.
D) By claiming that all sinners are hypocrites.

Key Takeaway
The Khawarij argued that a single major sin makes one an unbeliever, using Iblis as evidence that knowledge and obedience do not prevent takfir.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a major sin you have committed or are tempted to commit. Do you comfort yourself with your knowledge of Allah and your other good deeds? How does the story of Iblis expose the danger of that comfort? What will you do today to take that sin seriously before Allah?
?
The Khawarij built a harsh doctrine on a single example. Are there teachings or people you have judged harshly based on one proof without considering mercy? Ask Allah to purify your heart from extremism, and commit to studying the full picture before passing judgment.
?
Iblis lost everything through one act of disobedience. What is one 'small' sin in your life that you have minimized? How does this lesson challenge you to repent from it now, before it leads to a greater loss?

The Murji'a: Faith Without Works
The author presents the Murji'a view that faith is speech and belief even without action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Murji'a definition of faith and its implications for the relationship between faith and works.

Core Idea
The Murji'a separate faith entirely from works, claiming neither sin harms the believer nor obedience benefits the disbeliever.

This passage presents the Murji'a position in contrast to the Mu'tazila and Khawarij. The author describes how the Murji'a defined faith as mere speech and belief, independent of actions. Understanding this extreme view helps clarify the spectrum of Islamic theological positions on faith and works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the concise formulation.
The passage contrasts with other sects discussed earlier.
2
Identify the two components of faith according to the Murji'a
Find the two elements that constitute faith in the Murji'a definition: qawl (speech) and 'aqd (belief).
3
Reflect on the consequences
Consider what the Murji'a claim about sin and obedience. How does this differ from other positions?
This is the key doctrinal point: sin does not harm faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقصرت المرجئة في مقابلتهم حيث قالوا: الإيمان قول وعقد وإن عري عن العمل، فلا يضر مع الإيمان معصية، كما لا ينفع مع الكفر طاعة.
And the Murji'a fell short in opposition to them, saying: Faith is speech and belief, even if stripped of action; so no sin harms with faith, just as no obedience benefits with disbelief.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage presents the Murji'a position. In contrast to the Mu'tazila and Khawarij, the Murji'a went to the opposite extreme. They defined faith (iman) as verbal confession (qawl) and inward conviction ('aqd), regardless of whether actions are present. Consequently, they claimed that committing sins does not harm a believer's faith, just as performing acts of obedience does not benefit a disbeliever. This effectively separates faith entirely from works.

Key Terms
المرجئة
الإيمان
قول وعقد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Murji'a are antinomians who reject all religious obligations.
Correction: While the Murji'a deemphasize works as part of faith, they do not necessarily reject the performance of obligations or forbid sin; they simply separate the definition of faith from actions.

Checkpoint
According to the Murji'a in this passage, what is the effect of sin on a person who has faith?
A) Sin removes faith entirely.
B) Sin does not harm faith.
C) Sin requires immediate repentance to maintain faith.
D) Sin proves that faith was never present.

Key Takeaway
The Murji'a defined faith as speech and belief, independent of actions, so sin does not harm faith and obedience does not benefit disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Has there been a time when you justified a sin by thinking, 'I still have faith, so it's okay'? That mirrors the Murji'a error. What specific sin have you normalized because you assumed it doesn't harm your faith? Today, ask Allah for forgiveness and commit to leaving that sin, proving that your faith is not just words.
?
The Murji'a said obedience does not benefit a disbeliever—but what about you? If you claim to have faith, does your life reflect that in action? In what area are you content with only inner belief and verbal confession while neglecting obedience? What one act of obedience will you undertake today to demonstrate that your faith is alive?

The Karamiyya Extreme: Faith as Mere Utterance
The author presents the Karamiyya view that faith is only verbal confession, even for hypocrites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extreme position of the Karamiyya sect, which reduces faith to mere verbal confession, and why mainstream scholars reject it.

Core Idea
For the Karamiyya, faith is only lip service, no heart required.

In the spectrum of opinions on faith, some groups go to extremes. The Karamiyya represent perhaps the most radical position, claiming that even a hypocrite's verbal confession makes him a true believer before Allah. This lesson examines their view and the author's strong rejection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and notice the tone
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Observe how the author describes the Karamiyya – he uses strong language like 'foulness and absurdity'.
The author's tone shows how deviant this view is considered.
2
Identify the Karamiyya definition of faith
Find the phrase where they define faith. It is 'mere utterance' (قول مجرد) and 'verbal confession alone' (الإقرار باللسان فحسب).
3
Understand their claim about hypocrites
See what they say about a person who confesses but is inwardly a liar or hypocrite. The passage says such a person is still 'a believer truly before Allah'.
This is the most extreme part of their doctrine.
4
Reflect on the author's criticism
The author says 'Would that they had said...' meaning even a milder claim would be bad, but they went further. This highlights how far their view deviates from mainstream Islam.
Mainstream Islam requires both inner belief and outer confession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشدهم تقصير الكرامية الذين ينفر الطبع السليم عن نقل مقالاتهم وذكر مذاهبهم؛ لخبثها وركاكتها قالت الإيمان قول مجرد، وهو الإقرار باللسان فحسب، وإن كان المقر كاذبًا منافقاً فهو مؤمن، وليتهم قالوا مؤمن عندنا، بل قالوا : مؤمن حقا عند الله تعالى حتى يثبت في حقه مشاركته المؤمنين في أحكام الإسلام.
And the most extreme in shortcoming are the Karamiyya, from whose foulness and absurdity the sound nature recoils from transmitting their statements and mentioning their doctrines. They said: Faith is bare utterance, and it is verbal confession alone, even if the confessor is a lying hypocrite, then he is a believer. Would that they had said 'a believer according to us,' but they said: 'a believer truly before Allah the Exalted,' so that he would be established in his right to share with the believers the rulings of Islam.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage presents the Karamiyya, considered the most extreme of the groups. Their view is that faith is mere verbal confession (iqrar bil-lisan), even if the speaker is lying or a hypocrite. The author criticizes them harshly, saying their statements are repulsive and absurd. The Karamiyya not only say such a person is a believer in our judgment, but they claim he is a true believer before Allah, thus granting him all the legal rulings of Islam. The author adds 'would that they had said...' to highlight their radical departure from mainstream Islam.

Key Terms
الكرامية
الإقرار باللسان
مؤمن حقا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the Karamiyya believe about a person who verbally confesses faith but is inwardly a hypocrite?
A) He is a believer only in worldly matters.
B) He is a true believer before Allah.
C) He is a disbeliever because of his hypocrisy.
D) He is a fasiq (grave sinner).

Key Takeaway
The Karamiyya teach that faith is only verbal confession, even if the person is a hypocrite, but this view contradicts the Qur'an and Sunnah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever uttered the shahada while your heart was indifferent or distracted? If the Karamiyya are wrong, what does that moment reveal about the state of your iman?
?
The Karamiyya claim a hypocrite is a true believer before Allah. In your own life, when have you worn the mask of faith while your actions or thoughts contradicted it? How does this teaching force you to confront the gap between your tongue and your heart?
?
If faith were only words, you could say the shahada and live however you please. But you don't. Why not? What does that internal resistance tell you about the reality of iman that the Karamiyya denied?

Sectarian Views on Grave Sinners: Extremes on Faith and Punishment
Presents Quranic verses on eternal punishment and responses of Khawarij, Murji'ah, Karramiyyah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three major sectarian positions (Khawarij, Murji'ah, Karramiyyah) regarding the grave sinner and how they deviate from the balanced Sunni view.

Core Idea
The grave sinner's fate is a battlefield of extremes: excommunication, license, and hollow confession.

The question of the grave sinner (sahib al-kabira) has long divided Islamic theology. Quranic verses threaten eternal punishment, but does that mean every grave sinner becomes a disbeliever? The Khawarij said yes, the Murji'ah said no action matters, and the Karramiyyah said only the tongue matters. This passage presents these extremes, setting up the Sunni middle path.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic proofs
Notice the verses cited about eternal punishment for disobedience and sin. What do they seem to imply about the fate of the grave sinner?
These verses are used by all sides, but interpreted differently.
2
Contrast with the Khawarij argument
Understand how they used the story of Iblis to justify takfir (excommunication) of grave sinners. Iblis was knowledgeable but committed one major sin and was eternally punished.
The Khawarij take this as a precedent for all grave sinners.
3
Examine the Murji'ah response
See how they overcorrected by divorcing actions from faith entirely. For them, faith is only statement and conviction; sin does not harm it.
This view makes faith independent of actions.
4
Reflect on the Karramiyyah extreme
Recognize why their view is considered repulsive: they say faith is mere verbal confession, even if the person is a hypocrite, and that such a person is a true believer before Allah.
This reduces faith to empty words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منها قوله تعالى : ( ومن يعص الله ورسوله ويتعد حدوده يدخله نارا خالدًا فيها ) [النساء : ١٤]، وقوله تعالى : ( بلى من كسب سيئة وأحاطت به خطيئته فأولئك أصحاب النار هم فيها خالدون ) [البقرة : ۸۱]، وقوله تعالى : ( ومن يقتل مؤمنًا متعمدا فجزاؤه جهنم خالدًا فيها ) [النساء : ٩٣]. وأيضًا فإن الرب تعالى ذكر حال الأشقياء وحال السعداء، وربط الخلود بمكانهما ، ولم يذكر في القرآن قسما ثالثًا فقال : ( فأما الذين شقوا ) [هود : ١٠٦] وأما الذين سعدوا ) [هود : ۱۰۸] ، وفي تفصيل الفريقين ( فريق في الجنة وفريق في السعير ). وزادت الخوارج عليهم بأن كفروا صاحب الكبيرة، واستدلوا عليه بقصة إبليس ؛ إذ كان عارفا بالله مطيعا له غير أنه ارتكب كبيرة وهو الامتناع من السجود لآدم عليه السلام، فاستوجب اللعن والتكفير والتخليد في النار. وقصرت المرجئة في مقابلتهم حيث قالوا: الإيمان قول وعقد وإن عري عن العمل، فلا يضر مع الإيمان معصية، كما لا ينفع مع الكفر طاعة. وأشدهم تقصير الكرامية الذين ينفر الطبع السليم عن نقل مقالاتهم وذكر مذاهبهم؛ لخبثها وركاكتها قالت الإيمان قول مجرد، وهو الإقرار باللسان فحسب، وإن كان المقر كاذبًا منافقًا فهو مؤمن ، وليتهم قالوا مؤمن عندنا، بل قالوا : مؤمن حقا عند الله تعالى حتى يثبت في حقه مشاركته المؤمنين في أحكام الإسلام.
Among them is His saying, the Most High: 'And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger and transgresses His limits, He will cause him to enter a fire, abiding therein forever' [al-Nisa 4:14], and His saying: 'Yes, whoever earns evil and his sin has encompassed him, those are the companions of the Fire, they will abide therein forever' [al-Baqara 2:81], and His saying: 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his reward is Hell, abiding therein forever' [al-Nisa 4:93]. Also, the Lord, the Most High, mentioned the state of the wretched and the state of the blessed, and tied eternity to their places, and did not mention a third division in the Qur'an, so He said: 'As for those who are wretched' [Hud 11:106] and 'As for those who are blessed' [Hud 11:108], and in the detailing of the two groups: 'A group in Paradise and a group in the Blaze.' And the Khawarij went beyond them by declaring the committer of a major sin to be a disbeliever, and they argued for it with the story of Iblis, for he was knowledgeable of Allah, obedient to Him, except that he committed a major sin i.e., refusing to prostrate to Adam, peace be upon him, so he deserved the curse, excommunication, and eternal punishment in the Fire. And the Murji'ah fell short in opposition to them when they said: Faith is statement and conviction, even if devoid of action, so no sin harms with faith, just as no obedience benefits with disbelief. And the most extreme in their shortcoming are the Karramiyyah, from whom sound nature recoils from transmitting their sayings and mentioning their schools due to their vileness and feebleness. They said: Faith is mere statement, which is verbal confession alone, even if the confessor is a liar and a hypocrite, he is a believer. Would that they had said 'a believer according to us,' but they said: 'a believer truly before Allah, the Most High,' so that he is established as sharing with the believers in the rulings of Islam.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of various sectarian positions on the status of the grave sinner (sahib al-kabira). It begins with Quranic proofs that the grave sinner deserves eternal punishment in Hell, citing several verses. Then it explains that the Khawarij went to the extreme of excommunicating (takfir) the grave sinner, using the story of Iblis as evidence: Iblis was knowledgeable and obedient but committed the major sin of arrogance, so he was cursed and eternally punished. In contrast, the Murji'ah fell short by saying that faith is only statement and conviction, and actions do not affect it, so sins do not harm a believer. The most extreme in shortcoming was the Karramiyyah, who said faith is merely verbal confession, even if the person is a hypocrite; they went so far as to claim such a person is a true believer before Allah. The passage points out the extremes and the middle position of Ahl al-Sunnah will be presented later.

Key Terms
صاحب الكبيرة
الخوارج
المرجئة
الكرامية
الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Muslims agree on the definition of faith (iman) and the status of grave sinners.
Correction: This passage shows that sects radically differ: Khawarij require actions for faith, Murji'ah separate actions completely, and Karramiyyah reduce faith to mere speech.

Checkpoint
Which sect argued that faith is merely verbal confession, even if the person is a hypocrite?
A) Khawarij
B) Murji'ah
C) Karramiyyah
D) Ahl al-Sunnah

Key Takeaway
The three sectarian positions on the grave sinner each represent an extreme, deviating from the balanced Sunni view that actions are essential to faith but not negating it entirely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a Muslim openly engaging in major sins, does your heart lean towards condemning them as outside the faith (like the Khawarij) or excusing them as 'faith in the heart' regardless of actions (like the Murji'ah)? What does the balanced view taught by the Sunnah require you to do today?
?
Have you ever relied on your verbal declaration of faith while neglecting the actions that should follow, thinking that your confession alone is sufficient for salvation? Examine your life: where does this complacency manifest, and what will you change right now?
?
The Khawarij used the example of Iblis to condemn the grave sinner to eternal fire. Do you ever judge others by their worst sin, forgetting that Allah's mercy encompasses all except those who die upon disbelief? How can you simultaneously uphold the seriousness of sin without falling into despair or self-righteousness?
